How Fast to Cut Back? Two Views Until Payoff Is Clear, Haste Means Big Waste

Author: By PETER PASSELL

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/how-fast-cut-back-two-views-until-payoff-is-clear/docview/109716580/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: WHAT, Americans worry? To the frustration of environmental activists, the still-distant risks from global warming have yet to grab the attention of Main Street. But to many economists, the difficulty in mobilizing public support to meet America's international commitment to reduce greenhouse gas emissions to 1990 levels by 2010 may yet prove a blessing.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=How+Fast+to+Cut+Back%3F+Two+Views+Until+Payoff+Is+Clear%2C+Haste+Means+Big+Waste&amp;author=By+PETER+PASSELL&amp;issn=03624331&amp;title=New+York+Times+%281923-%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-12-01&amp;spage=8&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Global Warming Triggers Renewed Interest in Nuclear Power; Controversy: The industry has been on a long losing streak, dogged by safety issues, waste-disposal problems and huge costs. But concern about climate change is causing some to reconsider.: [Bulldog Edition]

Author: Hanley, Charles J

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-triggers-renewed-interest-nuclear/docview/421226417/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Wherever he can, the U.N. nuclear agency chief, Hans Blix, is spreading the message that governments will soon realize they must embark on "the era of expanded nuclear power." And in Washington, at least, the debate over climate change is spurring some to take another look. Science advisors to President Clinton have recommended tripling the budget for nuclear-energy research. The White House, meanwhile, has moved to help the U.S. nuclear industry abroad, and Congress is trying to help it at home. "The world's need for nuclear energy will essentially be the subtext of what goes on in Kyoto in December," said Steve Kerekes, spokesman for the U.S. industry's Nuclear Energy Institute.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Global+Warming+Triggers+Renewed+Interest+in+Nuclear+Power%3B+Controversy%3A+The+industry+has+been+on+a+long+losing+streak%2C+dogged+by+safety+issues%2C+waste-disposal+problems+and+huge+costs.+But+concern+about+climate+change+is+causing+some+to+reconsider.&amp;author=Hanley%2C+Charles+J&amp;issn=04583035&amp;title=Los+Angeles+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-30&amp;spage=28&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: In the corridors and meeting halls of the great debate over global warming, one faction is offering a straightforward plan for saving Earth's atmosphere.
Their technology would power the world through the 21st century without adding an ounce of heat-trapping gas to the skies. It doesn't rely on pipelines or supertankers. It's clean and dependable.
It has just one problem. But it's a big one. It's nuclear.
Wherever he can, the U.N. nuclear agency chief, Hans Blix, is spreading the message that governments will soon realize they must embark on "the era of expanded nuclear power." And in Washington, at least, the debate over climate change is spurring some to take another look.
Science advisors to President Clinton have recommended tripling the budget for nuclear-energy research. The White House, meanwhile, has moved to help the U.S. nuclear industry abroad, and Congress is trying to help it at home.
In Japan, too, powerful interests are rallying to make "nuclear" the main source of energy for the world's No. 2 economy.
Some anti-nuclear activists are convinced the industry will never overcome its image as uneconomical and risky. But others worry that global warming may be opening a window of opportunity.
"Nuclear Death Is No Answer to the Climate Disaster," declared a banner unfurled by protesters outside the general conference of Blix's International Atomic Energy Agency in Vienna.
Nuclear advocates hope the global warming conference beginning Monday in Japan will boost the industry's fortunes.
"The world's need for nuclear energy will essentially be the subtext of what goes on in Kyoto in December," said Steve Kerekes, spokesman for the U.S. industry's Nuclear Energy Institute.
In Kyoto, some 150 nations will try to negotiate reductions in such "greenhouse gases" as carbon dioxide, a byproduct of the burning of coal, oil and natural gas in power plants worldwide. Scientists believe the gas buildup, trapping heat in the atmosphere, will disrupt Earth's climate.
Proposed solutions focus on improving energy efficiency and turning to solar, wind and other renewable energy sources. But Blix calls this a "dreamland" compared with nuclear energy's proven capacity.
France, for example, which uses atomic energy for almost three-quarters of its electricity, led Europe in reducing carbon dioxide emissions between 1980 and 1990, cutting them 26% on a per-capita basis.
Nuclear plants--442 in 30 countries, supplying 17% of the world's electricity--already keep man-made carbon dioxide emissions at least 8% below what they otherwise would be, says Blix's agency, which both guards against proliferation of nuclear weapons and promotes peaceful uses of the atom.
European and U.S. nuclear industry executives, appearing at pre-Kyoto talks last month in Germany, told delegates theirs was the "ready solution" for global warming.
The industry has been on a long losing streak, dogged by safety questions, an inability to dispose of its dangerous wastes and huge cost overruns. A U.S. plant would cost at least $4 billion to build; one that Turkey is planning is estimated at $2.4 billion or more.
Despite these troubles, construction started in 1996 on nine new power reactors, the most since 1985. All are in Asia, nuclear's new frontier.
China alone may spend $50 billion on nuclear power technology in the coming years, industry analysts say. A U.S.-Chinese agreement last month cleared the way for manufacturers in America, where no reactor has been sold since 1978, to cash in on Beijing's plans.
In another move to help the industry, the U.S. House and Senate have voted to create a storage facility for nuclear waste in Nevada.
Although U.S. global warming proposals do not envision expanding nuclear power's role, a White House experts' panel on energy, citing the risk of global warming, has recommended that the Energy Department's annual budget for nuclear fission research and development be tripled to $120 million by 2003.
It's "prudent insurance," said panel member Robert W. Conn, engineering dean at UC San Diego.
"If global warming is a major environmental issue . . . then one can hardly not consider the possibility that you may have to deploy some nuclear power as part of an overall strategy to reduce greenhouse gases," Conn said.
In Japan, which relies on fission for a third of its electricity, pro- and anti-nuclear voices grow louder as the Kyoto talks approach.
The influential Keidanren business federation has called for Japan to "position atomic energy as the central source among the various sources of basic energy."
The government, which says it is considering adding 20 plants to the 52 Japan already has, estimates that doubling the country's nuclear power output would reduce greenhouse gas emissions by 7% from 1990 levels.
But a series of accidents at atomic power plants this year undercut public support for nuclear power, and an accelerated buildup doesn't seem economically feasible. "This is absurd and unrealistic," anti-nuclear activist Jinzaburo Takagi said in Tokyo.
Takagi fears, nevertheless, that pro-nuclear forces will make inroads at Kyoto.
"The Japanese government wants to take advantage of international pressure on carbon emissions to advocate nuclear energy," he said.
As carbon dioxide spews heavenward, the industry bides its time.
"We've been treated with some disdain," said the nuclear institute's Kerekes. "But I think we're going to be something of a Cinderella."
Michael Oppenheimer, on the other hand, believes the industry will be more pumpkin than princess when midnight strikes in Kyoto.
"Nuclear has a big economic monkey on its back. It's too expensive," said the atmospheric expert for the Environmental Defense Fund. "They can say whatever they want. It can't overcome that economic problem."
PHOTO: A nuclear power plant in Kashiwazaki Niigata prefecture, Japan. A conference on global warming begins in Kyoto on Monday.; PHOTOGRAPHER: Associated Press

CONFLICTING VIEWS ABOUT GLOBAL WARMING ARE AS DIFFERENT AS HOT AND COLD: [FIVE STAR LIFT EDITION]

Author: Post-Dispatch Washington Bureau

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/conflicting-views-about-global-warming-are-as/docview/403734383/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: From "Rising Sun, Gathering Winds; Policies to Stabilize the Climate and Strengthen Economies," by Christopher Flavin and Seth Dunn (Worldwatch Paper): "Just as homeowners buy insurance to cope with potential dangers without knowing exactly how the future will unfold, so would governments be wise to invest in affordable means of slowing growth in greenhouse gas emissions in order to minimize the risks of climate change. . . . "The threats that rapid climate change poses to society and the natural world are unprecedented. The temperature change resulting from the projected doubling of effective CO2 (carbon dioxide) concentrations in the next century could shift the ecological zones of one third of the world's forests northward at rates well beyond the maximum migration rates of most trees and shrubs, resulting in widespread forest death and wildfires.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=CONFLICTING+VIEWS+ABOUT+GLOBAL+WARMING+ARE+AS+DIFFERENT+AS+HOT+AND+COLD&amp;author=Post-Dispatch+Washington+Bureau&amp;issn=19309600&amp;title=St.+Louis+Post+-+Dispatch&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-30&amp;spage=B.4&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Here are two conflicting views of the global warming issue:
From "Rising Sun, Gathering Winds; Policies to Stabilize the Climate and Strengthen Economies," by Christopher Flavin and Seth Dunn (Worldwatch Paper):
"Just as homeowners buy insurance to cope with potential dangers without knowing exactly how the future will unfold, so would governments be wise to invest in affordable means of slowing growth in greenhouse gas emissions in order to minimize the risks of climate change. . . .
"The threats that rapid climate change poses to society and the natural world are unprecedented. The temperature change resulting from the projected doubling of effective CO2 (carbon dioxide) concentrations in the next century could shift the ecological zones of one third of the world's forests northward at rates well beyond the maximum migration rates of most trees and shrubs, resulting in widespread forest death and wildfires.
"An increase in extreme weather, such as droughts and floods, is also likely to exacerbate water and food scarcity. At the same time, a 15-95 centimeter rise in sea level could turn people now living on islands and in coastal areas into environmental refugees. And the increase of heat waves and expanded range of infectious diseases, such as malaria and cholera, could cause health problems for millions."
***
From "The Scientific Case Against the Global Climate Treaty," by S. Fred Singer (The Science & Environmental Policy Project):
"The Global Climate Treaty, signed at the 1992 `Earth Summit' in Rio de Janeiro, rests on three propositions that are either questionable or demonstrably false:
"1. The Climate Treaty supposes that a human influence has been detected in the climate record of the last hundred years, thereby validating the computer-generated predictions of a major future warming. But the climate has not warmed significantly over the last half-century, and not at all over the last 20 years, in contrast to theoretical predictions.
"2. It further supposes that any future warming would produced catastrophic consequences, including droughts, floods, hurricanes, rapid and significant sea level rise, the collapse of agriculture, and the spread of tropical disease. But the recorded climate of the past 3,000 years appears to dispel that. Historically, warmer temperatures have been, on the whole, beneficial for human welfare and the development of civilization.
"3. It presumes - with no scientific definition - to know which atmospheric levels of greenhouse gases are `dangerous' and which are not. To stabilize CO2 concentration at present levels, 30 percent above pre-industrial values, would require a drastic reduction of emissions and energy use - more than 60 percent worldwide. But again, the historical record indicates that higher levels of CO2 - and they have been much higher in the past - may in fact provide benefits.
"Some scientists, including the late Roger Revelle, known as the father of greenhouse warming, have speculated that some of these benefits have already turned up in improved agricultural yields."

Subject: Greenhouse effect; Global warming

IT'S CLIMATE VS. ECONOMY AT SUMMIT IN JAPAN NATIONS PLAN TO PUSH FOR COMPROMISES AT THE 10-DAY CONFERENCE AIMED AT SLOWING THE PACE OF GLOBAL WARMING.: [METRO EDITION]

Author: Associated Press

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/climate-vs-economy-at-summit-japan-nations-plan/docview/279095969/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Commuter hussle. Tokyo's subway system is one key way in which this crowded city keeps its greenhouse gas emissions lower than those in the United States, where more people drive. ASSOCIATED PRESS BOX: The unknowns How much past warming is man-made and how much came from natural causes? Exactly how much warmer do certain levels of gas make the atmosphere? Do clouds and oceans make the atmosphere cooler or warmer, and how? How do more population and economic growth affect emission levels? What effect will new technologies or lifestyles have?

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=IT%27S+CLIMATE+VS.+ECONOMY+AT+SUMMIT+IN+JAPAN+NATIONS+PLAN+TO+PUSH+FOR+COMPROMISES+AT+THE+10-DAY+CONFERENCE+AIMED+AT+SLOWING+THE+PACE+OF+GLOBAL+WARMING.&amp;author=Associated+Press&amp;issn=&amp;title=Orlando+Sentinel&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-30&amp;spage=A.21&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Global warming debate, A-16 and Insight
Representatives of 150 nations are converging on this city of ancient temples to negotiate a 21st-century insurance policy for the planet, a deal to control energy use as a first installment on protecting the atmosphere against global warming.
"There are times when only an act of courage can spur progress in world affairs," U.N. Secretary-General Kofi Annan said in a statement wishing the conference success as it opens Monday.
Chief U.S. negotiator Stuart E. Eizenstat predicted a "movable feast" of compromises. If successful, the 10 days of negotiations could lead to shifts toward new-technology automobiles and fuel-saving driving habits, away from coal-powered electricity plants and toward new farming and forestry practices.
Such controls over carbon dioxide and other "greenhouse" gases could, in turn, begin to forestall the damaging rises in temperatures, sea levels and severe weather that scientists predict from global warming.
But almost every feature of this plan - including who should act, how and when - is in dispute. And powerful opposition has developed against early action, especially among oil-producing countries and U.S. coal, oil and other interests likely to be hurt. Industry lobbyists are among the thousands of diplomats, scientists, environmentalists and journalists assembling in Japan's old capital in the midst of unusually mild weather that has Japanese fretting that "global warming" may ruin their Winter Olympics in February in Nagano.
But the vagaries of any day's or month's weather cannot be attributed confidently to global climate change. And this imprecision among scientists has weakened the hand of those trying to combat the phenomenon.
At the 1992 Earth Summit in Brazil, the world's nations signed a Climate Change Treaty that, because of U.S. opposition, set only a voluntary goal: reducing greenhouse gas emissions to 1990 levels by 2000. It soon was clear most countries would miss that goal, and in 1995 governments agreed to negotiate legally binding targets and timetables.
The underlying principle of climate change is undisputed: Carbon dioxide, methane, nitrous oxide and a few other gases, mostly from fuel combustion, are transparent to incoming sunlight but trap the heat that Earth emits.
In 1995, as atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide stood about 30 percent higher than in pre-industrial times, a U.N.-sponsored network of climate scientists issued a pivotal report, declaring global warming was under way and man was apparently partly to blame. Average temperatures had risen by up to 0.6 degrees Celsius (1 degree Fahrenheit) in the past century.
The scientists predicted growing emissions would boost temperatures 2 to 6 degrees Fahrenheit by 2100 and raise sea levels 6 to 37 inches, inundating islands and shorelines because the warmth would expand ocean volume and melt glaciers. The warming also would shift climate zones and make regional weather wetter, drier and generally more turbulent, they said.
Critics have seized on the uncertainties of the projections to resist early action.
Key issues confronting the negotiators: Proposals to reduce industrial nations' emissions range from an ambitious 20 percent cutback below 1990 levels by 2005, to the low-end U.S. plan for reducing emissions to, but not below, 1990 levels by 2012.
Washington is pushing hard for a system of trading "emissions permits" so that a utility in Chicago could, in effect, continue burning coal by buying a permit from a country where it might prove cheaper to convert to more climate-friendly natural gas.
The U.S. team also is looking for ways to commit developing countries to emissions reductions.
Illustration PHOTO: Commuter hussle. Tokyo's subway system is one key way in which this crowded city keeps its greenhouse gas emissions lower than those in the United States, where more people drive. ASSOCIATED PRESS BOX: The unknowns How much past warming is man-made and how much came from natural causes? Exactly how much warmer do certain levels of gas make the atmosphere? Do clouds and oceans make the atmosphere cooler or warmer, and how? How do more population and economic growth affect emission levels? What effect will new technologies or lifestyles have?

Subject: Global warming

SEEKING TO OVERCOME CONCERN FOR RISKS, HIGH COST Nuclear power sees an opening Global warming becomes focus of industry campaign to expand: [Early Edition]

Author: Hanley, Charles J

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/seeking-overcome-concern-risks-high-cost-nuclear/docview/260674544/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: In the corridors and meeting halls of the great debate over global warming, one faction is offering a straightforward plan for saving Earth's atmosphere. Wherever he can, the U.N. nuclear agency chief, Hans Blix, is spreading the message that governments will soon realize they must embark on "the era of expanded nuclear power." The White House, meanwhile, has moved to help the U.S. nuclear industry abroad, and Congress is trying to help it at home.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=SEEKING+TO+OVERCOME+CONCERN+FOR+RISKS%2C+HIGH+COST+Nuclear+power+sees+an+opening+Global+warming+becomes+focus+of+industry+campaign+to+expand&amp;author=Hanley%2C+Charles+J&amp;issn=10828850&amp;title=Milwaukee+Journal+Sentinel&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-30&amp;spage=6&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: In the corridors and meeting halls of the great debate over global warming, one faction is offering a straightforward plan for saving Earth's atmosphere.
Their technology would power the world through the 21st century without adding an ounce of heat-trapping gas to the skies. It doesn't rely on pipelines or supertankers. It's clean and dependable.
It has just one problem. But it's a big one. It's nuclear.
Wherever he can, the U.N. nuclear agency chief, Hans Blix, is spreading the message that governments will soon realize they must embark on "the era of expanded nuclear power." And in Washington, at least, the debate over climate change is spurring some to take another look.
Science advisers to President Clinton have recommended tripling the budget for nuclear-energy research. The White House, meanwhile, has moved to help the U.S. nuclear industry abroad, and Congress is trying to help it at home.
In Japan, too, powerful interests are rallying to make "nuclear" the main source of energy for the world's No. 2 economy.
Some anti-nuclear activists are convinced the industry will never overcome its image as uneconomical and risky. But others worry that global warming may be opening a window of opportunity.
"Nuclear Death Is No Answer To The Climate Disaster," declared a banner unfurled by protesters outside the general conference of Blix's International Atomic Energy Agency in Vienna, Austria.
Nuclear advocates hope the global warming conference beginning Monday in Japan will boost the industry's fortunes.
"The world's need for nuclear energy will essentially be the subtext of what goes on in Kyoto in December," said Steve Kerekes, spokesman for the U.S. industry's Nuclear Energy Institute.
In Kyoto, some 150 nations will try to negotiate reductions in such "greenhouse gases" as carbon dioxide, a byproduct of the burning of coal, oil and natural gas in power plants worldwide. Scientists believe the gas buildup, trapping heat in the atmosphere, will disrupt Earth's climate.
Proposed solutions focus on improving energy efficiency and turning to solar, wind and other renewable energy sources. But Blix calls this a "dreamland" compared with nuclear energy's proven capacity.
France, for example, which uses atomic energy for almost three-quarters of its electricity, led Europe in reducing carbon dioxide emissions between 1980 and 1990, cutting them 26% on a per-capita basis.
Nuclear plants 442 in 30 countries, supplying 17% of the world's electricity already keep man-made carbon dioxide emissions at least 8% below what they otherwise would be, says Blix's agency, which both guards against proliferation of nuclear weapons and promotes peaceful uses of the atom.
European and U.S. nuclear industry executives, appearing at pre-Kyoto talks last month in Germany, told delegates theirs was the "ready solution" for global warming.
The industry has been on a long losing streak, dogged by safety questions, an inability to dispose of its dangerous wastes and huge cost overruns. A U.S. plant would cost at least $4 billion to build; one that Turkey is planning is estimated at $2.4 billion or more.
Despite these troubles, construction started in 1996 on nine new power reactors, the most since 1985. All are in Asia, nuclear's new frontier.
China alone may spend $50 billion on nuclear power technology in the coming years, industry analysts say. A U.S.-Chinese agreement last month cleared the way for manufacturers in America, where no reactor has been sold since 1978, to cash in on Beijing's plans.
In another move to help the industry, the U.S. House and Senate have voted to create a storage facility for nuclear waste in Nevada.
Although U.S. global warming proposals do not envision expanding nuclear power's role, a White House experts' panel on energy, citing the risk of global warming, has recommended the Energy Department's annual budget for nuclear fission research and development be tripled to $120 million by 2003.
It's "prudent insurance," said panel member Robert W. Conn, engineering dean at the University of California at San Diego.
"If global warming is a major environmental issue . . . then one can hardly not consider the possibility that you may have to deploy some nuclear power as part of an overall strategy to reduce greenhouse gases," Conn said.
As carbon dioxide spews heavenward, the industry bides its time.
"We've been treated with some disdain," said the nuclear institute's Kerekes, "but I think we're going to be something of a Cinderella."
Michael Oppenheimer, on the other hand, believes the industry will be more pumpkin than princess when midnight strikes in Kyoto.
"Nuclear has a big economic monkey on its back. It's too expensive," said the atmospheric expert for the Environmental Defense Fund. "They can say whatever they want. It can't overcome that economic problem."
Credit: Associated Press

Energy use first topic of planetary powwow: [Final Edition]

Author: Hanley, Charles J

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/energy-use-first-topic-planetary-powwow/docview/260012142/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Increasing emissions of carbon dioxide, methane and some other gases into the atmosphere trap heat in a "greenhouse effect." They are transparent and allow sunlight in but retain heat the Earth emits, with some escaping back toward space. A look at the process: GLOBAL WARMING Representatives of 150 nations are in Kyoto, Japan, to try to hammer out a deal to protect the atmosphere against global warming into the 21st century. KYOTO, Japan - Representatives of 150 nations are converging on this city of ancient temples to negotiate a 21st-century insurance policy for the planet, a deal to control energy use as a first installment on protecting the atmosphere against global warming.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Energy+use+first+topic+of+planetary+powwow&amp;author=Hanley%2C+Charles+J&amp;issn=10971645&amp;title=Las+Vegas+Review+-+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-30&amp;spage=1.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: eizenstat
J. Axamethy/Associated Press
Source: World Resources Institute
Energy use first topic of planetary powwow
Increasing emissions of carbon dioxide, methane and some other gases into the atmosphere trap heat in a "greenhouse effect." They are transparent and allow sunlight in but retain heat the Earth emits, with some escaping back toward space. A look at the process: GLOBAL WARMING
Representatives of 150 nations are in Kyoto, Japan, to try to hammer out a deal to protect the atmosphere against global warming into the 21st century.
By Charles J. Hanley
Associated Press
KYOTO, Japan - Representatives of 150 nations are converging on this city of ancient temples to negotiate a 21st-century insurance policy for the planet, a deal to control energy use as a first installment on protecting the atmosphere against global warming.
The talks are highly technical and complex, the politics challenging.
"There are times when only an act of courage can spur progress in world affairs," U.N. Secretary-General Kofi Annan said in a statement wishing the conference success as it opens Monday.
Chief U.S. negotiator Stuart E. Eizenstat predicted a "moveable feast" of hard-fought compromises.
If successful, the 10 days of negotiations could lead in a decade or so to shifts toward new-technology automobiles and fuel-saving driving habits, away from coal-powered electricity plants, and toward new farming and forestry practices.
Such controls over carbon dioxide and other "greenhouse" gases could, in turn, begin to forestall the damaging rises in temperatures, sea levels and severe weather that scientists predict from global warming.
But almost every feature of this 21st-century plan - including who should act, how and when - is in dispute. And powerful opposition has developed against early action, especially among oil-producing countries and U.S. coal, oil and other interests likely to be hurt.
Industry lobbyists are among the thousands of diplomats, scientists, environmentalists and journalists assembling in Japan's old capital in the midst of unusually mild weather that has Japanese fretting that "global warming" may ruin their Winter Olympics in February in Nagano.
But the vagaries of any day's or month's weather cannot be attributed confidently to global climate change. And this imprecision among scientists has weakened the hand of those trying to combat the phenomenon.
At the 1992 Earth Summit in Brazil, the world's nations signed a Climate Change Treaty that, because of U.S. opposition, set only a voluntary goal: reducing greenhouse gas emissions to 1990 levels by 2000.
It soon was clear most countries would miss that goal, and in 1995 governments agreed to toughen the treaty by negotiating legally binding targets and timetables. Several preliminary rounds of talks laid the groundwork for final discussions here over a treaty protocol.
The underlying principle of climate change is undisputed: Carbon dioxide, methane, nitrous oxide and a few other gases, mostly from fuel combustion, are transparent to incoming sunlight but trap the heat that Earth emits back to space.
In 1995, as atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide stood about 30 percent higher than in pre-industrial times, a U.N.-sponsored network of climate scientists issued a pivotal report, declaring global warming was under way and man was apparently partly to blame. Global average temperatures had risen by up to 0.6 degrees Celsius (1 degree Fahrenheit) in the past century.
The scientists predicted growing emissions would boost temperatures an additional 1 and 3.5 degrees Celsius (2 to 6 degrees Fahrenheit) by 2100, and raise sea levels 15 to 95 centimeters (6 to 37 inches), inundating islands and shorelines, because the warmth would expand ocean volume and melt glaciers.
The warming also would shift climate zones abruptly and make regional weather wetter, drier and generally more turbulent, they said.
Critics have seized on the uncertainties of the projections to resist early action.
"There's a danger of having policy driven by the worst-case scenario," complained the American Petroleum Institute's William O'Keefe, chairman of the Global Climate Coalition, a U.S. industry group that contends emission controls will throw hundreds of thousands of Americans out of work.
Environmentalists counter than even a degree more warming could be damaging - flooding much of low-lying, overcrowded Bangladesh, for example.
And the worst-case scenario could be calamitous for everyone, said Michael Oppenheimer of the U.S. Environmental Defense Fund.
"In the face of uncertainty, what do you do?" he asked. "Some are willing to take a gamble."
Key issues confronting the negotiators:
-Proposals to reduce industrial nations' emissions range from an ambitious 20 percent cutback below 1990 levels by 2005, to the low-end U.S. plan for reducing emissions to, but not below, 1990 levels by 2012. The Clinton administration says its plan would mean reductions of more than 25 percent in what U.S. emissions otherwise would be.
-Washington is pushing hard for a system of trading "emissions permits," so that a utility in Chicago could, in effect, continue burning coal by buying a permit from a country, such as Poland, where it might prove cheaper to cut emissions by converting to more climate-friendly natural gas.
-The U.S. team also is looking for ways to commit developing countries to emissions reductions.
The industrial world, largely responsible for the carbon buildup, agreed in 1992 to exempt poorer nations from cutbacks. But the U.S. Senate, fearing economic competition from China and others free to burn cheap coal, threatens to kill any treaty protocol that does not cover developing nations.
Eizenstat said his negotiators are working to extend commitments to a "critical mass" of developing countries. Wealthier U.S. friends, such as South Korea and Argentina, might opt to adhere to targets.
The protocol won't dictate how countries should cut back on emissions. But recommended solutions will include conversion to "hybrid" automobiles that phase out the internal combustion engine; improved mass transit; phasing out power plants relying on high-carbon coal; introducing new feeds for cattle, which emit major amounts of methane; and better protection for forests, which absorb carbon dioxide.

Title: Energy use first topic of planetary powwow:   [Final Edition ]

COMPROMISES EXPECTED IN GLOBAL WARMING TALKS

Author: Hanley, Charles J

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/compromises-expected-global-warming-talks/docview/252901184/se-2?accountid=10226

Global warming brings a glow to green energy On the eve of the Kyoto climate change talks, ANTONY BARNETT reports on a bright future for the renewable power industry

Author: Barnett, Antony

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-brings-glow-green-energy-on-eve/docview/250351403/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: GENERATING enough electricity from wind, water and sun to provide light for homes and power for industry has long been the dream of environmentalists. And for just as long, the reality has been a disappointment. While the coal industry is on its knees and losing friends in government, supporters of renewable energy believe the wind is blowing firmly in their direction. The Government has instigated a comprehensive review of the renewable energy sector and is committed to quadrupling the production of electricity from such sources over the next 13 years. At the moment, just over 2.5 per cent of Britain's electricity comes from renewable sources such as gas from household waste and wind power. Labour says it wants this to reach 10 per cent by 2010. And a European Commission White Paper, out last week, set an even more ambitious target, 12 per cent.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Global+warming+brings+a+glow+to+green+energy+On+the+eve+of+the+Kyoto+climate+change+talks%2C+ANTONY+BARNETT+reports+on+a+bright+future+for+the+renewable+power+industry&amp;author=Barnett%2C+Antony&amp;issn=00297712&amp;title=The+Observer&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-30&amp;spage=006&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: GENERATING enough electricity from wind, water and sun to provide light for homes and power for industry has long been the dream of environmentalists. And for just as long, the reality has been a disappointment.
But now, as representatives of 160 nations meet in the Japanese city of Kyoto to discuss greenhouse gas emissions and climate change, there is a feeling that maybe - just maybe - renewable energy's time has come.
In the past it appeared too expensive and risky to finance, and simply not efficient enough to compete with fossil fuels. But what was once a yawning gap between conventional and renewable sources has narrowed.
New technologies mean electricity generated from wind and municipal waste can, in certain cases, be as cheap and as easy to produce as with conventional fuels.
Many are sceptical about what Kyoto will achieve. But the issues the conference seeks to address are not going to go away. And the British renewable energy industry stands at a crossroads.
While the coal industry is on its knees and losing friends in government, supporters of renewable energy believe the wind is blowing firmly in their direction. The Government has instigated a comprehensive review of the renewable energy sector and is committed to quadrupling the production of electricity from such sources over the next 13 years.
At the moment, just over 2.5 per cent of Britain's electricity comes from renewable sources such as gas from household waste and wind power. Labour says it wants this to reach 10 per cent by 2010. And a European Commission White Paper, out last week, set an even more ambitious target, 12 per cent.
The challenge of meeting these targets is huge, and implies a revolution in the UK's entire energy policy.
In recent months, oil giants BP and Shell have between them invested hundreds of millions of pounds in solar power and other alternative energy sources, such as burning specially grown timber.
Some dismiss this as public relations gimmickry: after all, they invest billions rather than millions in the development of fossil fuels. But BP chief executive John Browne has gone on record as saying that in 50 years' time, half the world's energy could be provided by renewable sources.
At the moment, BP's turnover from solar business is pounds 50 million a year. But that is expected to rise to pounds 600m a year by 2010. Shell believes that by 2020 the renewables market will be worth around pounds 148bn. The Government's own figures suggest the world market for renewable energy will reach pounds 40bn by 2000.
Dr Jeremy Leggett, chairman of the Government's solar power taskforce, says: 'The big difference today is we are now getting blue-chip players involved. It is giving the industry a new degree of credibility.'
But it's not just the oil majors who are having to face up to the new reality. Insurance companies including General Accident and GRE, already hit by claims from unexpected storms, are worried that dramatic climate change will lead to massive storm-damage claims in the future. They have joined up with the Government taskforces to examine the potential of renewable energy.
Yet while the political and public will appears to support a significant expansion of this sector, achieving the ambitious targets will depend on a successful overhaul of the complex system which takes electricity generated from renewable sources into our homes.
Eight years ago, it was believed that because renewable energy was more expensive than conventionally generated power, it had to be supported if it were to get off the ground. In 1989, the Conservative government introduced a mechanism known as the non-fossil fuel obligation - NFFO, pronounced nuffo. The Government would order the regional electricity companies (Recs) to buy a certain amount of renewable-sourced power.
Typical projects include generation from landfill gas, household and industrial waste, hydro-electric plants and wind - solar power is still too expensive. The Recs would then sign a 15-year contract to buy the power.
The buyers - the Recs - were then given a subsidy to cover the gap between what they were paying for this electricity and the cheapest price at which they could have bought on the open market. The subsidy came from a levy on all electricity bills amounting to just over 1 per cent.
The NFFO system is still in place. And last week, energy minister John Battle announced a new, fifth round of NFFO - the final one before the Government review.
There has been some criticism of the NFFO system, but it has at least created a market for supplying renewable energy and driven down costs by encouraging the most efficient producers.
Such has been the change in the economics of renewable energy, that Dr Catherine Mitchell of the Science Policy Research Unit believes the regional electricity companies no longer need to be subsidised for using the cheapest renewable sources.
'Electricity companies should simply be forced to take a certain amount of power from renewable resources,' she says. 'This can be done through a change in their licence conditions. They should then just pay a price that reflects the costs of that electricity.'
The advantage of such a reform would be that it would not require any primary legislation and could be undertaken by the electricity regulator.
To reach the Government target of obtaining 10 per cent of electricity through renewables by 2010 will require an increase in renewable capacity of around 350 megawatts every year.
But can this be done? Many renewable projects have failed to get off the ground because of the problems of winning planning permission. Of the 619 contracts that have been awarded under NFFO, only 208 are up and running, producing just 470 megawatts. While wind farms and energy-from-waste generators may enjoy public support, nobody wants them in their back yards. Because of this, many see wind turbines located off-shore as the best way forward for the UK.
The problem is that such turbines are expensive. PowerGen's ambitious plan to build a wind farm at Scroby Sands off the Norfolk coasts did not get a NFFO contract because it was too costly.
Chancellor Gordon Brown is rumoured to be happy about proposals to allow pounds 150m a year to be spent on renewables, and many hope a large slice of this will go to support the offshore-wind sector.
There is also another exciting prospect for the renewables industry. From next April competition begins in the residential electricity market and households will be able to shop around for their power supplier.
Already companies including Eastern and Sweb are offering special 'green' tariffs. These will allow environment-conscious customers to pay a bit more (about 10 per cent) on their electricity bills to fund renewable energy projects.
The Parliamentary Renewable & Sustainable Energy Group found that 86 per cent of the public would rather buy electricity generated in an environment-friendly way. Good news for Greens? Yes, except that only 21 per cent of people would be prepared to pay extra for it. Still, with 24 million electricity users, there must be enough green consumers out there to warrant further investment in renewables.
Domestic power, page 16

GWYNNE DYER - WORLD ISSUE - Like a bunch of

Author: Specials Wire

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/gwynne-dyer-world-issue-like-bunch/docview/345656534/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The U.S., for example, promised at the 1992 Earth Summit in Rio to cut its carbon dioxide emissions back to 1990 levels by the year 2000. Instead, U.S. carbon emissions are 13 per cent higher than in 1990, and Washington is now saying that it can't get back down to the target figure until 2013. What happened? What went wrong? Basically, [Clinton] belatedly realized that U.S. fossil-fuel producers, in alliance with other major industries that worry about higher energy costs, control enough U.S. senators to block any treaty that hurts their interests. So rather than fight a hopeless battle, he has chosen pre-emptive surrender. Without a serious American offer on cutting emissions, none of the other holdouts at Kyoto will be under any pressure to yield, for the U.S. is the most flagrant polluter of all (four per cent of the world's population, 24 per cent of its greenhouse gas emissions). So the best that can be hoped for at Kyoto is dramatic failure.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=GWYNNE+DYER+-+WORLD+ISSUE+-+Like+a+bunch+of&amp;author=Specials+Wire&amp;issn=&amp;title=Times+-+Colonist&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-29&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: frogs in a pot, we can't sense what's coming' THE UNITED NATIONS Climate Summit that opens in Kyoto, Japan, on Monday will probably not end in a flaming row. Countries must not lose face, and so they are more likely to cobble together some shoddy compromise that is worth nothing. But a spectacular failure would be much better, for at least it might get the donkey's attention. Or more precisely, the frog's attention. Environmentalists often compare human behavior to that of frogs - who, if you drop them into a pot of boiling water, will jump right out again and survive. But sit afrog in a pot of cool water, raise the heat gradually, and he'll sit there happily until he boils to death.
Are humans really like frogs? Of course they are. Take Malaysians, for example.
The haze is gone from Malaysia now, but the cabaret group Instant Cafe is still getting a lot of mileage out of it. They were playing a corporate function in Kuala Lumpur last weekend - people who'd coughed and wept their way through the haze for all of September and October - and the audience rocked with laughter when they did their Haze song.
Come and visit Malaysia.
Come and stay for lots of days.
You don't need your suntan lotion.
You'll be safe from UV rays.
No need to sunbathe by the ocean.
Get dark by strolling in our haze.
The "haze" is mostly smoke from the thousands of forest fires that are set in Indonesia every dry season: first you log out any commercially valuable trees, then you burn the rest because that's the cheapest way to clear the land for cultivation. The smoke from huge areas of burning forest drifts over neighboring Malaysia, blocking out the sun and causing respiratory disorders.
Yet few Malaysians think they can do anything about it, because they know that the companies that are setting most of the fires in Indonesia have close links with President Suharto's government in Jakarta, and generally get their way.
They are also vaguely aware that destroying the forests raises the amount of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere and contributes to global warming. But that problem isn't affecting their daily lives yet, and they have lots of present worries to keep them busy.
People in large industrialized democracies like the United States like to think they are different: better information, more open societies, a more educated public. But in fact they are equally captive to special interest groups.
The U.S., for example, promised at the 1992 Earth Summit in Rio to cut its carbon dioxide emissions back to 1990 levels by the year 2000. Instead, U.S. carbon emissions are 13 per cent higher than in 1990, and Washington is now saying that it can't get back down to the target figure until 2013. What happened?
The Clinton administration knows what it should do. At a preparatory meeting of the climate change conference in Geneva earlier this year, the U.S. mobilized the world's big insurance companies, who proved that global warming was already hitting their balance sheets in terms of soaring claims for storm damage.
That debunked the "junk science" (paid for by fossil-fuel producers) that pretends global warming is just an unproven hypothesis. With the European Union offering emission cuts to 15 per cent below 1990 levels by 2010 and the U.S. government also apparently on board, success in Kyoto seemed within reach.
What went wrong? Basically, Clinton belatedly realized that U.S. fossil-fuel producers, in alliance with other major industries that worry about higher energy costs, control enough U.S. senators to block any treaty that hurts their interests. So rather than fight a hopeless battle, he has chosen pre-emptive surrender.
Just as in Jakarta, the tail is wagging the dog. Many more Americans have an interest in curbing global warming than in the industries that cause it, but the majority is less conscious of its interests: the water in the pot is getting hotter, but only gradually. So the prospective victims exert less political pressure, and the other side gets its way.
Without a serious American offer on cutting emissions, none of the other holdouts at Kyoto will be under any pressure to yield, for the U.S. is the most flagrant polluter of all (four per cent of the world's population, 24 per cent of its greenhouse gas emissions). So the best that can be hoped for at Kyoto is dramatic failure.
The Europeans should stick to their 15 per cent cuts proposal and make no compromises. They won't get their way, but that will at least ensure that the problem isn't shelved for five years after a cynical deal that leaves everyone free to do whatever they want.
Complete failure would at least make everybody come back for another conference next year. In the meantime, we should pray for weather so extreme that it wakes the frogs up.
Gwynne Dyer is an independent journalist and historian whose articles appear in 35 countries.

People: Dyer, Gwynne

Failure at Kyoto summit would be a good thing: [Final Edition]

Full text: The UN Climate Summit that opens in Kyoto, Japan, on Monday will probably not end in a flaming row.
Countries must not lose face, and so they are more likely to cobble together some shoddy compromise that is worth nothing. But a failure would be much better -- it might get the donkey's attention.
Or more precisely, the frog's attention. Environmentalists often compare human behavior to that of frogs -- who, if you drop them into a pot of boiling water, will jump right out again and survive. But sit a frog in a pot of cool water, raise the heat gradually, and he'll sit there happily until he boils to death.
Are humans really like frogs? Of course they are. Take Malaysians, for example.
The haze in that country is mostly smoke from the thousands of forest fires that are set in Indonesia every dry season: First you log out any commercially valuable trees, then you burn the rest because that's the cheapest way to clear the land for cultivation. The smoke from huge areas of burning forest drifts over Malaysia, blocking out the sun and causing respiratory disorders.
Yet few Malaysians think they can do anything about it, because they know that the companies that are setting most of the fires in Indonesia have close links with President Suharto's government in Jakarta, and generally get their way.
They are also vaguely aware that destroying the forests raises the amount of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere and contributes to global warming. But that problem isn't affecting their daily lives yet, and they have lots of present worries to keep them busy.
People in large industrialized democracies such as the United States like to think they are different: better information, more open societies, a more educated public. But, in fact, they are equally captive to special interest groups.
The United States, for example, promised at the 1992 Earth Summit in Rio to cut its carbon dioxide emissions back to 1990 levels by the year 2000. Instead, U.S. carbon emissions are 13 per cent higher than in 1990, and Washington is now saying that it can't get back down to the target figure until 2013. What happened?
The Clinton administration knows what it should do. At a preparatory meeting of the climate change conference in Geneva earlier this year, the U.S. mobilized the world's big insurance companies, which proved that global warming was already hitting their balance sheets in terms of soaring claims for storm damage.
That debunked the "junk science" (paid for by fossil-fuel producers) that pretends global warming is just an unproven hypothesis. With the European Union offering emission cuts to 15 per cent below 1990 levels by 2010 and the U.S. government also apparently on board, success in Kyoto seemed within reach.
What went wrong? Basically, Clinton belatedly realized that U.S. fossil-fuel producers, in alliance with other major industries that worry about higher energy costs, control enough U.S. senators to block any treaty that hurts their interests. So rather than fight a hopeless battle, he has chosen pre-emptive surrender.
Just as in Jakarta, the tail is wagging the dog. Many more Americans have an interest in curbing global warming than in the industries that cause it, but the majority is less conscious of its interests.
Without a serious American offer on cutting emissions, none of the other hold-outs at Kyoto will be under any pressure to yield, for the U.S. is the most flagrant polluter of all (four per cent of the world's population, 24 per cent of its greenhouse gas emissions). So the best that can be hoped for at Kyoto is dramatic failure.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Canada+limps+into+Kyoto+arena+GLOBAL+WARMING+%2F+Former+environmental+leader+%27spent+five+years+fiddling+while+everyone+else+moved+on.%27&amp;author=Knox%2C+Paul&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-28&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   GLOBAL WARMING / Former environmental leader 'spent five years fiddling while everyone else moved on.'
Canada limps into Kyoto arena
GLOBAL WARMING / Former environmental leader 'spent five years fiddling while everyone else moved on.'
Friday, November 28, 1997
The Globe and Mail
IT was 1988, and Canada was trying hard to be green.
At a pathbreaking conference in Toronto, Prime Minister Brian Mulroney called for an international law of the atmosphere to curb the human impact on the world's climate.
The next year, Lucien Bouchard, as federal environment minister, warned Canadians to prepare to change their lifestyles "radically" to combat global warming. Then, at the 1992 Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro, Canada pledged to reduce emissions of climate-changing gases to 1990 levels by 2000.
Today, the federal cabinet is having trouble deciding on its negotiating stand, just three days before the opening of the most crucial set of climate-change talks since Rio.
A news conference planned for today in Ottawa to announce Canada's position was abruptly cancelled last night, underscoring the indecision of a country that was once seen as a leader on environment issues.
Whatever role Canada chooses to play at the talks opening Monday in Kyoto, Japan, its image will take on a decidedly brownish cast.
Canada has failed spectacularly to meet its own targets for cuts in emissions. It has taken only timid steps to reduce fossil-fuel consumption.
And if the Kyoto meeting agrees on measures that offer economic opportunities as well as economic costs, Canada is increasingly likely to end up scrambling to adapt instead of breaking the trail.
"We spent five years fiddling while everyone else moved on," said Douglas Russell, a senior Canadian climate-change negotiator from 1991 to 1996 who resigned partly out of frustration at the lack of agreement among governments and industries on the issue.
The 10-day Kyoto conference is shaping up as a battle between industrialized countries and the developing world over who should bear the cost of actions to combat the threat of global warming.
But the final agreement may reward industrial nations that agree to help poorer countries become more energy-efficient.

  

The industrial nations would do this by exporting environmental technology or expertise. That could benefit European countries and the United States, which have made greater strides than Canada since the Rio summit in forging environmental links with the developing world.
Kyoto is the third in a series of conferences after the adoption in Rio of a global climate-change treaty. Its focus is setting mandatory limits on greenhouse gases, so called because they block the release of heat into outer space from the atmosphere.
Most human-created GHGs are produced through the burning of fossil fuels such as oil, gasoline, natural gas and coal. Other sources include aerosol sprays and methane from agriculture and garbage dumps.
Most climate scientists believe human activity, including greenhouse-gas production, has helped increase the Earth's average surface temperature over the past century. Many predict drastic consequences for people and industries if the planet continues to warm up.
Climate also is influenced by natural phenomena, and the precise human contribution to global warming is not known. But Canada and most other governments say they have enough evidence to conclude that some kind of action is needed.
"We now are going to have to make important decisions about the future without having absolute proof," said John Fraser, a former Progressive Conservative cabinet minister who is now Canada's special ambassador for the environment. "And I don't think it's beyond our intellectual capacity."
It's not the details of carbon atoms, sulphur aerosols or computerized climate models that will drive the debate at Kyoto. It's the strategic interest, both economic and environmental, of countries and negotiating blocs.
Each nation's position will be shaped by the answers to three questions: How grave is the threat to us from climate change? How much will it cost us to adapt? And what opportunities does the future offer?
Potential threats are almost endless.
A warming-induced rise of a metre in sea levels would swamp some island nations and leave millions homeless along coastlines, where much of the world's population growth is taking place. Rising temperatures could melt permafrost, extend the range of insect pests, affect forests and make storms more severe.
Estimates of the cost of reducing emissions vary greatly. Since the easiest way to cut GHGs would be to reduce fossil-fuel consumption, potential effects include higher prices for gasoline and electric power, and higher costs for job-creating industrial projects.
Four projections summarized in a recent Conference Board of Canada report forecast that stabilizing emissions at 1990 levels would cut between 0.5 and 2.3 percentage points off Canada's projected 30-per-cent economic growth between now and 2010.
But costs are hard to predict, because so many other factors affect the course of the economy. The mix of energy sources in each country is crucial, as are urban planning, transportation and energy-pricing policies.
Costs could be offset if, as U.S. President Bill Clinton has urged, delegates at Kyoto agree to work toward a permit-trading system, which would allow countries that reduced emissions quickly to sell unused quotas to those lagging behind. Possible benefits include increased trade involving environmental products and a reduction, in both rich and poor countries, of pollution unrelated to climate change that is also caused by burning fossil fuels.
Several emissions targets have been proposed in the months leading to Kyoto. The toughest, a 20-per-cent cut from 1990 levels by 2005, comes from the Alliance of Small Island States. The weakest is put forward by Australia, which wants to be allowed to boost its own emissions 18 per cent by 2010. That reflects a well-developed, energy-intensive economy with coal as its major export.
The 15-nation European Union proposes a 15-per-cent emissions cut, but says it will achieve this as a group, with its poorer members, such as Portugal and Greece, allowed more leeway than Germany and Britain. Japan is expected to propose a 5-per-cent cut.
Prime Minister Jean Chrétien is reportedly seeking to take a tougher stand than the United States, but Canada's position is not expected to differ significantly from that of its major trading partner.
In fact, the U.S. position is crucial to the entire Kyoto meeting.
The United States produces nearly 24 per cent of worldwide carbon-dioxide emissions, more than any other country. Its per-person share, nearly 20 tonnes a year, is also among the highest. No deal will be worth much unless the United States signs it.
Mr. Clinton surprised many last year when he endorsed mandatory emission controls. Washington now favours stabilizing emissions at 1990 levels by 2012,and is aggressively promoting the idea of countries being able to buy or sell emissions quotas.
U.S. negotiators also will demand that developing countries commit themselves to control emissions.
The U.S. Senate has declared that it will refuse to ratify any deal that lacks such guarantees. For some senators, such as those representing coal-producing states, that position may simply be a ploy to head off any agreement. But there are firm reasons for demanding such pledges from the developing world, which includes some of the world's biggest producers of greenhouse gases.
China, with nearly 15 per cent of the total, is No. 2. South Korea produces almost as much as France. With rapidly expanding heavy industries, such countries are expected to be major contributors during the next century.
"The real deal breaker is how to get the developing countries on board,"said Mr. Russell,who is now a partner in Global Change Strategies International Ltd., an Ottawa consulting firm.
Formally, the countries of Asia, Africa and Latin America form a negotiating bloc, but it includes everything from the small island states to oil-exporting Middle Eastern nations, which abhor the idea of cuts in fossil-fuel use.
The climate-change debate is a replay of earlier environmental controversies. Poor countries say universal mandatory targets would hobble their prosperity and force them to pay for the excesses of European and North American industry over the past two centuries.
Eventually, Mr. Russell said, the right mixture of incentives might persuade poor countries to agree to mandatory targets.
These could include emissions-quota trading and so-called joint-implementation agreements, under which rich countries would get credit for helping poor countries cut their emissions. Credit could also be given for increasing the area of "carbon sinks" such as forests, which reduce the amount of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere.
But delegates in Kyoto might have to settle for a deal that sets targets now for developed countries but merely prolongs the negotiating process for the developing world.
Technically speaking, the meeting will seek agreement on a protocol for action under the treaty signed in Rio, known as the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change. It would not come into effect until a specified number of countries ratified it. Delays of two or three years are not uncommon before international treaties take effect.
Still unclear is how mandatory controls would be enforced. Violators could lose voting rights in future treaty talks, or be barred from participating in permit trading. More extreme measures could include trade sanctions.
Any mandatory scheme adopted at Kyoto will force Canada's federal and provincial governments to do what they've been reluctant to do since Rio: decide who would pay the costs involved in achieving targets, and who would be "If the protocol put forward in Kyoto is not in Canada's interest, if it doesn't justify the risk and have support in Canadian society . . . Canada should be prepared not to sign it," Jim Hughes, manager of energy analysis for Imperial Oil Ltd., told a seminar audience in Toronto last week.
At the other end of the scale are insurance companies, whose mounting losses from storms and floods are persuading them that climate change is a real threat.

  

*******

  

Warning: Human-induced changes in Earth's climate may affect health and economic prospects.

  

Response: Talks opening Monday in Kyoto, Japan, will seek agreement on a program of cuts in fossil-fuel use and other measures.

  

Reaction: Uncertainty about climate change makes it hard to pinpoint what is needed. Severe cuts could do more harm than good by restricting economic growth.

  

Counter-reaction: The process offers a chance to get moving on environmental programs that have the potential to create growth and jobs.

THE BIG QUESTION Factoring in the human influence: [Surveys edition]

Author: Boulton, Leyla

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/big-question-factoring-human-influence/docview/248511364/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The past 18 months have seen a remarkable shift by industry lobbyists away from the argument that climate change is too uncertain to warrant fighting the problem. But the implications for multi-billion dollar industries that stand to lose from measures taken to reduce carbon dioxide emissions from the consumption of fossil fuels has made climate science one of the most politically-charged areas of scientific research. At the other end of the spectrum, the insurance industry, which believes it has the most to lose from inaction, has mobilised in support of tough emission cuts. But the WEC has since focused on both the difficulties and opportunities for promoting more efficient energy use to tackle climate change. Even US industry lobbyists, who have put heavy pressure on President Bill Clinton not to agree any greenhouse gas emission curbs at the conference on climate change in Kyoto next month, have changed tack.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=THE+BIG+QUESTION+Factoring+in+the+human+influence&amp;author=Boulton%2C+Leyla&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-28&amp;spage=06&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: * by Leyla Boulton
Factoring in the human influence
Climate change is "something that governments will need to visit over and over"
The past 18 months have seen a remarkable shift by industry lobbyists away from the argument that climate change is too uncertain to warrant fighting the problem.
A consensus has settled around the view of the Inter-governmental Panel on Climate Change , the scientists researching the problem for governments, that "the balance of evidence suggests there is a discernible human influence on global climate".
But the implications for multi-billion dollar industries that stand to lose from measures taken to reduce carbon dioxide emissions from the consumption of fossil fuels has made climate science one of the most politically-charged areas of scientific research. At the other end of the spectrum, the insurance industry, which believes it has the most to lose from inaction, has mobilised in support of tough emission cuts.
Just over a year ago, the World Energy Council , representing the energy industries of 100 countries, and the US Global Climate Coalition , were accusing the IPCC of distorting the evidence in a summary for policy makers.
Michael Jefferson, deputy secretary-general of the WEC, recently stood by his criticisms of the IPCC for not sticking with an original wording which said "the body of statistical evidence ... now points towards a discernible influence" of humans on climate.
But the WEC has since focused on both the difficulties and opportunities for promoting more efficient energy use to tackle climate change. Even US industry lobbyists, who have put heavy pressure on President Bill Clinton not to agree any greenhouse gas emission curbs at the conference on climate change in Kyoto next month, have changed tack.
Instead of openly attacking the integrity of the IPCC, which it last year accused of "scientific cleansing", the Global Climate Coalition has switched its focus to what one analyst described as the "soft underbelly" of Kyoto.
This is the contention, emphasised by a $13m television advertising campaign by US industry lobbyists, that developing nations are under no obligation to agree emission cuts expected of industrialised nations.
The change of target was in response to increasing public concern about the climate, a growing divergence of views within industry and a sharp rebuff by the US administration last year to "naysayers and special interests bent on . .. obfuscating climate change science".
The din over the basic science may have died down, but the the fact is that very little is known about the effect of climate change on the world.
"I can understand that politicians are at a loss as they don't have anything tangible to pin their arguments on," says Prof Bert Bolin, the IPCC's outgoing president.
The IPCC says the planet's average surface temperature could rise by up to 3.5 degree centigrade and sea levels by half a metre by 2100 if emissions of greenhouse gases, the most important of which is CO2 from the burning of fossil fuels, increase unabated.
This would see emissions of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere, currently at 360 parts per million by volume (ppmv), rising to 700ppmv by the end of the century, compared to a pre-industrial level of 280ppmv.
As well as rises in temperature and sea levels, some areas would suffer greater rainfall and others increased drought.
The "ideal" option in terms of greenhouse gases would be a return to pre-industrial levels which existed before the big increase in fossil fuel consumption which is thought to be affecting climate. But even IPCC scientists acknowledge this is not feasible, if only because it would deny developing countries the right to grow even if industrialised nations drastically cut emissions.
But if energy efficiency is increased and there is a shift to renewable energy, emissions could go up by 10-20 per cent in first half of the next century and then stabilise at 450ppmv by the end of next century. Under that scenario, temperatures could still rise, but by a smaller average of 1.5C or 2C by 2100. Sea levels could rise 20cm to 30cm.
As if that were not bad enough, it is also likely that climate change is already happening. Independent information suggests that a rise of between 0.3C and 0.6C in temperatures since the late 19th century is greater than anything over previous centuries.
Tom Karl, senior scientist at the National Climate Data Centre in Asheville, North Carolina, says that recent floods in the US have been consistent with what could be expected from global warming. Precipitation has increased by 10 per cent over the last century.
"We can't tell you this trend is going to be catastrophic but the changes are not inconsistent with the notion that they could be catastrophic," he said.
The uncertainty means the Kyoto conference is likely to be nothing but a first step towards mitigating the potential effects of climate change.
But there are two reasons for beginning to act early: first, carbon dioxide emitted now will stay in the atmosphere for at least 100 years and second, reductions begun now could reduce the need for precipitate action later.
Policymakers point to other benefits to the environment - for instance by reducing air pollution also caused by the consumption of fossil fuels - even if such policies prove inappropriate for climate change.
As scientific knowledge improves, climate change, according to Mr Karl, is "something that governments will need to visit over and over again to adjust policies accordingly". Copyright Financial Times Limited 1997. All Rights Reserved.

ALTERNATIVE ENERGY SOURCES Nothing quite like the sun: [Surveys edition]

Author: Boulton, Leyla

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/alternative-energy-sources-nothing-quite-like-sun/docview/248495061/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Solar energy from photovoltaic panels could be the single most important long-term means of achieving the deep cuts in the greenhouse gas emissions associated with climate change. This at least is the claim made by the UK government's solar industry task force earlier this month. But to take advantage of the fledgling technology's "explosive growth (expected) sometime early in the next century" it urged the government to stimulate investment in the technology in order to drive down costs. Building up domestic manufacturing capacity would enable Britain to capture a promising export market while creating new jobs at home in a labour-intensive industry .

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=ALTERNATIVE+ENERGY+SOURCES+Nothing+quite+like+the+sun&amp;author=Boulton%2C+Leyla&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-28&amp;spage=05&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: * by Leyla Boulton
Nothing quite like the sun
Japan and the US have adopted a more aggressive role to advance solar power
Solar energy from photovoltaic panels could be the single most important long-term means of achieving the deep cuts in the greenhouse gas emissions associated with climate change.
This at least is the claim made by the UK government's solar industry task force earlier this month.
But to take advantage of the fledgling technology's "explosive growth (expected) sometime early in the next century" it urged the government to stimulate investment in the technology in order to drive down costs. Building up domestic manufacturing capacity would enable Britain to capture a promising export market while creating new jobs at home in a labour-intensive industry .
The alternative of "allowing our competitors to corner an excessive share of the global market ... would effectively be ceding domestic jobs to them", warned the task force.
Its members include two UK manufacturers, BP Solar and Intersolar, plus banks and insurance companies which increasingly perceive the potential effects of climate change as a threat to their business.
The group is chaired by Jeremy Leggett, a former environmentalist who has also set up Solar Century , a global purchasing club for solar power which is the sort of private sector initiative traditionally preferred by the UK.
But a few governments, namely the US and Japan, two of the foreign competitors cited by the task force, have opted for more aggressive market-forcing programmes. These attempt to drive down the costs of solar versus conventional energy through government procurement or pump-priming.
Japan, host of the December 1-10 inter-governmental conference in Kyoto on climate change, has led the way with a plan to install solar roofs on 70,000 homes by 2002. It is home to Kyocera , a market leader in emerging international solar markets.
In June, the US administration unveiled a programme to install 1m solar roofs in the US by 2010 although the details of how it proposes to do so are unclear.
The UK government meanwhile is reviewing its policy on renewables which it wants to generate 10 per cent of the country's electricity needs by 2010. But it is waiting for the outcome of the Kyoto meeting before publishing the review's conclusions early next year.
Michael Meacher, environment minister, recently appeared to rule out suggestions that the government provide £18m annual support sought by the British Photovoltaic Association . In return the industry had offered to build £100m manufacturing capacity in the UK.
British Petroleum , whose subsidiary BP Solar , with 10 per cent of the market, is the world's third biggest producer of photovoltaic panels, believes that with "appropriate government support and investment" solar can become competitive in supplying peak electricity demand within the next 10 years.
But Mr Meacher, appearing to foreshadow the outcome of the review, said solar power was still too "expensive" to justify UK subsidies on the scale sought by the British Photovoltaic Association and it would only come into its own in "five or 10 years time". The government believes it will get more value for money in the meantime by promoting wind power and more efficient use of fossil fuels.
Chris Rose, deputy director of Greenpeace UK , the environmental pressure group, argues, however, that "there's only so much belt- tightening you can do with the consumption of fossil fuels" in order to combat climate change.
Once these gains have been made, renewables such as solar will be necessary to drive greenhouse gas emissions down to the level required seriously to mitigate climate change.
The British Photovoltaic Association concedes that "PV is not expected to become competitive as a pure generation source under UK conditions for the foreseeable future", with one exception - photovoltaic panels, "high-grade architectural claddings costing £600-£1,000 per square metre".
This use is at the crux of Mr Leggett's Solar Century, which is trying to recruit banks and insurance companies to install PV panels as cladding on office buildings.
And Mr Leggett says there is much more the UK government can do to support the industry without handing out large subsidies.
Apart from tax breaks, these include introducing mandatory requirements for new construction to contain a minimum amount of solar in suitable buildings and easing of planning permission for projects including PV.
In the absence of significant government support, however, private UK companies are likely to focus on chasing foreign business as best they can.
One solar market that is already viable is the electrification of remote communities in the Third World. Solar technology was the best way to generate electricity "for millions of households in the developing world," the Washington-based World Watch Institute said in Shining Examples, a report detailing local schemes which help finance the panels' installation.
British Petroleum plans to increase its sales of photovoltaic panels 10-fold to $1bn a year over the next decade. John Browne, BP's chief executive, noted recently that although there was sufficient sunlight to generate all the energy the world needs, it now generates only 0.001 per cent of the total.
By 2020 however he believes that up to 5 per cent of world energy could be supplied by renewable energy such as solar power. More recently, Royal Dutch / Shell , the world's biggest oil company, joined the fray with an announcement that it would spend $500m on developing a fifth core business in renewables including solar.
Established participants such as BP, are worried less about newcomers, such as Shell, than about existing competitors such as Kyocera which stand to receive a big boost from the Japanese government.
The proprietary nature of much of the technology also means that even Shell will be hard-placed to break into the market, says Bob Kelly, chairman and chief executive officer of Houston-based Enron Renewable Energy Corporation . Copyright Financial Times Limited 1997. All Rights Reserved.
