Full text: We all learned in science class that hypotheses are supposed to be tested against observation. But in the global-warming debate, it appears this simple axiom does not apply: Climate change has become an article of faith
SCIENCE FICTION The day the Earth warmed up
We all learned in science class that hypotheses are supposed to be tested against observation. But in the global-warming debate, it appears this simple axiom does not apply: Climate change has become an article of faith
Saturday, November 22, 1997
Toronto -- A FUNNY thing happened on the way to the international global-warming conference: The Earth failed to heat up.
Yes, you heard correctly. Over the past decade, the Earth's average surface temperature has increased by zero. Nothing. Nada. In fact, there's evidence that it's actually cooled a bit.
But you wouldn't know that from the confusing media coverage of the climate-change question. The debate about whether the burning of fossil fuels is having any effect on the atmosphere has become increasingly polarized with the approach of the Dec. 1 summit in Kyoto, Japan, of the (take a deep breath) Parties to the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change.
The Party delegates are not meeting to chew over the science of the issue. They already agreed in Rio de Janeiro in 1992, and in Berlin in 1994, to stabilize the atmospheric release of heat-trapping "greenhouse gases," such as carbon dioxide and methane, at 1990 levels. This was to protect us from the supposed danger of being fried in a planetary convection oven. Rather, they are meeting to negotiate a legally binding deadline, likely extending the original voluntary one of the year 2000 by about 10 years.
The Europeans (for whom this is very much a trade issue because of their more diversified energy economies) are pushing hardest. U.S. President Bill Clinton supports the targets, but the U.S. position is that it won't sign the treaty unless developing nations, which will contribute to the greatest increase in emissions in the years to come, agree to some obligations in Kyoto.
Prime Minister Jean Chrétien apparently wants to outdo the Americans and, at a recent meeting, federal and provincial environment ministers agreed to endorse the UN stabilization plan and revised deadline. Only Quebec objected, saying the measures don't go far enough.
Yet Canada is behind the eight ball. Despite voluntary industry initiatives, Natural Resources Canada projects emissions will be 8.2 per cent higher in the year 2000 than in 1990, and 36 per cent higher by 2020. Reversing the trend will be costly. Emission limits, trade sanctions and special taxes could have a profound impact on Canada's resource-dependent economy, especially on the steel and aluminum industries, energy production, transportation, cattle farming and agriculture. To appease Alberta, Mr. Chrétien recently ruled out a well-head carbon tax, but other taxes could force retail fuel prices to soar.
This is serious stuff.
The Conference Board of Canada predicts compliance measures needed to return emissions to 1990 levels will reduce Canada's annual economic growth by $5.4-billion to $30-billion by the year 2010. An economic simulation prepared for Environment Canada by DRI/McGraw Hill suggests stabilization would require a phased-in 50-per-cent hike in energy prices.
As a result, between now and 2012, the economy could underperform to the tune of $150-billion, with living standards lowered by higher prices and interests rates, plus a weak Canadian dollar.
Though we will all be asked to accept such sacrifices, it's difficult for the lay person to determine whether or not the science behind the climate-change theory is sound. Environmentalists, some scientists and politicians argue -- passionately -- that the phenomenon is real. Oil companies, certain scientists and other politicians counter -- just as passionately -- that it's all a bunch of hooey.
Who's right?
The answer, of course, lies in the evidence. When I agreed to research and write this story, I had not formed an opinion one way or the other. But as this environmental detective story unfolded, I found that both sides -- the environmentalists and the industry lobby groups -- have been self-serving and subjective.
The evidence appears to have been tampered with, the jury rigged. Politicians, UN bureaucrats and some scientists have tried to discredit skeptical scientists (aren't scientists supposed to be skeptical?) for challenging the conventional wisdom.
We all learned in science class that hypotheses are supposed to be tested against observation. But in the global-warming debate, it appears this simple axiom does not apply: Climate change has become an article of faith.
The UN Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change forecasts a rise in global temperature of between 0.8 and 3.5 degrees if something doesn't halt the doubling of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere expected in the next century. (This is down from earlier estimates of 1.5 to 4.5 degrees).
By now, we're all familiar with the presumed Doomsday that will result if this happens. In a speech this month, Mr. Chrétien compared the oil companies to the tobacco giants in their denial of their products' harmful effects, then reminded his audience that, "If we do not take action, climate change will create droughts, floods and other disasters around the world. It will create unprecedented tides of refugees." He didn't belabour the other alleged consequences, which include: violent storms, tornadoes, forest fires, crop failures, rising sea levels and plagues spread by mosquitoes that will migrate into formerly frigid regions.

  

GLOBAL-warming theory originated in the writings of 19th-century French mathematician and scientist Jean Fourier, and was given modern credence in 1988 when NASA climatologist Jim Hansen testified before the U.S. Congress that the summer drought that year was attributable in part to global warming.
A number of groups soon discovered they could profit from the bad news. Environmental organizations such as Greenpeace have found the apocalyptic scenario is the best fundraising tool since baby seals. Greenpeace has organized expeditions at both ends of the Earth to report on everything from melting icecaps in Antarctica to thawing permafrost in the Mackenzie River Basin.
The Suzuki Foundation's Climate Change Campaign has run splashy ads calling global warming "the most urgent slow-motion catastrophe facing mankind." The mosquito story was given special emphasis in the ads, as was the opportunity to make donations. (The mosquito theory was recently discredited in the Nov. 7 issue of the journal Science.)
Government scientists have cashed in too. The U.S. government doles out more than $2-billion each year for researchers to study the issue. In Canada, government departments and universities have benefited from an $85-million Global Warming Strategy fund established in 1992 under the Federal Green Plan, among other revenues. This doesn't mean their agenda amounts to malfeasance; only that, with other funding spigots turned off, scientists in the public service have a vested interest in producing research that remains "inconclusive" so that they can continue to attract funding.
For many politicians, the scientific case is closed. On July 26, President Clinton opened a White House conference on the subject with the announcement that "The overwhelming balance of evidence and scientific opinion is that it is no longer a theory but now a fact that global warming is real." He cited the supposed consensus of 2,500 scientists in an oft-cited report of the UN's Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change. Vice-President Al Gore seems to attribute every extreme weather event to global climate change. On one occasion, Interior Secretary Bruce Babbitt even stated that skeptics of the Clinton administration's global-warming proposals are "un-American in the most basic sense."
With these endorsements, is it any wonder ordinary folk are scared out of their wits and tell pollsters they support strong government action? However, before you start building your own sea wall against a future Water World, there are a few things to consider.
First, the growing "consensus of scientists" mentioned by Mr. Clinton (and just about every uncritical news story) is a complete myth. Climate change is hotly debated among scientists, and the list of skeptics is growing. Leading the charge are four renowned climatologists: Dr. Patrick J. Michaels of the University of Virginia in Charlottesville, North Carolina; Dr. Robert Balling, a climatology professor at Arizona State University in Tempe; Dr. Richard Lindzen, Alfred P. Sloan professor of meteorology at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology in Boston; and Dr. Fred Singer, distinguished research professor at George Mason University and professor emeritus of environmental science at the University of Virginia.
These four horsemen of the nonapocalypse speak at conferences, testify before Congress and publish papers that use real-world measurements to effectively skewer the computer-driven climate projections of their peers, including those of the UN panel. A few of them receive some funding from fossil-fuel companies and are therefore dismissed by environmentalists as industry shills or tools of the U.S. anti-environmental "wise use" movement.
But discrediting these scientists because of their funding instead of their work is hardly credible. Their papers are peer-reviewed and appear in respected journals such as Science and Nature. Less than 5 per cent of Dr. Singer's annual budget comes from oil companies (a $5,000 donation from Exxon). In fact, environmental groups themselves receive millions of dollars in corporate donations. Does this mean their message is tainted?
In any case, the evidence (as opposed to the theory) is increasingly supportive of the observations of these modern-day Galileos, once dismissed as a "small and noisy band of skeptics."
In 1995, the UN panel released a thick report containing sections in which scientists took pains to state that global warming and climate change are not yet proven. But politicians and environmental groups never quote these sections. Instead, they seize upon a statement -- that human activities are having a "discernible effect" on the atmosphere -- appearing in the Policymaker's Summary written by a small group that included UN bureaucrats and other non-scientists.
Says Dr. Patrick Michaels: "The 'discernible effect' statement may be politically sensational, but it's scientifically bankrupt." He and others accused the writers of the Policymaker's Summary of spin-doctoring and of deliberately playing down the high degree of scientific uncertainty about the global-warming theory.
Dr. Fred Singer argues that the report does not represent a "consensus of 2,500 scientists," pointing out that its 11 chapters were written by only about 80 authors. Most of the hundreds of "contributors" listed are simply experts who allowed their studies to be quoted without necessarily supporting the report's conclusions.
Says Dr. Frederick Seitz, "In my more than 60 years as a member of the American scientific community, including service as president of both the National Academy of Sciences and the American Physical Society, I have never witnessed a more disturbing corruption of the peer-review process."
At an international meeting in 1995, 100 climatologists showed that they were fed up with the way bureaucrats had distorted their research by signing a Leipzig Declaration, which expressed their anger over the unreliable global-warming projections generated through computer-based General Circulation Models. Yet the politicians and the media continue to refer to the "consensus of scientists."
Climate-change skeptics agree that Earth's average temperatures have risen by a modest 0.3 to 0.6 degrees in the 140 years since records began. But they point out that most of this warming occurred before 1940 and was followed by decades of climate cooling, from about 1940 through the 1960s, at a time when greenhouse-gas emissions actually increased. Many climatologists feared the world was heading toward a mini-ice age. Even the UN panel's chairman Bert Bolin admits that the pre-1940 warming is probably a natural recovery from an earlier natural cooling.
Indeed, of the 96 years of this century, so far 32 show a warming trend and 64 a cooling. The warming has not been evenly distributed. Some parts of the globe have not warmed at all, while others, like Canada's interior and the Antarctic Peninsula, appear to have warmed by one and two degrees respectively.
But the most damning evidence concerns the past decade. Weather satellites have detected no increase at all. On the contrary, they indicate a cooling of 0.13 since 1979, and the data are independently verified by weather-balloon records as well as the readings of ground-level instruments calibrated to account for urban "heat island" effects.
Dr. Michaels is unequivocal: "The perfect agreement between the satellite data and the weather balloons has been extremely humiliating for the global-warming proponents . . . Even worse for them, the land-surface records of the past decade show a warming of zero."
But the other side is not backing down. Dr. Gordon McBean is assistant deputy minister in the atmospheric environmental service of Environment Canada and formerly chaired the international World Climate Research Program. While he doesn't dispute the accuracy of the satellite data, he feels the skeptics have reached the wrong conclusions. "It's inappropriate to judge long-term global-warming trends from satellite records that only began in about 1979," he says. Dr. McBean argues that changes in the atmosphere indicate that human actions are affecting the climate in ways that solar spots or volcanic activity would not. "Our computer models now give a reasonable simulation of the climate since about 1900 and, though the amount of climate change predicted varies, they all indicate it will occur."
Dr. Michaels observes that this kind of explanation is typical of "consensus" scientists. "Scientists are supposed to look at evidence, not run from it," he says, adding, "When the temperature didn't go up as predicted, the increasingly desperate and shrill apologists for the warming theory changed their mantra to the vague message of climate change." Dr. Michaels and other skeptics also point out that there is no evidence at all that Earth has suffered from an increase in violent weather. For instance, studies of anemometer readings used to gauge hurricanes since the Second World War show a decline in the strength of tropical storms, not an increase.
It's a touchy issue. Hurricane Andrew, in 1992, was the most expensive U.S. disaster ever, causing $30-billion (U.S.) in damage. But is this because the weather is worse or because more people inhabit Florida's coastline than decades ago? Atlantic hurricane activity over the warming period between 1970 and 1987 was less than half the activity observed for the cooling period between 1947 and 1969.
There is no consensus in the insurance industry that climate change is upon us. U.S. insurance and reinsurance companies (among the most affected) remain particularly skeptical. Even the UN panel report states: "Overall, there is no evidence that extreme weather events, or climate variability, have increased, in a global sense through the 20th century, although data and analysis are poor and not comprehensive."
Dr. Singer points out that: "A small rise in sea levels of about 18 centimetres per century has been observed for several centuries, but there is evidence that this derives from the drifting of continental plates and subsequent tectonic changes in the shape of the ocean basin, not from the thermal expansion of sea water." He says that during the warming period between 1900 and 1940, there was actually a drop in sea levels indicating that global warming, if it occurs, could in fact slow any rise in sea levels.
Another troubling question is one that the UN Intergovernmental Panel's report fully acknowledges: Even if the climate is changing, it's impossible to say to what (if any) extent human beings are influencing what might be natural variation.
Change is inevitable for climate, especially if one takes a long-term view. Ice ages occur roughly every 100,000 years, followed by warm periods lasting 10,000 to 12,000 years. Even the warm periods experience wild fluctuations. Between 5,000 and 8,000 years ago, Earth went through long periods that were two to three degrees hotter than today. Paleoclimatologists at the University of Maine have discovered from fossils that the climate of the Prairies took a sudden turn for the better about 800 years ago after 1,500 years of dust-bowl conditions. The abrupt change from dry to wet cycles, they say, may be connected to different moon and sun cycles. What is now the arid Sahara desert was once a swampy land populated by hippos and crocodiles.
Had a fine English wine recently? Doubtful. Yet, records show that a few hundred years ago England's climate was ideal for growing grapes. This was during a short-lived hot spell in the middle of what climatologists call the Little Ice Age, which lasted from the early 1400s to about 1850. This period suggests that cold climates, not warm, spawn the most violent weather. Severe storms in the North Sea in 1421 and 1446 claimed 100,000 lives, while a storm in 1570 claimed more than 400,000.
Earth temperatures closely correlate to sunspot and solar-flare activity, which occurs in cycles of roughly 11 years within much longer trends. When the number of sunspots was sharply reduced between 1640 and 1720, our planet cooled about 1.1 degrees and Europe was plunged into a deep freeze. Glaciers and winters lengthened, and English paintings from the time show people skating on the Thames River and celebrating freezings with Frost Fairs. In pioneer days, people walked in winter from Staten Island to Manhattan across a frozen New York harbour.
Experts at the Naval Research Laboratory in Washington say that about 50 per cent of the climate change since 1850 can be attributed to the sun, and average temperatures since the Second World War have closely tracked sunspot cycles. Complicating matters, geophysicists at the University of Hawaii claim that increased undersea seismicity and volcanic activity on the Pacific Ocean floor may be contributing to the appearance of strong El Nino weather events in recent years.
So, what should we do?
The conclusion of Volume 3 of the UN Panel report states: "A prudent way to deal with climate change is through a portfolio of actions aimed at mitigation, adaptation and improvement of knowledge. The appropriate portfolio will differ for each country. The challenge is not to find the best policy today for the next 100 years, but to select a prudent strategy and to adjust it over time in light of new information."
That certainly sounds reasonable. Given all the evidence that contradicts the theory that the Earth is warming, that the climate is changing, and that mankind is the cause, perhaps the Canadian delegation in Kyoto should argue that the global-warming hypothesis needs to be revised. A prudent strategy for Canada would be to monitor the situation, but any decision to limit greenhouse-gas emissions would be premature and should be shelved.

  

Guy Crittenden is editor-in-chief of Hazardous Waste Materials Management magazine and Solid Waste and Recycling magazine in Markham, Ont.
Climate-change diary
Fall, 1990, Geneva : The First Scientific Assessment of the United Nations Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (formed in 1988) lays the foundation for the Rio Treaty.
June, 1992, Rio de Janeiro : Some 154 Earth Summit delegates sign the UN Framework Con-vention on Climate Change, agreeing to stabilize greenhouse-gas emissions at 1990 levels by the year 2000.
April, 1995, Berlin : The First Conference of the Parties to the 1992 UN convention strengthen their commitment to the Rio Treaty with the Berlin Mandate.
November, 1995, Leipzig, Germany: One-hundred independent climatologists sign the Leip - zig Declaration to protest the distortion of atmospheric science by bureaucrats in the service of political agendas.
July, 1996, Geneva: The Second Conference of the Parties leads to the Geneva Declaration, which calls for the post-2000 commitments to be legally binding.
June, 1997, Denver, Colorado : At a summit of G7 nations, Canada, the U.S. and Japan agree to modify their earlier commitments and push for a10-year extension to the emissions dead-line.
Dec. 1-10, 1997, Kyoto, Japan : The Third Conference of the Parties will negotiate legally binding greenhouse-gas reduction targets and strict timetables as agreed in the Geneva Decla-ration, likely extending the 2000 stabilization deadline to 2010 or 2012.

Title: SCIENCE FICTION The day the Earth warmed up We all learned in science class that hypotheses are supposed to be tested against observation. But in the global-warming debate, it appears this simple axiom does not apply: Climate change has become an article of faith

Global warming: [Final Edition]

Author: Hume, Stephen

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming/docview/242901167/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Potential for increases in the northward range of encephalitis- bearing mosquitoes; the frequency and severity of water-borne infections including hemorrhagic e-coli, typhoid, toxoplasomosis, cryptosporidium and salmonella; increases in the range and incidence of tick-borne infections like Lyme disease and Rocky Mountain fever; seasonal epidemics of upper respiratory ailments associated with automobile smog and ground level ozone in the Lower Mainland; expanded range for bats infected with rabies; exploding rodent populations infected with hantavirus; the spread of coastal toxic algae blooms which contaminate shellfish.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Global+warming%3A&amp;author=Hume%2C+Stephen&amp;issn=08321299&amp;title=The+Vancouver+Sun&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-22&amp;spage=H.1.FRO&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Ran with factboxes "Health risks of global warming" which has been appended to the end of this story.
Here come the bugs: The biggest increase ever recorded in water surface temperature off the West Coast portends the arrival of a slew of diseases once confined to a tropical ghetto.
Last summer, while ocean scientists recorded the sharpest increase in surface temperatures in history off the West Coast, public health officials were tracking a worrisome epidemic of poisonings caused by a sea-based bacteria known as vibrio parahaemoliticus.
Although the bacteria is always present, it is almost never a major health concern. But in 1997, as ocean surface temperatures rose from one to three degrees, British Columbia suddenly had about 100 Vibrio poisonings, confirms Shaun Peck, deputy public health officer for the provincial government.
Klaus Schallie, the regional shellfish expert for the Canadian food inspection agency, says he's reluctant to tie the outbreak directly to rising water temperatures because toxic algae blooms known as red tides are not uncommon. He wonders whether the vibrio outbreak was connected instead to a sharp decrease in coastal salinity caused by rapidly melting snowpacks in the interior.
"I don't believe it was a single factor like temperature. It's complicated. There are a lot of unknowns. It's really hard to make a generalization," Schallie says. But he agrees that the vibrio outbreak was unprecedented.
The B.C. phenomenon provided an eerie echo of the Atlantic seaboard, where proliferating toxic algae blooms were accompanied by outbreaks of Pfiesteria piscicida, a tiny sea creature that mutates and causes flesh-eating infections. Last summer it proved a major disruption for a $1.5-billion fishing industry.
Donald Anderson, director of the National Office for Marine Biotoxins and Harmful Algae Blooms at the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institute, assessed the problem for the media last month.
"The problem is big and the problem is serious," he said. The organism has killed one billion fish in eastern U.S. waters since 1991.
Increasingly, while mainstream science debates its skeptical fringe over whether global warming is a natural cycle or a man-made phenomenon, public health physicians sweat about the practical risks that rising temperatures already pose for hundreds of millions of people.
A rise in average planetary temperature - even by as little as one or two degrees over the next century - will expose vast populations in temperate regions of Europe, North America and Asia to exotic diseases previously restricted to a narrow tropical zone.
And Andrew Weaver, a prize-winning scientist at the University of Victoria's School of Earth and Ocean Sciences, says world temperature is indisputably on the rise.
Concrete evidence of global warming includes the melting of permafrost across Canada's arctic, the rapid retreat of alpine and arctic glaciers and, perhaps most telling, insurance industry records, which show extreme weather events are more frequent.
The annual inflation-adjusted insured cost of major windstorms, for example, has increased from about $500 million in 1966 to more than $11 billion in 1996, says Jim Harries, policy development manager for the Insurance Bureau of Canada.
The economic implications extend beyond property damage.
In Florida, where public health officials are battling a tenacious regional outbreak of a mosquito-borne virus that can cause fatal brain inflammations, even Orlando's Walt Disney World has been forced to take precautions. Hotel pools, water parks, golf courses, fishing and campfire areas are now closed to tourists during evening hours when the mosquitoes' bite is on.
And Caribbean cruise ships now avoid ports where mosquito- transmitted dengue fever is prevalent, placing the region's $12- billion US tourist industry at risk, warns Paul Epstein, of Harvard Medical School's Centre for Health and the Global Environment.
Malaria and cholera - both associated with rising water temperatures - have returned to Europe with a vengeance on the heels of the hottest summers in recorded history.
Europe had 200,000 malaria cases in 1995. The World Health Organization predicts millions of new sufferers by 2000. The range of malaria mosquitoes already extends as far north as Britain. Meanwhile, in the last four years, the number of registered cholera cases - a disease once thought eradicated - increased ninefold.
North American public health authorities have similar headaches. Malaria outbreaks occurred in New Jersey in 1991 and in New York in 1993. Since 1980, mosquito-borne encephalitis epidemics have occurred in Florida, Mississippi, New Orleans, Texas, Arizona, California and Colorado.
B.C. has had more than 600 malaria cases reported over the last 18 months. While all of these are believed to involve travellers from Asia, mosquitoes capable of transmitting the disease are present in the Lower Mainland.
The absence of a reservoir of malarial infection in the human population prevents local transmission in B.C., but public health officials acknowledge that the possibility is clearly there.
Expansion of range for mosquitoes carrying disease-causing parasites like malaria and deadly viruses like those which cause yellow fever, dengue fever and encephalitis are emerging as a major public health concern worldwide.
And rising water temperature will aid in the spread of cholera - and accelerate a worldwide expansion in the number of what scientists call HABs or harmful algae blooms.
"We have to be thinking about these things," warns Arlene King, an epidemiologist at the B.C. Centre for Disease Control in Vancouver. "One of these days we're going to wake up and say: `It's finally happened. Why didn't we do anything about it?' "
But she says public health authorities are chronically under- resourced, stretched thin from dealing with more immediate outbreaks.
"Public health is not glamorous. It's not sexy like tertiary health care," she says. "It's hard to get people to pay attention to public health issues - particularly long-term ones."
That view is shared by the World Health Organization, which warned in its 1996 report that a "fatal complacency" greets resurgent malaria, cholera, encephalitis and the emergence of 30 new infectious diseases which include the almost always fatal hemmorhagic fever Ebola and North America's lethal hantavirus.
Weaver says skeptics on global warming are either working with data which is profoundly out of date or have expertise in other disciplines and do not fully understand climate change.
He says the most compelling evidence that the planet is warming up comes not from abstract theory but from direct comparisons between historical records of actual change and what mathematical models predict for the same periods.
For example, historical records at Ellensburg, Wash., show an increase in average temperature of one degree and a 20-per-cent increase in average annual precipitation since 1929.
Greater rainfall worries epidemiologists, too. It means more turbidity in drinking water. That means increased viral, bacterial and protozoan load on systems where provincewide there are already 240 boil-water advisories in effect for community water supplies.
Outbreaks of cryptosporidium and toxoplasmosis have affected even large urban water supplies in B.C. in the last few years.
"If you have more rain and flash flooding, you're going to get more flushing," says King. "We don't want more animal and human feces being flushed into our water systems."
Peck says the medical concern approaches consensus. He returned from the annual conference of the American Public Health Association with a sheaf of medical literature expressing worry over long-term health risks from rising temperatures (See information box: Effects of risng temperatures on human health).
Among the major concerns for Canadian epidemiologists:
Potential for increases in the northward range of encephalitis- bearing mosquitoes; the frequency and severity of water-borne infections including hemorrhagic e-coli, typhoid, toxoplasomosis, cryptosporidium and salmonella; increases in the range and incidence of tick-borne infections like Lyme disease and Rocky Mountain fever; seasonal epidemics of upper respiratory ailments associated with automobile smog and ground level ozone in the Lower Mainland; expanded range for bats infected with rabies; exploding rodent populations infected with hantavirus; the spread of coastal toxic algae blooms which contaminate shellfish.
"I think we should be concerned," says King. "We should be planning about how to cope. One of the things we have to think about is really beefing up our public health capacity to deal with these issues that I think are invevitable.
"The single biggest vaccination against future problems is to educate as many people as possible."
HEALTH RISKS OF GLOBAL WARMING
Among the major concerns for Canadian epidemiologists:
- The potential for increases in the northward range of encephalitis-bearing mosquitoes.
- The frequency and severity of water-borne infections including hemorrhagic e-coli, typhoid, toxoplasomosis, cryptosporidium and salmonella.
- Increases in the range and incidence of tick-borne infections like Lyme disease and Rocky Mountain fever.
- Seasonal epidemics of upper respiratory ailments associated with automobile smog and ground level ozone in the Lower Mainland.
- Expanded range for bats infected with rabies.
- Exploding rodent populations infected with hantavirus.
- The spread of coastal toxic algae blooms which contaminate shellfish.

Emissions: [Final Edition]

Author: Robinson, John

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/emissions/docview/242928979/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: At the 1992 Earth Summit in Rio, Canada led the way in arguing for a strong climate policy. Today, Canada has abandoned its original target of stabilizing greenhouse gas emissions at 1990 levels by the year 2000. It now predicts emissions will be 13 per cent higher in 2000 than they were just seven years ago. While great uncertainty does exist on the details of climate change, the existence of a greenhouse effect is not in dispute, nor is the fact that the atmospheric concentrations of greenhouse gases like carbon dioxide are increasing due mainly to fossil-fuel burning and land clearing. There is a strong scientific consensus that human activities already affect the climate and that continued growth in emissions will only accelerate these changes. It is striking how little attention is paid to the impacts. Indeed, several years ago, at least one poll suggested that winter- weary Canadians liked the idea of global warming. Part of the problem is that little can be said with certainty about specific local and regional impacts of climate change. Nevertheless, there has been a great deal of analysis, in Canada and elsewhere, and these studies paint a disturbing picture of potential changes in temperature, precipitation and the frequency of storms, droughts, forest fires, insect infestations and floods.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Emissions%3A&amp;author=Robinson%2C+John&amp;issn=08321299&amp;title=The+Vancouver+Sun&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-21&amp;spage=A.21&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: We need more than hot air: Admittedly there is controversy over greenhouse gases and global warming. But public debate cannot afford to ignore them.
At the 1992 Earth Summit in Rio, Canada led the way in arguing for a strong climate policy. Today, Canada has abandoned its original target of stabilizing greenhouse gas emissions at 1990 levels by the year 2000. It now predicts emissions will be 13 per cent higher in 2000 than they were just seven years ago.
Until recently, Canada resisted the idea of targets and timetables, and will apparently only adopt a target before December's meeting in Kyoto to avoid international embarrassment.
The political reasons for Canada's backpedalling are rooted in the influence of Alberta and the oil industry. However, just as striking as the political foot-dragging is the degree to which the terms of the debate have shifted. Certain taken-for-granted positions have narrowed the debate and prevented important arguments from being taken seriously. The result is a wrong-headed framing of the debate, much public confusion and, most importantly, missed opportunities.
What are these dubious propositions? The first is that the science of climate change is uncertain. Never in history has an environmental issue been so extensively studied nor has there been so elaborate a process of reviewing the science and coming to an international consensus. Yet we continue to hear how uncertain the science is.
While great uncertainty does exist on the details of climate change, the existence of a greenhouse effect is not in dispute, nor is the fact that the atmospheric concentrations of greenhouse gases like carbon dioxide are increasing due mainly to fossil-fuel burning and land clearing. There is a strong scientific consensus that human activities already affect the climate and that continued growth in emissions will only accelerate these changes.
Skeptics exist. Indeed, they should be welcomed. But to pretend that the existence of a very small number of skeptics demonstrates deep scientific uncertainty about the existence of climate change is simply wrong.
The second dubious proposition is that we can ignore the potential impacts of climate change.
It is striking how little attention is paid to the impacts. Indeed, several years ago, at least one poll suggested that winter- weary Canadians liked the idea of global warming. Part of the problem is that little can be said with certainty about specific local and regional impacts of climate change. Nevertheless, there has been a great deal of analysis, in Canada and elsewhere, and these studies paint a disturbing picture of potential changes in temperature, precipitation and the frequency of storms, droughts, forest fires, insect infestations and floods.
All of those would have significant impacts on forestry, agriculture, human health, and communities and economies around the world. The world will not collapse due to global climate change, but it is prudent to undertake measures to reduce the risk of significant damages. It's no accident that the insurance industry is calling for stronger policy measures to reduce greenhouse-gas emissions.
The third dubious proposition is that the costs of reducing emissions are too high. This is a canard.
Hundreds of studies have pointed out energy-efficiency measures that would not only reduce emissions, but also cut costs. Our government has simply failed to deliver on these `worth-doing- anyway' measures. Beyond this, we should reduce emissions if the costs are less than the damages we would thereby avoid.
The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change has estimated that damage might amount to several percentage points of the gross domestic product in industrialized countries. In addition, we should take into account other benefits such as reduced smog, congestion and the health impacts of local air pollution.
Finally, it would be prudent to spend modest additional amounts, a kind of insurance premium, to avoid the risk of catastrophic impacts for which we can't yet attach costs.
What do these considerations mean for Canada? Aren't we so big, so cold and so dependent on cheap energy, that attempts to reduce emissions would be ruinously expensive, as Alberta Energy Minister Ty Lund suggests?
The accompanying chart shows Canada's carbon dioxide emissions between 1958 and 1994. It illustrates three points.
First, emissions were already nearly stabilized from the early 1970s to the early 1990s. While past forecasts of emissions rose sharply, the actual experience was of very slow growth.
Second, while emissions almost stabilized, GDP continued to grow. So much for ruinous impacts.
Third, it suggests that achieving the stabilization target of the United Nations' framework convention is quite a reasonable goal.
Why then does conventional wisdom suggest the opposite? In some respects a bigger obstacle than the energy industry is the federal energy department's ideological objection to the very idea of an active climate policy.
Part is the neglect of the damage side and part is the fact that, even if Canadian society as a whole benefits, certain sectors, industries and political constituencies will be hurt. Canada's large energy-producing industry and its powerful political lobby oppose any significant measures to reduce fossil fuel emissions.
In all the fear-mongering, what matters is what gets ignored. The tendency to discount the science, ignore the impacts, and focus on the costs prevents us from seeing that the climate-change issue may represent a major opportunity. If the recent rhetoric has any substance at all, then we should consider whether an activist, climate policy might be a significant lever in allowing us to lead in the new information-technology age.
In other words, enacting a policy that would foster the move to environmentally benign, energy-efficient and information-intensive technologies may be a good way to enhance Canadian competitiveness in the global economy. Far from incurring ruinous costs, the countries and companies that move fastest in developing technologies and processes to reduce greenhouse-gas emissions, improve environmental quality and create jobs are likely to do rather well in a more crowded and congested future.
Whether these arguments are right or wrong, a public debate that ignores them cannot be productive. In my view, the policy positions resulting from such a truncated discussion are abdications of public responsibility on the part of our governments.
Robinson, director of the Sustainable Development Research Institute at the University of B.C., was principal lead author of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change .

Gas measure aids risk assessment System allows calculation of companies' contribution to climate change: [London edition]

Author: Boulton, Leyla

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/gas-measure-aids-risk-assessment-system-allows/docview/248548090/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: A tool has been developed in Britain to help investors assess companies' contribution to climate change and their resulting exposure to financial risk. A so-called global warming indicator enables companies to measure their output of carbon dioxide from fossil fuel consumption, whether in making and transporting products or heating their offices. Carbon dioxide is the most important greenhouse gas associated with global warming. The indicator's sponsors argue that companies least able to curb their emissions could incur financial penalties.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Gas+measure+aids+risk+assessment+System+allows+calculation+of+companies%27+contribution+to+climate+change%3A&amp;author=Boulton%2C+Leyla&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-17&amp;spage=08&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: A tool has been developed in Britain to help investors assess companies' contribution to climate change and their resulting exposure to financial risk.
A so-called global warming indicator enables companies to measure their output of carbon dioxide from fossil fuel consumption, whether in making and transporting products or heating their offices.
The measure has been produced by Imperial College and National Provident Institution , the insurance company. They hope it will become an internationally accepted reporting requirement in company accounts.
Carbon dioxide is the most important greenhouse gas associated with global warming. The indicator's sponsors argue that companies least able to curb their emissions could incur financial penalties.
"That companies should produce such an indicator is not simply a matter of corporate responsibility towards a chronic global issue, it is also a matter of providing shareholders with more adequate information about exposure to new costs," the proposal for the scheme says.
"Such costs, arising from taxes and changing market conditions, are likely to arise as counter measures to increased global warming take hold."
Developed countries meet in Kyoto, Japan, next month to negotiate their first legally binding reductions in greenhouse gas emissions. Many governments are expected to step up measures to discourage fossil fuel waste as well as to move away from carbon intensive fuels such as coal and promote renewable sources.
Some experts believe financial markets could become the most potent source of pressure for environmental improvements. Tessa Tennant, head of ethical investment at NPI, said the indicator would be particularly useful for differentiating between companies in the same sector.
Investors' emerging responsiveness to environmental considerations was illustrated on October 21 when shares in RJB Mining , the coal mining company, fell 23 per cent. Investors were reacting to a sell recommendation by Dresdner Kleinwort Benson , which said environmental measures would batter the coal industry in spite of hopes the Labour government would prop it up.
The indicator scheme is being circulated to UK accountants and companies for review.
It is the latest attempt to link companies' financial and environmental performance. Last year, Business in the Environment , a UK non-profit organisation, tried to rank Britain's 100 biggest companies according to their commitment to environmental management. Companies' subsequent efforts to improve their ranking in this year's exercise reveal just how much influence even voluntary benchmarking schemes wield.
Once the indicator has been reviewed by some of its potential users, it will be available for any business to try out, initially on a voluntary basis. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1997. All Rights Reserved.

AG LOBBYISTS HONE PRIORITIES FOR CONGRESS' SECOND HALF

Author: Lowell, Jessica

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/ag-lobbyists-hone-priorities-congress-second-half/docview/373586791/se-2?accountid=10226

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=AG+LOBBYISTS+HONE+PRIORITIES+FOR+CONGRESS%27+SECOND+HALF&amp;author=Lowell%2C+Jessica&amp;issn=1075783X&amp;title=Wyoming+Tribune+-+Eagle&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-16&amp;spage=A.3&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: CHEYENNE -- Agricultural lobbyists gearing up for the second half of the 105th Congress have found that a slate of conservative lawmakers didn't necessarily help their causes in the first half.
"We'll have to work harder. We'll have to work smarter," said Dennis Stolte, American Farm Bureau Federation senior director of Environment and Food Safety.
Part of that will be reselling the Farm Bureau's message to reach urbanites and suburbanites, using farmers and ranchers as spokespeople, Stolte said. Those occupations rank highly in believability and credibility, he said.
While agricultural interests did not win all battles it lined up for, Stolte said they did gain ground in agriculture tax issues, such as reducing estate taxes and achieving 100 percent deductions on health insurance for farmers and ranchers.
In the second half of the session, he said, lobbyists will continue to work in the tax area and will seek a good grazing bill.
Beyond that, Stolte offered up three predictions on the final day of the Wyoming Farm Bureau Federation's annual meeting on how the regulatory atmosphere is shaping up:
Environmental and food safety concerns will intensify the pressure for new practices.
The threat of e. coli, being passed through domestic beef and poultry and salmonella and hepatitis being passed through imported fruits and vegetables will likely drive regulatory practices inside farm gates.
Water quality regulations on wetlands, ground water and non- point source pollution will affect grazing practices. What's more, nutrient management, already a buzz word in the Great Lakes, Chesapeake Bay and along the upper Mississippi River valley, aims to control the overenrichment of watersheds.
Confined animal feeding operations likely will engender a big debate in agriculture circles.
Air quality issues will meet or exceed water quality regulations in their impact on farms and ranches.
Voluntary emission targets have become legally enforceable limits that apply to the 30 industrialized countries of the 160 that signed on to the global climate change treaty in Rio de Janeiro in 1992.
Agriculture ranks high on the list of activities that produce greenhouse gases.
Stolte said measures being contemplated to control their production could include emission controls on methane, controls on feed types, limits on production per acre of some crops and taxes on fossil fuels.

What will take fossil fuel's place?

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/what-will-take-fossil-fuels-place/docview/384767394/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: After all, the central target of the climate change treaty advocates is the carbons produced when relatively cheap fossil fuels are burned to produce energy. Restrict access to fossil fuels or raise their price and you make the alternatives more attractive. Then too, if dirty fossil fuels like tar sands and coal are to be the latest object of ecological concern, other previously demonized methods of power generation may find themselves rehabilitated. Step forward nuclear energy and hydroelectricity.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=What+will+take+fossil+fuel%27s+place%3F&amp;author=&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-15&amp;spage=D.6&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: What will take fossil fuel's place?
Saturday, November 15, 1997
UNINTENDED negative consequences of well-meaning policies are the stuff of legends. When a judge protected the forest habitat of the northern spotted owl, his aim was not to drive up the price of lumber on the Chicago Commodities Exchange. Attempts to force households to produce less garbage have frequently resulted in an increase in illegal and uncontrolled dumping.
An agreement in Kyoto on how to deal with fears of man-made climate change would be no different, but one hears very little serious discussion of the foreseeable consequences of a big Green win in Japan. Now might be a good time to start.
After all, the central target of the climate change treaty advocates is the carbons produced when relatively cheap fossil fuels are burned to produce energy. Restrict access to fossil fuels or raise their price and you make the alternatives more attractive. Then too, if dirty fossil fuels like tar sands and coal are to be the latest object of ecological concern, other previously demonized methods of power generation may find themselves rehabilitated. Step forward nuclear energy and hydroelectricity.
Of course unless Kyoto gets really out of hand, it will take quite a while for new nuclear power plants to become truly competitive on a level playing field. In this country we have subsidized the nuclear power industry and its exports, shielded it from its insurance liabilities, and failed to make adequate provisions for decommissioning and spent fuel disposal. Still, we have existing nuclear plants in three provinces (Ontario gets 60 per cent of its electricity from nuclear power). These represent largely sunk costs, and the unfunded decommissioning liabilities already exist.
Much of nuclear's bad reputation comes from incompetent planning that drove up some plants' construction costs, as well as appalling management and safety procedures that can be fixed by competition and a more effective regulatory and insurance regime. They are not inherent in nuclear power. As a power source that produces no greenhouse gas emissions to speak of, every tightening of the fossil fuel screw will make nuclear power from existing and possibly even new plants relatively more feasible.
A bigger winner, though, will be hydro power. It is easy to turn down hydro power contracts for ecological reasons, as New York did with Quebec several years ago, when demand is down and power is cheap and plentiful. Things may well be different with a stiff climate change treaty on the books. Quebec, Newfoundland, British Columbia and Manitoba are sitting on undeveloped hydro resources that, according to those provinces' utilities, can be brought on stream at low prices. And again, hydroelectricity is no culprit on the global warming front.
Of course the destruction of our quaint local electricity monopolies and the rapid emergence of competition and consumer choice will also affect this tableau. The monopolies have suppressed a great deal of generating potential. Industrial co-generation, small-scale hydro and alternative energy projects have all been frustrated. Still, a serious move toward reducing carbon emissions will force us to a more dispassionate analysis of nuclear and hydro power than we have been capable of in recent years.

WARMING COULD HIT STATE HARD PANEL OFFERS DIRE WARNING: [ALL EDITION]

Author: Mike Ivey The Capital Times

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/warming-could-hit-state-hard-panel-offers-dire/docview/395133681/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Dennis Corrigan, a former General Motors scientist now working for Ovonic Battery Co., touted recent advances in cleaner automobiles. He offered tours in a Geo Metro, powered by a new generation nickel metal-hydride battery. The car tops out at 70 mph and can travel over 240 miles on one charge. Investments in energy-efficient technologies -- such as natural gas-fired water heaters or high-efficiency air conditioning systems -- would result in immediate reductions in greenhouse gas emissions because of reduced energy use, the report says. About 33 percent of the carbon dioxide emissions in Wisconsin come from coal-fired power plants. Rich Rygh/The Capital Times Dennis Corrigan, a former General Motors scientist now working for Ovonic Battery Co., shows off a Geo Metro, powered by a new generation nickel metal-hydride battery, during a town meeting on global warming Wednesday at Monona Terrace. The car tops out at 70 mph and can travel over 240 miles on one charge. While the prototype cost $70,000, Corrigan said mass-produced versions could be competitive with gasoline-powered Geo Metros.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=WARMING+COULD+HIT+STATE+HARD+PANEL+OFFERS+DIRE+WARNING&amp;author=Mike+Ivey+The+Capital+Times&amp;issn=07494068&amp;title=Madison+Capital+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-13&amp;spage=1.C&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The impacts of global warming could be especially acute in Wisconsin, a panel of scientific and public policy experts has warned.
Wisconsin and the other Great Lakes states already experience extreme weather swings. But with average temperatures in the region predicted to increase from 2 to 4 degrees Fahrenheit by the year 2050, Wisconsin could be particularly vulnerable to increased droughts, floods or heat waves.
And the impact on the state economy may be severe, said John Foley, a professor of Atmospheric and Oceanic Sciences at the University of Wisconsin. He recounted how insurance companies nearly went bankrupt trying to pay out damages from the flood of 1993.
"These kinds of events cost us real money," said Foley during a town meeting on global climate change Wednesday at the Monona Terrace Convention Center.
The meeting, which drew an audience of about 125, coincided with next month's conference in Kyoto, Japan, where world leaders will discuss reducing carbon dioxide emissions. CO2 is a by-product of fossil fuel burning and the major contributor to the greenhouse effect, as gases gather in the Earth's upper atmosphere, trapping heat and warming the planet.
But while environmentalists and some scientists have pushed for immediate action to cut emissions, business interests have generally argued that trying to curb energy use will stifle economic growth.
Panel speakers, including former Gov. Tony Earl and Secretary of State Doug La Follette, tangled with the public policy and lifestyle questions of addressing global climate change.
In Madison, the predicted temperature increases will be enough to keep Lake Mendota from freezing over every year by 2050, said UW limnologist John Magnuson.
"We're already seeing lakes in Wisconsin thawing earlier each spring," said Magnuson.
These changes, Magnuson said, will have impacts on fish populations, with increases in warm water fish such as bass or carp and declines in cold water species such as trout.
The milder temperatures could also have dramatic impacts on the state economy. According to a recent EPA report, climate changes could result in a 55 percent decline in forested areas in northern Wisconsin. This would not only hurt the state timber industry, but would also have negative impacts on recreation and tourism.
Agreeing on solutions won't be easy, however, the panelists warned.
Earl, now a partner with Quarles & Brady law firm, observed that it has taken years just to get stakeholders to agree that global warming is occurring.
"If it's been difficult to agree on the science it's going to be far more difficult to agree what to do with policy," he said.
But Earl recounted how state utility companies had initially opposed mandatory reductions in sulfur emissions during the 1980s. Yet the program proved so successful it was eventually incorporated into the federal Clean Air Act.
Dennis Corrigan, a former General Motors scientist now working for Ovonic Battery Co., touted recent advances in cleaner automobiles. He offered tours in a Geo Metro, powered by a new generation nickel metal-hydride battery. The car tops out at 70 mph and can travel over 240 miles on one charge.
While the prototype cost $70,000, Corrigan said the prices will fall as the market increases. He also said improvements in solar and wind power will eventually make rechargeable electric cars truly energy efficient.
Not all of the panelists were so optimistic, however. La Follette said corporations with a stake in the status quo will fight policy changes at every level to protect their economic interests. He referred to efforts to dispute the theory of global warming, despite widespread agreement among scientists.
"The propaganda campaign is working," La Follette said. "The government continues to fiddle while Rome burns, with the fiddles bought and paid for by the people who started the fire."
But addressing global climate issues now could actually help the state's economy, according to a new report from the Consortium for Integrated Resource Planning at the UW.
The study says that an investment of $1.75 billion in more-efficient technologies between 1997 and 2010 would reduce energy and operating expenditures in Wisconsin by $4.44 billion, for a net savings of $2.7 billion.
Investments in energy-efficient technologies -- such as natural gas-fired water heaters or high-efficiency air conditioning systems -- would result in immediate reductions in greenhouse gas emissions because of reduced energy use, the report says. About 33 percent of the carbon dioxide emissions in Wisconsin come from coal-fired power plants.
Illustration Rich Rygh/The Capital Times Dennis Corrigan, a former General Motors scientist now working for Ovonic Battery Co., shows off a Geo Metro, powered by a new generation nickel metal-hydride battery, during a town meeting on global warming Wednesday at Monona Terrace. The car tops out at 70 mph and can travel over 240 miles on one charge. While the prototype cost $70,000, Corrigan said mass-produced versions could be competitive with gasoline-powered Geo Metros.

Kyoto's dark secret is taxes

Author: Corcoran, Terence

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/kyotos-dark-secret-is-taxes/docview/384828539/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: As if to fulfill that commitment, Mr. [Chr]étien renewed his promise not to impose a "carbon tax." That may keep Alberta and the oil industry happy, but it does nothing for the rest of the country. A carbon tax is a specific tax that would be applied on the production of oil, gas or other fossil fuel. If not a carbon tax, however, some other form of tax will have to be applied.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Kyoto%27s+dark+secret+is+taxes&amp;author=Corcoran%2C+Terence&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-12&amp;spage=B.2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Kyoto's dark secret is taxes
Wednesday, November 12, 1997
THE secret Chrétien mission to endow Canada with a climate change policy and $1-a-litre gasoline sets down in Regina today. There, Environment Minister Christine Stewart and Natural Resources Minister Ralph Goodale will formally attempt to get the provinces to buy into a half-baked Canadian program to control greenhouse gas emissions. How the provinces react to the plan will give Canadians the first real indicator of their country's position entering the global climate change conference in Kyoto Dec. 1.
The Kyoto conference, where Canada is preparing to sign an international agreement to cut energy use and reduce carbon emissions, is less than three weeks away, but still Ottawa has not released any policy or discussion papers. This is a government that will subject a proposal to allow banks to sell insurance policies through their branch networks to scores of task forces, committee reviews and other high-profile public ventilations. On climate change, however, the Chrétien team has yet to produce even a leaked one-page note on the climate change issue or on the economic costs associated with a global campaign to change the world's economy.
We may learn today whether provincial officials have received anything more concrete than the cryptic musings of the Prime Minister, much in the way Canadians learned about the Meech Lake accord. The news will get heaved out for public consumption at staged media briefings, and even then the full scope of the decisions will remain unclear.
In many respects, Canada has become a microcosm of the international negotiation system, a dark impenetrable process controlled by bureaucrats, politicians and assorted non-government organizations. They meet in secret, exchange views, conduct internal debates, grant approvals and reach agreements that have major, even catastrophic, implications -- all without providing the public with any meaningful information.
The Prime Minister calls this closed process of policy making one of "taking stock, consulting with partners across Canada: provincial governments, industrial sectors, NGOs." But why should this consulting and policy formation not be open to all Canadians? As it stands, Ottawa could sign a binding agreement in Kyoto, committing Canadians to massive energy cuts and higher energy taxes and costs, without a Commons debate or parliamentary approval.
In a speech last week, Mr. Chrétien made vague allusions to some of the "guiding principles" his government is using as it prepares for Kyoto. He talked about being "fair to all regions," which may be a coded commitment not to burden Alberta and Saskatchewan with a disproportionate share of the billions of dollars in costs and lost growth that are inevitable under any of the contemplated carbon control programs.
As if to fulfill that commitment, Mr. Chrétien renewed his promise not to impose a "carbon tax." That may keep Alberta and the oil industry happy, but it does nothing for the rest of the country. A carbon tax is a specific tax that would be applied on the production of oil, gas or other fossil fuel. If not a carbon tax, however, some other form of tax will have to be applied. Among the possibilities are new gasoline taxes collected nationally that would be designed to reduce consumption, or elaborate "emissions trading" mechanisms that would also in the final analysis be nothing more than major new taxes.
The auto industry favours new taxes on gasoline, environmentalists support new taxes, and many economists endorse new taxes if they are dolled up as "market mechanisms" such as tradable emissions permits. Market mechanisms, however, are nothing more than regulations and taxes disguised as "price" changes, supposedly to alter economic behavior by mimicking a free market. The result, more often than not, is revenues redistributed to government or somebody selected by government; in other words, a tax.
New taxes are the most logical outcome -- and may well be the real objective -- of any global agreement to control greenhouse gas emissions. Ottawa has claimed it will attempt to stick to a voluntary control program that will allow industry to meet emissions reduction targets on their own, without regulation or taxation -- an unlikely, if not impossible, scenario. If there's agreement in Kyoto, a Kyoto tax is sure to follow.
We may begin to learn more about Ottawa's plan to reduce emissions and raise taxes today, providing provincial environment ministers are willing to share some of the Chrétien government's secret agenda. On the other hand, we may all remain in the dark as Ottawa dispatches negotiators to Kyoto to sign an agreement no Canadian will have had a role in preparing.

War of the world Environment: The smug foot soldiers of the `carbon club' secured victory for big business in the opening battles of the climate talks. But Jeremy Leggett sees seeds of hope for a fightback

Author: Leggett, Jeremy

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/war-world-environment-smug-foot-soldiers-carbon/docview/244512126/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The ambassador from Samoa spoke from the heart at the close of the latest session of the climate talks in Bonn last week. `Let not the many be sacrificed for the few,' he said. `There would be moral repugnance in that.' But, as usual, such anguished pleas by developing nations for action on global warming were elbowed out by the oil, coal, auto and utility industries. More than 80 of their lobbyists patrolled the corridors, seeking to sabotage the latest attempt to give teeth to the Convention on Climate Change. Watching the carbon club at work, it seemed to me that an entirely new form of conflict has broken out in the world. It has all the deeply-felt divisions of civil war: governments are painfully divided among themselves; business groupings are polarised; some industrial sectors, notably the hydrocarbon sector, are divisively split. But this new form of conflict is also manifestly global. Whole blocs, regions and economy types line up against each other. European Union progressives lock horns with traditional allies in North America and Australasia. Within the G77, the developing world bloc, Opec lines up against fellow members without oil. The Alliance of Small Island States points accusingly at the industrialised world, with its profligate greenhouse gas emissions, and talks of cultural genocide. The diplomatic rituals veil the strongest of feelings. In Bonn, the Americans petulantly walked out of a meeting with the developing countries, igniting fury in their trail.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=War+of+the+world+Environment%3A+The+smug+foot+soldiers+of+the+%60carbon+club%27+secured+victory+for+big+business+in+the+opening+battles+of+the+climate+talks.+But+Jeremy+Leggett+sees+seeds+of+hope+for+a+fightback&amp;author=Leggett%2C+Jeremy&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-12&amp;spage=004&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The ambassador from Samoa spoke from the heart at the close of the latest session of the climate talks in Bonn last week. `Let not the many be sacrificed for the few,' he said. `There would be moral repugnance in that.' But, as usual, such anguished pleas by developing nations for action on global warming were elbowed out by the oil, coal, auto and utility industries. More than 80 of their lobbyists patrolled the corridors, seeking to sabotage the latest attempt to give teeth to the Convention on Climate Change.
Watching the carbon club at work, it seemed to me that an entirely new form of conflict has broken out in the world. It has all the deeply-felt divisions of civil war: governments are painfully divided among themselves; business groupings are polarised; some industrial sectors, notably the hydrocarbon sector, are divisively split. But this new form of conflict is also manifestly global.
Whole blocs, regions and economy types line up against each other. European Union progressives lock horns with traditional allies in North America and Australasia. Within the G77, the developing world bloc, Opec lines up against fellow members without oil. The Alliance of Small Island States points accusingly at the industrialised world, with its profligate greenhouse gas emissions, and talks of cultural genocide. The diplomatic rituals veil the strongest of feelings. In Bonn, the Americans petulantly walked out of a meeting with the developing countries, igniting fury in their trail.
Adding to the complexity of this drama - soon to reach a vital and perhaps final act at the Kyoto Climate Summit in December - players can, and do, switch sides. Australia once stood in the trenches with the progressives; now its diplomats work with the most merciless of the carbon club's snipers. America's President once gave an Earth Day call to arms; now he allows the carbon club to dictate terms for a retreat.
But through this multi-dimensional battleground, complex as it is geopolitically, two clear sides stand out: those who think global warming is a dire problem that humankind can no longer delay dealing with, and those who don't - or, for some unfathomable reason, don't seem to care.
Should we call this civil/global conflict the carbon war? Would that be an overstatement? After all, though we trust that no actual shots will be fired, the UN tells us the casualties will be measured in hundreds of millions in the decades to come if greenhouse gas emissions are not cut significantly. Indeed, if the action advocates - our Prime Minister among them - are right, the fate of the planet, and a liveable future on it, are ultimately at issue. It is as simple as that. And if there is not yet the whiff of cordite, then the first vapour trails of hatred are in the air at the climate talks .
Like many a war, there can be no winners if hostilities persist. The carbon club members maintain that they are defending business and nation. But, according to the UN's best estimates, the angry tide of climate change's impact catalogue will in time wash over economies, grabbing territory and laying waste with the ferocity of the most efficient invading army. Who can do good business - much less run a stable economy - in a world with its thermostat awry? Take just one of the insurance industry's recent warnings. Munich Re, the largest reinsurer in the world, observes in its latest report that `the possible extent of losses caused by extreme natural catastrophes in one of the world's major metropolises or industrial centres could be so great as to cause the collapse of entire countries' economic systems and could even bring about the collapse of the world's financial markets'.
Of course, the action deniers call such talk - and, indeed, global warming itself - hot air. To them, `caution' means not a global effort to cut greenhouse gas emissions, but business as usual. They are surprisingly few in number, relative to the action advocates, and their state of innocent or wilful denial is ever more transparent. But they are winning - and most painfully so, at the moment. The smugness of their foot soldiers as they went about their work last week in Bonn was palpable. After the negotiations finally ground to stalemated conclusion, the chief lobbyists for oil and coal could be seen outside the negotiating chamber pumping hands enthusiastically with the Opec diplomats who do the lion's share of their dirty work on the inside.
Is all lost? Will governments now inevitably fail to secure the `legally-binding targets and timetables for significant emissions reductions' which most of them have promised to negotiate by the time of Kyoto? Seeds of hope still drift in the wind. BP has broken ranks with the carbon club, back-tracking on their worst disinformation and announcing that solar energy has a big future in the fight against global warming. The company's head of environmental affairs was a lonely man among his oil industry colleagues last week - but not totally so. Enron, America's biggest gas company, and Daimler Benz no less, addressed a briefing entitled `Yes to Kyoto'.
They don't see problems with emissions reductions, just new business opportunities. And whereas 130 American companies put out a joint newspaper advert in the US a few weeks ago, exhorting President Clinton to do nothing, this week 13 companies took the opposite step, among them Turner Broadcasting, Mitsubishi America, Bechtel, and Nike.
The world may yet down carbon arms and pull something out of the fire. But President Clinton will need to find a spine, and Europe's leaders will need to keep their nerve, trusting Daimler Benz and BP's emerging greenhouse views rather than those of General Motors, Exxon, and all the rest.
Dr Jeremy Leggett, Charterhouse Fellow in Solar Energy at Oxford University and chief executive of The Solar Century, is chairman of the Government's Industry Solar Task Force.

Why we should care

Author: Obee, Dave

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/why-we-should-care/docview/345665211/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: What are greenhouse gases? Greenhouse gases, including water vapor, carbon dioxide and methane, occur naturally in the atmosphere. Providing an insulating layer, they trap heat energy on earth making it 33 degrees warmer than it would otherwise be. But industrial and other human activities have increased the natural level of greenhouse gases, raising the likelihood of climate change and global warming. What's the major r cause of greenhouse gases? The B.C. Environment Ministry says carbon dioxide accounts for 83per cent of B.C.'s greenhouse emissions, created mostly by the burning of fossil fuels. Transportation is the largest single source of CO2.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Why+we+should+care&amp;author=Obee%2C+Dave&amp;issn=&amp;title=Times+-+Colonist&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-11&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: What are greenhouse gases? Greenhouse gases, including water vapor, carbon dioxide and methane, occur naturally in the atmosphere. Providing an insulating layer, they trap heat energy on earth making it 33 degrees warmer than it would otherwise be. But industrial and other human activities have increased the natural level of greenhouse gases, raising the likelihood of climate change and global warming. What's the major r cause of greenhouse gases? The B.C. Environment Ministry says carbon dioxide accounts for 83per cent of B.C.'s greenhouse emissions, created mostly by the burning of fossil fuels. Transportation is the largest single source of CO2.
Forestry accounts for more than a quarter of the province's greenhouse gases, according to Northwest Environment Watch. This is due to burning and decay of debris left in the forest after it has been logged.
The level of greenhouse gases from human activity is strongly influenced by energy prices and economic activity, the Environment Ministry says. Emissions actually dropped in the early 1980s as a result of relatively high energy prices. Ironically cold weather, like the winter of 1989, can raise emissions along with heating bills.
Why should we care about climate change?
Environment Canada says northern countries like Canada will experience greater warming than those near the equator over the next century.
Scientists internationally have identified a number of potential impacts, the federal department says. These include: reduced fish stocks due to changes in ocean temperatures; rising sea levels causing flooding and erosion in B.C., Nova Scotia and Prince Edward Island; an increase in forest fires; regional water shortages; melting permafrost in the Arctic; spreading of malaria and other tropical diseases; an increase in the frequency and intensity of "extreme weather events" ranging from heat waves to tornadoes.
The insurance industry has concluded that human activity is already influencing climate change on a global scale, leading to a recent spate of unusual weather patterns.
