The Sky Isn't Falling, and the Ocean Isn't Rising

Author: By S. Fred Singer

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/sky-isnt-falling-ocean-rising/docview/308253162/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Global warming devotees have been making alarmist predictions about the rising sea levels they think will follow an increase in the Earth's average temperatures. The horror stories include the flooding of low-lying coastal areas, the disappearance of island nations, the inundation of America by environmental refugees, and an exponential explosion in insurance claims. Activists apparently don't realize that the much-ballyhooed climate models that predict global warming cannot make any quantitative predictions at all about sea levels. Nevertheless, climate specialists have constructed a corrected "global" sea-level record; it shows that sea levels have been rising at the rate of about seven inches per century. Is this rise connected to climate? Not likely. The best estimates based on geologic data indicate that this has been going on for several centuries -- a period in which the global climate has fluctuated significantly. Rather, the current thinking is that the steady rise in sea levels is connected to slow tectonic changes in the shape of the ocean basin -- which human beings, of course, can do nothing about. Calculations of any climate effect on sea levels must take account of this more or less steady rise.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+Sky+Isn%27t+Falling%2C+and+the+Ocean+Isn%27t+Rising&amp;author=By+S.+Fred+Singer&amp;issn=09219986&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-11&amp;spage=10&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Global warming devotees have been making alarmist predictions about the rising sea levels they think will follow an increase in the Earth's average temperatures. The horror stories include the flooding of low-lying coastal areas, the disappearance of island nations, the inundation of America by environmental refugees, and an exponential explosion in insurance claims. Activists apparently don't realize that the much-ballyhooed climate models that predict global warming cannot make any quantitative predictions at all about sea levels.
They also don't realize that informed speculation about rising sea levels has been steadily falling. Initial estimates by the Environmental Protection Agency projected that a doubling of atmospheric carbon dioxide would cause sea levels to rise by between 80 and 120 inches. By 1990 these estimates had been reduced by 75%. In 1996 a United Nations science advisory panel predicted a rise of only 15 to 22 inches by 2100 -- still based on shaky assumptions.
Even these much smaller estimates are cause for skepticism, because changes in sea level are notoriously difficult to measure, and reliable information is hard to come by. All historical data are based on tide gauges, mainly on the shorelines of Northern Europe and North America. Long-term sea level trends must be extracted after adjusting for waves, storm surges and tidal variations. Scientists must also contend with the fact that the land surface may be rising or falling. Some land surfaces, in Scandinavia for example, are rebounding after being compressed by the weight of glaciers. Other land areas are subsiding, as petroleum or ground water is pumped out.
Nevertheless, climate specialists have constructed a corrected "global" sea-level record; it shows that sea levels have been rising at the rate of about seven inches per century. Is this rise connected to climate? Not likely. The best estimates based on geologic data indicate that this has been going on for several centuries -- a period in which the global climate has fluctuated significantly. Rather, the current thinking is that the steady rise in sea levels is connected to slow tectonic changes in the shape of the ocean basin -- which human beings, of course, can do nothing about. Calculations of any climate effect on sea levels must take account of this more or less steady rise.
The next question is how global warming, if it occurs, would affect sea levels. On the one hand, a warmer climate would melt mountain glaciers and cause a thermal expansion of ocean water, accelerating sea levels' rise. But on the other hand, more water would evaporate from the surface of warmer oceans, leading to more rainfall, and -- over Greenland and the Antarctic -- to greater accumulation of snow and ice. This process essentially thickens the polar ice caps, thus lowering sea levels.
The problem has been to find whether the sea levels' rising or lowering would have a greater impact. This is a question that cannot be answered by theory, or by computer models. To produce an accurate conclusion, we have to examine data. Fortunately, we do have data from a relatively rapid global warming that occurred between about 1900 and 1940, as the climate recovered from a cool period called the Little Ice Age. Neither the Little Ice Age nor the warming that followed are ascribed to human influences; many scientists believe they were caused by subtle changes in the sun's radiation.
In any case, data from the warming of 1900-1940 shows a drop in sea levels, while the subsequent cooler period shows a sea-level rise. This effect is even more pronounced in comparisons of sea-level changes with sea-surface temperatures in the tropics, where most of the oceans' evaporation occurs.
These findings support the hypothesis that ice accumulation in the polar regions may have a greater impact on sea levels than do the melting of glaciers and the thermal expansion of ocean water. Support for this view also comes from concurrent but as yet incomplete measurements of ice accumulation at certain locations in Greenland and the Antarctic.
Contrary to activists' claims, what's clear is that global warming -- if it takes place -- would slow any rise in sea levels. Although more study is needed, this striking result should at least give pause to the Association of Small Island States and other groups that are pressing industrialized nations to adopt drastic reductions in fossil fuel use in order to avoid the global warming "catastrophe" so dear to the environmentalist lobby.
---
Mr. Singer, professor emeritus of environmental sciences at the University of Virginia and president of the Science & Environmental Policy Project in Fairfax, Va., is author of "Hot Talk, Cold Science: Global Warming's Unfinished Debate" (Independent Institute, 1997).

NEWS LITE WHITE HOUSE SCREENING EXPLORES AMERICA'S PAST: [VALLEY EDITION]

Abstract: In the very room where Meriwether Lewis and Thomas Jefferson plotted the cross-country search for a Northwest Passage, President Clinton played host Monday night at a White House reception and viewing of Ken Burns' latest documentary, "Lewis & Clark: The Journey of the Corps of Discovery." Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=NEWS+LITE+WHITE+HOUSE+SCREENING+EXPLORES+AMERICA%27S+PAST&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Daily+News&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-11&amp;spage=N.2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: WASHINGTON -- In the very room where Meriwether Lewis and Thomas Jefferson plotted the cross-country search for a Northwest Passage, President Clinton played host Monday night at a White House reception and viewing of Ken Burns' latest documentary, "Lewis & Clark: The Journey of the Corps of Discovery."
Notable guests at the White House reception were actors Matthew Broderick and Adam Arkin, whose voices were featured in the public television production, along with Burns, Sen. Charles Robb, D-Va.; actress Sarah Jessica Parker and historian Stephen Ambrose.
Clinton called the members of the 1804-1806 Lewis and Clark expedition the forebears of those who created the international space station and scientists studying the mysteries of human genetics and researching global climate change.
The president also noted the approaching millennium, saying the United States will welcome the next century "by highlighting American creativity and innovation and our insatiable desire to explore, as we are doing here tonight."
On a day when the shelving of the "fast track" trade bill weighed heavy on his administration, Clinton paid special homage to the cooperation Burns' film portrayed.
"In this town especially, we could all stand to remember that we could all be a little more noble, a little more full of energy, a little more charitable, a little more humble," he said.
The film, shown in a condensed version at the White House event, spanned the Lewis and Clark expedition's nearly 3-year voyage up the Missouri River and across the Rocky Mountains to the Pacific Ocean, through territory that was previously uncharted.
Ex-actress's life not so wonderful
Karolyn Grimes has had a fairly miserable life since playing Jimmy Stewart's daughter in "It's a Wonderful Life," wherein she delivered the sweet sentiment: "Every time a bell rings, an angel gets his wings."
At a weekend appearance in Omaha, Neb., she said her mom died when she was 14, her dad was killed a year later in a car crash, her first husband died in a hunting accident and her second of cancer.
Now living in Kansas, Grimes, 57, added: "In Hollywood, if they can't use you . . . they don't even know you. You've been this little prince or princess all these years, and suddenly you're nothing." But, she noted, she still believes in angels because "I was lifted from the Hollywood scene."
A story Coppola couldn't refuse
A little more than two years ago, Francis Ford Coppola walked into an airport newsstand to find some books to read on a flight to Paris. "I had never read a John Grisham book, and when I saw `The Rainmaker' on the stand, which was the No. 1 best seller, I decided to buy it," Coppola said.
His intention was to take notes as he read the novel, "to learn, to see what appealed to a modern audience." Instead, he became entranced with the book, about a young, idealistic lawyer pitted against an evil insurance company that fails to pay up on the policies it writes to the poor.
"I became a sucker for Grisham just like everyone else," Coppola said. "I couldn't put it down." What fascinated him, he explained, was that the book dealt with a world that rarely had been touched by Hollywood - the seedy side of the law business and corruption in the insurance industry. Coppola also found the young lawyer in the book an almost perfect hero. "He was like a chivalrous knight, sort of pure, helping damsels in distress," he said.
By the time he landed in Paris, Coppola had made up his mind to adapt the book and direct the film. He saw the story as vaguely similar to "The Godfather." "It had the same evocative nature of getting into the story and settings, it was popular fiction and, in both books, there was this underlying sense that if an injustice is done to you, there's somebody you can go to."
The film, opening Nov. 21, has a cast that includes Matt DAamon as the young lawyer, Claire Danes and Jon Voight.
Food book editor digests misspelling
Senior editor Maria Guarnaschelli, who took 3-1/2 years to get the revision of "Joy of Cooking" right, misspelled a contributor's name two of the three times she's mentioned.
"She's my dearest friend," the editor said of Lynne Rossetto Kasper, whose book, "The Splendid Table," Guarnaschelli edited. "I'm going to call her myself (to apologize). But why does that matter at this point?" Bake her an upside-down cake
Former governor pens political tale
Former Massachusetts Gov. William Weld might have a new career outside politics.
Weld has written a political thriller set in Boston and has signed a contract with Simon & Schuster, a source told The Boston Globe.
Weld's yarn, reportedly titled "Mackerel by Moonlight," centers on a candidate for district attorney in Boston.
Weld, 52, began the novel in January. He completed the book in March and sold it in August, shortly after resigning as governor.
Weld joins several other political figures who have written suspense novels, including former Sen. Gary Hart and Defense Secretary William Cohen. News Lite is compiled by Karen Duffy from Daily News staff and wire reports

Greenhouse gas reduction justified; Scientists support international treaty

Author: Duffy, Andrew; Southam Newspapers

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/greenhouse-gas-reduction-justified-scientists/docview/252530402/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: [...] the provocative question is not whether global warming exists, but whether enough is known about the phenomenon to take action to stem greenhouse emissions. Environmental scientists have made predictions about the future effects of global warming based on supercomputer models of the Earth's climate.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Greenhouse+gas+reduction+justified%3B+Scientists+support+international+treaty&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew%3BSoutham+Newspapers&amp;issn=0839296X&amp;title=Edmonton+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-11&amp;spage=A.12&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Senior Environment Canada scientist Gordon McBean, one of the nation's leading experts on global warming, readily concedes what he doesn't know about the phenomenon.
In an appearance before one of three parliamentary committees now studying climate change, McBean recently said scientists can provide politicians only with the facts as they know them.
Scientists cannot predict how much the planet will warm over the coming century or what will happen to regional ecosystems. The Earth's climate is simply too complex.
But what is known, he said, is this: "The scientific community is very confident the increasing greenhouse gas concentrations do exist and we're very confident it's the result of human activities."
McBean was a member of the United Nations panel of 2,000 climate scientists who confirmed the same, seemingly modest facts, in 1995. "The balance of evidence suggests there is a discernible human influence on global climate," concluded the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change.
Most Canadians are coming to understand the significance of that finding, which has led the world to the brink of a new global-warming treaty.
Canada will join 165 nations in Kyoto, Japan, next month to finalize a treaty that sets binding emission targets for carbon dioxide, nitrous oxide and methane. The gases are believed to be the main culprits in a 0.5 C warming of the planet over the past 100 years.
The treaty holds important implications for the Canadian economy since it will force the country to reduce its rate of energy consumption, now among the highest in the world.
Achieving that goal could raise the price of gasoline, throw many in the coal industry out of work and consume some part of Finance Minister Paul Martin's long-awaited fiscal dividend.
The oil, coal and auto industries -- heavy energy consumers -- have led an aggressive challenge to the IPCC findings. Their chief strategy has been to highlight dissenting voices in the scientific community.
Most environmental scientists, however, are convinced that global warming is a real and present danger. For evidence, they point to a pronounced warming trend over the past 40 years and the fact that the 10 warmest years since 1861 (when detailed record-keeping began) have occurred since 1980. The warmest year on record is 1995.
Even some industry leaders say prudence alone should make world leaders address the potential of global warming.
The Canadian Association of Petroleum Producers argues that, while the science is not rock solid, "taking steps that make sense now is a lot like buying insurance."
Meanwhile, John Browne, chair of British Petroleum, has said it would be "unwise and potentially dangerous" to ignore the threat. The time to act, he said, "is not when the link between greenhouse gases and climate change is conclusively proven, but when the possibility cannot be discounted."
Prime Minister Jean Chretien has poured scorn on those who dismiss the science of climate change, comparing them to tobacco officials who produced scientists to refute the link between smoking and lung cancer.
"Those arguments are not credible and they're not not responsible," he told an Ottawa audience.
Moreover, the provocative question is not whether global warming exists, but whether enough is known about the phenomenon to take action to stem greenhouse emissions. Is it necessary to make fundamental changes now or can they safely be delayed until climate models are more finely tuned?
Environmental scientists have made predictions about the future effects of global warming based on supercomputer models of the Earth's climate. Those models suggest that as carbon dioxide concentrations in the atmosphere increase, global temperatures rise. The models do not agree about how much the Earth will warm. Predictions range from 1.5 to 4.5 C by 2100.
Although that increase may not seem dramatic, a temperature drop of 5 C plunged the world into an Ice Age.
The most likely scenario, according to the UN panel, is that the Earth will warm 2.5 C by 2100, bringing the planet into a temperature range not seen for millions of years. Computer models suggest that kind of warming will be accompanied by a series of dangerous climactic events: floods, droughts, rising seas and violent storms.
Indeed, when world leaders convene in Kyoto Dec. 1, they'll be coming up with a risk management strategy for the planet.
So far, there's little political agreement about what kind of danger the world faces.
The Europeans, for example, believe the situation is dire. They've proposed reduction targets -- 15 per cent over 1990 levels by the year 2010 -- that reflect their concern.
Canada and the United States are among the nations who have adopted a go-slow approach. They want to curb emissions without damaging their economies and have proposed that greenhouse gas levels be stabilized by 2010 to 2012. For Canada, that would represent a 13-per-cent reduction from current levels because the country's emissions have increased substantially since 1990.
Computer climate models now simulate what the world will be like with more carbon dioxide in the atmosphere. The models suggest that surface temperatures will rise and increase evaporation from oceans, lakes and rivers. Since warm air can hold more moisture, it will make more water available to developing storms. It explains why the world will see drought in some areas and more violent, heavy storms in others.
The same models predict other potential dangers. Oceans are expected to rise as water molecules warm and expand and as polar ice melts. Coastal areas could be inundated, low- lying islands swamped.
Climactic zones could shift north with rising temperatures and there's a chance the ecosystems will move faster than animals and plants are able to adjust. Agriculture could be devastated, particularly in the world's hottest and poorest countries, while growing conditions may well improve in such places as Canada.
Climate scientists are growing increasingly confident in their models as their preliminary forecasts prove correct.
Still, scientists such as McBean concede more work needs to be done to fill in the knowledge gaps: how clouds affect warming levels, how oceans redistribute heat, how melting ice-cover will affect the surface temperature of the Earth.
"There is a possibility that there will be no climate change," he says.

The Sky Isn't Falling, and the Ocean Isn't Rising

Author: By S. Fred Singer

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/sky-isnt-falling-ocean-rising/docview/398589254/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Global-warming devotees have been making alarmist predictions about the rising sea levels they think will follow an increase in the earth's average temperatures. The horror stories include the flooding of low-lying coastal areas, the disappearance of island nations, the inundation of America by environmental refugees, and an exponential explosion in insurance claims. Activists apparently don't realize that the much-ballyhooed climate models that predict global warming cannot make any quantitative predictions at all about sea levels. Nevertheless, climate specialists have constructed a corrected "global" sea-level record; it shows that sea levels have been rising at the rate of about seven inches per century. Is this rise connected to climate? Not likely. The best estimates based on geologic data indicate that this has been going on for several centuries -- a period in which the global climate has fluctuated significantly. Rather, the current thinking is that the steady rise in sea levels is connected to slow tectonic changes in the shape of the ocean basin -- which human beings, of course, can do nothing about. Calculations of any climate effect on sea levels must take account of this more or less steady rise.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+Sky+Isn%27t+Falling%2C+and+the+Ocean+Isn%27t+Rising&amp;author=By+S.+Fred+Singer&amp;issn=00999660&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-10&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Global-warming devotees have been making alarmist predictions about the rising sea levels they think will follow an increase in the earth's average temperatures. The horror stories include the flooding of low-lying coastal areas, the disappearance of island nations, the inundation of America by environmental refugees, and an exponential explosion in insurance claims. Activists apparently don't realize that the much-ballyhooed climate models that predict global warming cannot make any quantitative predictions at all about sea levels.
They also don't realize that informed speculation about rising sea levels has been steadily falling. Initial estimates by the Environmental Protection Agency projected that a doubling of atmospheric carbon dioxide would cause sea levels to rise by between 80 and 120 inches. By 1990 these estimates had been reduced by 75%. In 1996 a United Nations science advisory panel predicted a rise of only 15 to 22 inches by 2100 -- still based on shaky assumptions.
Even these much smaller estimates are cause for skepticism, because changes in sea level are notoriously difficult to measure, and reliable information is hard to come by. All historical data are based on tide gauges, mainly on the shorelines of Northern Europe and North America. Long-term sea level trends must be extracted after adjusting for waves, storm surges and tidal variations. Scientists must also contend with the fact that the land surface may be rising or falling. Some land surfaces, in Scandinavia for example, are rebounding after being compressed by the weight of glaciers. Other land areas are subsiding, as petroleum or ground water is pumped out.
Nevertheless, climate specialists have constructed a corrected "global" sea-level record; it shows that sea levels have been rising at the rate of about seven inches per century. Is this rise connected to climate? Not likely. The best estimates based on geologic data indicate that this has been going on for several centuries -- a period in which the global climate has fluctuated significantly. Rather, the current thinking is that the steady rise in sea levels is connected to slow tectonic changes in the shape of the ocean basin -- which human beings, of course, can do nothing about. Calculations of any climate effect on sea levels must take account of this more or less steady rise.
The next question is how global warming, if it occurs, would affect sea levels. On the one hand, a warmer climate would melt mountain glaciers and cause a thermal expansion of ocean water, accelerating sea levels' rise. But on the other hand, more water would evaporate from the surface of warmer oceans, leading to more rainfall, and -- over Greenland and the Antarctic -- to greater accumulation of snow and ice. This process essentially thickens the polar ice caps, thus lowering sea levels.
The problem has been to find whether the sea levels' rising or lowering would have a greater impact. This is a question that cannot be answered by theory, or by computer models. To produce an accurate conclusion, we have to examine data. Fortunately, we do have data from a relatively rapid global warming that occurred between about 1900 and 1940, as the climate recovered from a cool period called the Little Ice Age. Neither the Little Ice Age nor the warming that followed are ascribed to human influences; many scientists believe they were caused by subtle changes in the sun's radiation.
In any case, data from the warming of 1900-1940 show a drop in sea levels, while the subsequent cooler period shows a sea-level rise. This effect is even more pronounced in comparisons of sea-level changes with sea-surface temperatures in the tropics, where most of the oceans' evaporation occurs.
These findings support the hypothesis that ice accumulation in the polar regions may have a greater impact on sea levels than do the melting of glaciers and the thermal expansion of ocean water. Support for this view also comes from concurrent but as yet incomplete measurements of ice accumulation at certain locations in Greenland and the Antarctic.
Contrary to activists' claims, what's clear is that global warming -- if it takes place -- would slow any rise in sea levels. Although more study is needed, this striking result should at least give pause to the Association of Small Island States and other groups that are pressing industrialized nations to adopt drastic reductions in fossil fuel use in order to avoid the global warming "catastrophe" so dear to the environmentalist lobby.
---
Mr. Singer, professor emeritus of environmental sciences at the University of Virginia and president of the Science & Environmental Policy Project in Fairfax, Va., is author of "Hot Talk, Cold Science: Global Warming's Unfinished Debate" (Independent Institute, 1997).
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: How Green Was Their Greenland" -- WSJ Nov. 26, 1997)

South America, Africa trade spotlighted

Author: Matthew C. Quinn STAFF WRITER

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/south-america-africa-trade-spotlighted/docview/413746131/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: A pair of international trade conferences drew intense interest Thursday in Atlanta, the eve of a House vote on President Clinton's request for powers that would speed new free trade agreements. Two protesters disrupted one of the conferences by unfurling a banner and shouting their opposition to the "fast track" negotiating proposal. More than 500 people attended a conference on infrastructure opportunities in South America held at the Westin Peachtree Plaza Hotel. The conference was sponsored by the U.S. Trade and Development Agency to help U.S. companies assess investment opportunities in the hemisphere. The attendance was twice what organizers expected. Businesses from about 25 states were represented.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=South+America%2C+Africa+trade+spotlighted&amp;author=Matthew+C.+Quinn+STAFF+WRITER&amp;issn=24731609&amp;title=The+Atlanta+Constitution&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-07&amp;spage=G.02&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   This article appeared in both The Atlanta Journal and The Atlanta Constitution.
A pair of international trade conferences drew intense interest Thursday in Atlanta, the eve of a House vote on President Clinton's request for powers that would speed new free trade agreements.
Two protesters disrupted one of the conferences by unfurling a banner and shouting their opposition to the "fast track" negotiating proposal.
More than 500 people attended a conference on infrastructure opportunities in South America held at the Westin Peachtree Plaza Hotel. The conference was sponsored by the U.S. Trade and Development Agency to help U.S. companies assess investment opportunities in the hemisphere. The attendance was twice what organizers expected. Businesses from about 25 states were represented.
"People are figuring out that if you want to grow your business and you don't look overseas, that you're not going to grow your business," said Ned Cabot, a spokesman for the federal agency.
The two protesters were escorted peacefully out of the Westin by security personnel after unfurling from a balcony a banner that said "Don't Fast Track the Destruction of the Amazon and Our Climate."
Atossa Soltani said she and another woman represent a group called Amazon Watch and had traveled from Los Angeles to register their views. She said the 125 major projects worth an estimated $200 billion being discussed at the three-day conference that wraps up today would "exacerbate climate change."
A separate conference intended to foster ties between Georgia businesses and Africa was held simultaneously at the Sheraton Four Points Hotel. That conference was organized by Rep. Cynthia McKinney (D-Ga.) and a Washington-based nonprofit group, Corporate Council on Africa.
"It is clear that with the convening of two major conferences on international business taking place here today that Atlanta is truly a gateway for America's international commerce," said George Munos, president of the Overseas Private Investment Corp., who addressed both groups.
Munos, whose federal agency offers "risk insurance" to U.S. firms that invest in emerging economies, spoke up for the fast track proposal that would be used to negotiate new free trade agreements in the hemisphere. "This is a very important moment in time," he said.
The Africa conference drew about 200 businesspeople, most of them from McKinney's 4th Congressional District. "Africa is hot right now," the lawmaker said. "The new democracies of the continent need to ride this curve for as long as possible. It also means profits over here, so it's a win-win situation for all concerned."
Munos also announced plans for a new $150 million U.S. fund that will make investments in African companies.
At a separate luncheon held at the law offices of King & Spalding, Diego Ramiro Guelar, the Argentine ambassador to the United States, called the legislation essential to negotiating a Free Trade of the Americas Agreement that would cover the entire hemisphere.
"If there is no fast track, this negotiation is unfortunately finished before it begins," he said.

Subject: International trade; Conferences

Location: Atlanta Georgia; South America; Africa

Title: South America, Africa trade spotlighted

Author: Matthew C. Quinn STAFF WRITER

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/south-america-africa-trade-spotlighted/docview/412680255/se-2?accountid=10226

Chretien rules out carbon tax: Confirms Liberal Party's stand on greenhouse gases: [Final Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/chretien-rules-out-carbon-tax-confirms-liberal/docview/269904088/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Prime Minister Jean Chretien says there'll be no carbon tax imposed in Canada to combat greenhouse gas emissions, confirming what other cabinet ministers have been guardedly saying for weeks. Since returning from a meeting of the Commonwealth last week, Chretien has been making commitments to place tighter controls on carbon dioxide emissions, the most important of the greenhouse gases. The emissions are created when fossil fuels are burned. Chretien also echoed what many environmentalists have said to deflect criticism of greenhouse gas controls -- that a reduction might actually stimulate the economy in other areas.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Chretien+rules+out+carbon+tax%3A+Confirms+Liberal+Party%27s+stand+on+greenhouse+gases&amp;author=&amp;issn=11899417&amp;title=The+Spectator&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-04&amp;spage=D.3&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Prime Minister Jean Chretien says there'll be no carbon tax imposed in Canada to combat greenhouse gas emissions, confirming what other cabinet ministers have been guardedly saying for weeks.
Chretien told a $325-a-plate Liberal fundraiser last night: "Let our opponents talk about taxes and other horror stories. But I have news for them, Halloween is over.
"We have already said that there will be no carbon tax, and I repeat it tonight.
"Let's talk about what we can do to pass on a healthy planet to our children and grandchildren."
Chretien's speech at the annual Maple Leaf dinner was as green as it gets, as he advocated a strong commitment to the environment at the pivotal climate conference to be held one month from now in Kyoto, Japan.
But he hinted that the government may decide to stagger a reduction of carbon dioxide emissions over a longer time frame. The United States has pledged it will stabilize its emissions at 1990 levels by 2008 to 2012.
MAKING COMMITMENTS
"We are not interested in penalizing or singling out one region or one industry, and that has never been the approach of this government," Chretien said.
"We want to have reasonable targets step by step. I know it will lead some to criticize us for being too soft, but it's what critics said about our deficit targets and we don't hear them say that anymore."
Since returning from a meeting of the Commonwealth last week, Chretien has been making commitments to place tighter controls on carbon dioxide emissions, the most important of the greenhouse gases. The emissions are created when fossil fuels are burned.
Both Energy Minister Ralph Goodale and his predecessor Anne McClellan, now justice minister, have already said a carbon tax is not an option for the Canadian government but until now Chretien has not said the same.
The Reform party has been pressing the Liberal government on the prospect of higher energy taxes, which Reform says will harm the oil industry and throw the economy into a tailspin.
Chretien didn't hesitate to take shots at the opposition.
"Some people refuse to accept the scientific evidence of global warming; they say the scientific proof is not conclusive," he said. "Do you remember how the tobacco companies claimed there was no proof that smoking caused lung cancer?"
Chretien also echoed what many environmentalists have said to deflect criticism of greenhouse gas controls -- that a reduction might actually stimulate the economy in other areas.
"We have the occasion to change this global consensus into a golden opportunity, that far from costing jobs in Canada will create more and more of them," he said.
"Look at companies like Ballard in Vancouver. They are the world leading edge company in electric buses and automobiles . . . look at the growing use of green technologies that are making new buildings much more energy efficient.
"These are the areas we should focus on."
Concentrations of greenhouse gases in the Earth's atmosphere have been rising ever since the Industrial Revolution.
Such gases are transparent to incoming radiation from the sun but are opaque to this radiation when it bounces back from Earth at a shorter wavelength. The result is rising heat trapped in the atmosphere.
Reform leader Preston Manning said yesterday he questions that assessment. "I'm still not convinced how serious the contribution is to global warming."
Chretien's statement came as the federal government moves to bring together the nation's leading environmental scientists to discuss the warming of Canada at a climate change symposium.
The symposium, scheduled for Nov. 24 to 26 in Toronto, will be held one week before federal officials go to Kyoto, Japan, to take part in final negotiations on a global warming treaty.
A series of research volumes will be presented at the symposium, including the much anticipated Canada Country Study that assesses the impact of global warming on the nation's ecosystems and its major economic sectors: agriculture, energy, fisheries, forestry, insurance, transport, recreation and tourism.
The symposium is part of an 11th-hour federal information campaign designed to make the slow march of global warming real to Canadians.
Canada remains the only major economic power that has yet to endorse a specific reduction target in advance of the Kyoto conference.

Blinkered perspective on global warming: [4]

Author: Hughes, Larry

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/blinkered-perspective-on-global-warming/docview/384607864/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The geological record shows that there is a correlation between increased concentrations of atmospheric carbon dioxide and rises in global temperature; there is no reason to believe that this relationship no longer holds true. For the past 200 years (since the start of the Industrial Revolution), anthropogenic activities account for the greatest increase in the atmospheric budget of carbon dioxide and carbon dioxide-equivalent gases.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Blinkered+perspective+on+global+warming&amp;author=Hughes%2C+Larry&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-01&amp;spage=D.7&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Blinkered perspective on global warming
Saturday, November 1, 1997
Halifax -- The Globe and Mail's continued stand against action on anthropogenic climate change is both ill-founded and misleading.
The geological record shows that there is a correlation between increased concentrations of atmospheric carbon dioxide and rises in global temperature; there is no reason to believe that this relationship no longer holds true. For the past 200 years (since the start of the Industrial Revolution), anthropogenic activities account for the greatest increase in the atmospheric budget of carbon dioxide and carbon dioxide-equivalent gases.
At present, selected satellite data may appear to suggest that certain parts of the atmosphere that should be warming are not; however, other data are less controversial. Polar-orbiting meteorological satellite images of high latitudes (well into the Arctic circle) show an increase in photosynthesis earlier in the spring; this could be due to increasing temperatures, increasing levels of carbon dioxide, or both.
Computer general circulation models (GCMs) cannot be dismissed as "educated guesswork." It was recently announced that a marked warming has been detected in Alaska, with an average increase of one degree per decade over the past 30 years; these temperature rises were predicted by existing GCMs.
There is a growing body of circumstantial evidence suggesting that increased levels of greenhouse gases are affecting the planet; for example, the unexpected strength of El Nino and the depletion of Arctic ozone. Climate change may not yet be an established fact, but insurance companies are not taking chances.

A family estate with hidden reserves: Minding Your Own Business: Cholderton has dug itself out of a hole, reports Clive Fewins: [London edition]

Author: Fewins, Clive

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/family-estate-with-hidden-reserves-minding-your/docview/248615215/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: From one side of his hilltop water treatment works Henry Edmunds can see the spire of Salisbury Cathedral, from the other, the busy A303 London to Exeter road as it crosses Salisbury Plain. From the same vantage point he can also just about see the area bounding the 800 households served by Britain's smallest water company. Edmunds, 47, is managing director and principal shareholder of that enterprise, the Cholderton and District Water Company. The company dates from 1904, when it was set up under an Act of Parliament by Henry Stephens, the philanthropic MP, owner of Stephens Inks and great-grandfather of Henry Edmunds.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=A+family+estate+with+hidden+reserves%3A+Minding+Your+Own+Business%3A+Cholderton+has+dug+itself+out+of+a+hole%2C+reports+Clive+Fewins%3A&amp;author=Fewins%2C+Clive&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-11-01&amp;spage=02&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: From one side of his hilltop water treatment works Henry Edmunds can see the spire of Salisbury Cathedral, from the other, the busy A303 London to Exeter road as it crosses Salisbury Plain. From the same vantage point he can also just about see the area bounding the 800 households served by Britain's smallest water company.
Edmunds, 47, is managing director and principal shareholder of that enterprise, the Cholderton and District Water Company.
The company dates from 1904, when it was set up under an Act of Parliament by Henry Stephens, the philanthropic MP, owner of Stephens Inks and great-grandfather of Henry Edmunds.
Edmunds took over the reins when his father died in 1975. He runs the business, together with the remainder of the 2,500-acre estate, in much the same way as did his father and grandfather.
During the past 15 turb-ulent years of privatisation, takeovers and amalgamations in the UK water supply industry, Cholderton and District Water Company has resisted all attempts to persuade it to be part of a larger organisation. It still supplies drinking water to fewer than 3,000 people.
Despite the odd emergency, which can mean heavy outlays on buying in labour and equipment, the annual figures have been remarkably even over the past few years, with turnover at around £110,000 and profits after tax of about £10,000.
There has been one exception to this. In 1994, 50 new houses were built in Shipton Bellinger, one of the two villages supplied by the company. The infrastructure and basic connection charges, £540 for each house, helped turnover rise to £115,000 in 1995 and net profits to £28,000.
For the year just ended, Edmunds is expecting a turnover of £120,000, with a net profit of £17,500.
"It is a satisfactory, if unexciting, performance for a company with only two full-time employees - a secretary and an engineer," Edmunds says. "But what is important is that the water company is a great asset to the Cholderton estate. I have 600 cattle and 450 breeding ewes, all of which need a great deal of water.
"I also find it satisfying that local people seem content with the service and the Drinking Water Inspectorate has branded our water 'excellent', with 100 per cent compliance with European standards. In 1996, we were the only UK water company to achieve this."
Nevertheless, despite the lack of a corporate identity, share options, wrangles over leakage rates and fat-cat salaries, and the managing director's car being a battered Land Rover, the company still has its exciting moments. Usually these are attributable to what the insurance industry would call "acts of God", and the climate change that Edmunds is convinced is upon us.
This year, for the first time, Cholderton and District Water Company had to use the standby supply that Edmunds and a team drawn from his 10-strong farm labour force installed over four years at a cost of £50,000.
"It is the first time we have used the second borehole in the six years since it was completed," says Edmunds. "I had decided to install a standby borehole after the very dry summer of 1976. We are a statutory water company - required by Act of Parliament to supply the area we cover - and I knew from our records that by the late 1980s the water table in the chalk under our main borehole had fallen by 70ft.
"This year, the borehole could not maintain the flow rate needed to fill our reservoir so we used water from the standby source to fill it.
"Although we had a reas-onable amount of summer rainfall this year, which reduced demand for mains water for garden watering, summer rainfall is absolutely useless from the point of view of aquifer recharge. Our ground water levels here have dropped even more this year. The water is 225ft down when pumped - a level that goes back to the very dry year of 1976.
"What we really need for aquifer recharge is lots of heavy winter rainfall. But there is lots of water in our new aquifer. I am optimistic that it will meet all our future needs."
Edmunds has just completed installing a £6,000 monitoring system that keeps a check on all the company's main supply pipes. It has shown him what he suspected had been the case for several years: that the company's leakage rate is well under 10 per cent.
"I am fairly sure it is nearer 5 per cent - pretty good, considering some of the large companies admit to rates nearer 40 per cent," he says.
When there is a leak it is a case of all hands to the plough - or rather off the plough. In June this year, a customer rang early one morning to report that he could hear the sound of rushing water beneath the roadside verge outside his house.
"When we lifted a nearby inspection cover we found a swirling torrent," says Edmunds. "To get at the burst pipe, which was in the ground below the inspection pit, we had to dig a huge hole so we could get below the level of the break in order to effect the repair.
"To do this we had to cut off the supply to the village and call in labour from the farm. When this happens the usual result is the managing director up to his knees in water with a spade in his hand, and this was no exception. However. the leak was repaired before lunch."
Edmunds' main accounting maxim is simple: stay in the black at all times. "We have had no lossmaking years, but I should not be averse to borrowing if I had to put in a treatment plant to remove organic chemicals if it proved necessary," he says.
Despite his bucolic surroundings and air of gentlemanly benevolence, Ed- munds sometimes has a fight on his hands when he insists on his legal right to install water meters in commercial premises. "I have never had to go to court yet. Sometimes a business might ring up Ofwat , the regulator, to gain confirmation that I am within my rights to insist on a meter, but that it is as far as it has gone so far," he says.
Neither is Edmunds averse to playing the role of village squire when it comes to collecting his dues. Every few days, the secretary Jennie Blackburn spends several hours driving round the homes of the elderly and those on income support collecting weekly payments.
"It's not the most efficient use of her time, but it helps keep the cash flowing in," Edmunds says. "Besides, the elderly people are often lonely and enjoy a visit."
* Cholderton and District Water Company , The Estate Office, Cholderton, Salisbury, Wilts SP4 ODR. Tel: 01980-629203. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1997. All Rights Reserved.

The Making of a Myth --- What Warming?

Author: By Robert L. Pollock

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/making-myth-what-warming/docview/308255732/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Brussels -- So President Clinton has issued his proposals to combat the supposed threat of global warming. Although they have been criticized by environmentalists and European officials for not going "far enough," they signal his acceptance of the theory that human activity (primarily emissions from the burning of fossil fuels) is adding significantly to the Earth's natural greenhouse effect, that this portends a significant warming of the global climate, and that it is worth diverting a significant amount of society's resources in an attempt to stave off that threat. Of course, in Mr. Clinton's mind, these propositions are no longer worth examining, since "the vast majority of the world's climate scientists have concluded" that man-made global warming is underway. While it may not appear to be "a clear and present danger," said Mr. Clinton, it is real and "far more subtle." It is true that between 1890 and 1990 land-based measurements, which unfortunately exclude much of the Earth's surface, show a warming of about 0.45 degrees Celsius. Trouble is (for the global warming theorists anyway) that 0.34 degrees of this increase occurred before 1940, while most man-made greenhouse gas emissions have occurred since then. In fact, during a period of high emissions, between 1940 and 1975, the average global temperature measurements dropped. And since then, data are inconclusive. Land-based measurements show a slight warming, but are dubious because they may be biased upward by the urban "heat island" effect. (It is well known that cities may be several degrees warmer than the surrounding countryside.) Meanwhile, satellite measurements starting in 1979, which are subject to no such bias and which cover a much greater portion of the Earth, show no warming at all.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+Making+of+a+Myth+---+What+Warming%3F&amp;author=By+Robert+L.+Pollock&amp;issn=09219986&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-24&amp;spage=6&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Brussels -- So President Clinton has issued his proposals to combat the supposed threat of global warming. Although they have been criticized by environmentalists and European officials for not going "far enough," they signal his acceptance of the theory that human activity (primarily emissions from the burning of fossil fuels) is adding significantly to the Earth's natural greenhouse effect, that this portends a significant warming of the global climate, and that it is worth diverting a significant amount of society's resources in an attempt to stave off that threat.
Of course, in Mr. Clinton's mind, these propositions are no longer worth examining, since "the vast majority of the world's climate scientists have concluded" that man-made global warming is underway. While it may not appear to be "a clear and present danger," said Mr. Clinton, it is real and "far more subtle."
So subtle, apparently, that it can't be detected. Actual temperature measurements offer no consistent support for the claim that the Earth is getting warmer, and the link between temperature and the level of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere is more tenuous still.
It is true that between 1890 and 1990 land-based measurements, which unfortunately exclude much of the Earth's surface, show a warming of about 0.45 degrees Celsius. Trouble is (for the global warming theorists anyway) that 0.34 degrees of this increase occurred before 1940, while most man-made greenhouse gas emissions have occurred since then. In fact, during a period of high emissions, between 1940 and 1975, the average global temperature measurements dropped. And since then, data are inconclusive. Land-based measurements show a slight warming, but are dubious because they may be biased upward by the urban "heat island" effect. (It is well known that cities may be several degrees warmer than the surrounding countryside.) Meanwhile, satellite measurements starting in 1979, which are subject to no such bias and which cover a much greater portion of the Earth, show no warming at all.
Why then do global warming theorists enjoy such popular support, and why are the nations of the world planning to sign a treaty in Kyoto, Japan, this December committing themselves to drastic and painful limitations on the use of fossil fuels? Partly because "carbon taxes" promise a new source of revenue, and partly because they actually have been fooled by a highly successful campaign that plays on public ignorance, and tries to disguise its political agenda in the name of science.
Environmentalist groups like Greenpeace, of course, are well-known proponents of the global warming theory. But they have a history of economy with the facts. More insidious are groups that claim to represent the consensus of informed scientific opinion, and which disguise their inconclusive findings in vague but ominous language. That, unfortunately, seems to have been the fate of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, a U.N.-sponsored body whose 1996 report on climate change was widely described as the "smoking gun" linking man-made greenhouse gases to global warming. Actually, the report said no such thing, but the misleading wording of its Policymakers' Summary (the only part of the report most readers will ever see) might give that impression.
In an important article that appeared on this page last year, Frederick Seitz, former president of the U.S. National Academy of Sciences, declared that during more than 60 years of professional life he had "never witnessed a more disturbing corruption of the peer review process than the events that led to this IPCC report." His charge was not that the majority of the scientists who had contributed to the report had not done good work. Rather, following acceptance of the final draft by members of the IPCC, at least 15 sections of Chapter 8, weighing evidence for and against human influence on the climate, were changed. A number of passages emphasizing uncertainty were absent from the published version. The excised material, he pointed out, includes the declaration that "Not one of the studies cited above has shown clear evidence that we can attribute the observed {climate} changes to the specific cause of increases in greenhouse gases."
Mr. Seitz's article spawned a fierce debate on the Journal's letters page, including multiple responses from Benjamin D. Santer, convening lead author of Chapter 8. He took responsibility for the changes and claimed that it was normal for drafts to be altered in response to comments from contributors. But he did not adequately explain why this had not been done before the paper's final approval, and even offered the strange (for a debate about scientific method) argument that his critics were hurting the "well-being" of his family.
The Policymakers' Summary is even more disturbing. It makes no mention of the fact that satellite data show no warming, but renders the verdict that "the balance of the evidence suggests that there is a discernible human influence on global climate." Depending on how you look at it, the statement is either meaningless or a truism. Of course organisms, including people, have significant impacts on the environment and climate in which they live -- we wouldn't have the current balance of oxygen and carbon dioxide in our atmosphere if it weren't for the activities of photosynthetic plants and the respiration of animals, for example. The relevant questions are what kind of influence and how significant. On a literal reading, these are questions the statement begs. The only reason for including it, therefore, would seem to be to allow its authors to imply something the facts cannot support stating directly.
So what about "the vast majority of the world's climate scientists" cited by Mr. Clinton? Well, according to S. Fred Singer, emeritus professor of environmental sciences at the University of Virginia, about 2,100 people were listed as reviewers and contributors on the IPCC report published in 1996. Most, however, aren't climate scientists at all, but scientists from other fields, policy experts or government representatives. There were only about 80 authors of the main report, and the Policymakers' Summary (which many key contributors do not support) was written by just a few. Compare this with about 100 climate scientists who signed the Leipzig Declaration in 1996, questioning the validity of computer-based global warming forecasts, and the debate seems less one-sided than many claim.
Even that, however, is ultimately beside the point. That this has become a purely political debate is shown by the emphasis constantly placed on who is saying what. It is silly to claim, as many do, that scientists taking money from industry are somehow more liable to corruption than those who depend on propounding alarmist theories to renew their government grants. But more fundamentally, the ground rules of scientific procedure are such that it is easy to spot intentional bias.
The "trust us" attitude displayed by some politicians and scientists doesn't wash. This debate is comprehensible by anyone with the ability to read temperature data and a basic understanding of scientific method. Recent temperature data show no unambiguous trend, and we know that there were many swings in global temperature before man started pouring greenhouse gases into the atmosphere.
As for the computer models used to make predictions of future warming, even the term "junk science" would be too kind. Science operates based on clear and testable -- that is, falsifiable -- hypotheses. The problem is, predictive models (which don't even correlate well to past data) are unfalsifiable first because we can't foresee the future, and because even if we could, we have no "control Earth" (that is, without man-made greenhouse gases) with which to compare it. As senior NASA scientist Roy W. Spencer has written, "The theory of global warming will probably never be validated or falsified since we cannot put the Earth in a laboratory to run experiments on." The problem of falisfiability becomes even more stark when global warming alarmists blame any unusual weather (say hurricanes) on climate change.
To be clear, I am not saying that the Earth will not warm in the near future, but only that we have no evidence that it will. Even if it does, we won't be sure whether the change was man-made or part of a natural cycle, and the fact that so many scientists stood by while their work was misused will stand as a dark day for their profession. If, for example, as the IPCC's Santer claims, his revisions did not substantially alter Chapter 8 of the report, he should endorse the excised passages and state publicly what they clearly imply: That the IPCC document is not conclusive enough to serve, as is happening, as a justification for the impending global climate treaty to be signed in Kyoto.
Many, of course, argue that a treaty is the appropriate response to uncertainty -- an "insurance policy on the future," in Mr. Clinton's words. But this is a fundamental misunderstanding of the concept. Insurance redistributes the costs of individual risks among members of a larger group. The only "insurance" society as a whole can have against unforeseen problems is the wealth and technology to deal with them. A treaty that devotes a significant proportion of society's energies to phantom threats may actually increase our risk.
Imagine how much better our environment might be if we stopped worrying about global warming and concentrated on well-known problems like the deforestation and unsanitary living conditions that afflict many of the world's poor countries. As John Christy, a key contributor to the 1996 IPCC report, observes, recalling his days as a missionary in Africa, "Cheap energy means longer and better lives. . . . Every country in the world has benefited from {America's} burning of carbon."
---
Mr. Pollock is an editorial page writer for The Wall Street Journal Europe.

Subject: Global warming; Scientists; Science; Greenhouse gases; Atmosphere; Fossil fuels; Emissions; Greenhouse effect; Human influences; Climate change; Scientific method; Bias

Excerpt From Clinton Comments on Global Warming: [Text]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/excerpt-clinton-comments-on-global-warming/docview/430863376/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The vast majority of the world's climate scientists have concluded that if the countries of the world do not work together to cut the emission of greenhouse gases, then temperatures will rise and will disrupt the climate. In fact, most scientists say the process has already begun. Disruptive weather events are increasing. Disease-bearing insects are moving to areas that used to be too cold for them. Average temperatures are rising. Glacial formations are receding. I want to emphasize that we cannot wait until the treaty is negotiated and ratified to act. We must begin now to take out our insurance policy on the future. In the international climate negotiations the United States will pursue a comprehensive framework that includes three elements, which taken together will enable us to build a strong and robust global agreement. First, the United States proposes at Kyoto that we commit to the binding and realistic target of returning to emissions of 1990 levels between 2008 and 2012. And we should not stop there. We should commit to reduce emissions below 1990 levels and the five-year period thereafter, and we must work toward further reductions in the years ahead.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Excerpt+From+Clinton+Comments+on+Global+Warming&amp;author=&amp;issn=03624331&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-23&amp;spage=A.20&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Following is an excerpt from remarks by President Clinton today to the National Geographic Society on the proposal for stabilizing industrialized nations' release of gases that may cause global warming, as recorded by the Federal News Service, a private transcription company:
The vast majority of the world's climate scientists have concluded that if the countries of the world do not work together to cut the emission of greenhouse gases, then temperatures will rise and will disrupt the climate. In fact, most scientists say the process has already begun. Disruptive weather events are increasing. Disease-bearing insects are moving to areas that used to be too cold for them. Average temperatures are rising. Glacial formations are receding.
Scientists don't yet know what the precise consequences will be, but we do know enough now to know that the Industrial Age has dramatically increased greenhouse gases in the atmosphere, where they take a century or more to dissipate and that the process must be slowed, then stopped, then reduced if we want to continue our economic progress and preserve the quality of life in the United States and throughout our planet.
We know what we have to do. Greenhouse gas emissions are caused mostly by the inefficient burning of coal or oil for energy. Roughly a third of these emissions come from industry, a third from transportation, a third from residential and commercial buildings. In each case, the conversion of fuel to energy use is extremely inefficient and could be made much cleaner with existing technologies or those already on the horizon, in ways that will not weaken the economy but in fact will add to our strength in new businesses and new jobs. If we do this properly, we will not jeopardize our prosperity; we will increase it.
With that principle in mind, I'm announcing the instruction I am giving to our negotiators as they pursue a realistic and effective international climate change treaty. And I'm announcing a far-reaching proposal that provides flexible, market-based and cost-effective ways to achieve meaningful reductions here in America.
I want to emphasize that we cannot wait until the treaty is negotiated and ratified to act. The United States has less than 5 percent of the world's people, enjoys 22 percent of the world's wealth, but emits more than 25 percent of the world's greenhouse gases. We must begin now to take out our insurance policy on the future. In the international climate negotiations the United States will pursue a comprehensive framework that includes three elements, which taken together will enable us to build a strong and robust global agreement. First, the United States proposes at Kyoto that we commit to the binding and realistic target of returning to emissions of 1990 levels between 2008 and 2012. And we should not stop there. We should commit to reduce emissions below 1990 levels and the five-year period thereafter, and we must work toward further reductions in the years ahead.
The industrialized nations tried to reduce emissions to 1990 levels once before with a voluntary approach, but regrettably most of us, including especially the United States, fell short. We must find new resolve to achieve these reductions. And to do that we simply must commit to binding limits.
Second, we will embrace flexible mechanisms for meeting these limits. We propose an innovative joint implementation system that allows a firm in one country to invest in a project that reduces emissions in another country and receive credit for those reductions at home. And we propose an international system of emissions trading.
These innovations will cut worldwide pollution, keep costs low and help developing countries protect their environment, too, without sacrificing their economic growth.
Third, both industrialized and developing countries must participate in meeting the challenge of climate change. The industrialized world must lead, but developing countries also must be engaged. The United States will not assume binding obligations unless key developing nations meaningfully participate in this effort. As President Carlos Menem stated forcefully last week when I visited him in Argentina, a global problem such as climate change requires a global answer.
If the entire industrialized world reduces emissions over the next several decades, but emissions from the developing world continue to grow at their current pace, concentrations of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere will continue to climb. Developing countries have an opportunity to chart a different energy future, consistent with their growth potential and their legitimate economic aspirations. What Argentina, with dramatic projected economic growth, recognizes is true for other countries as well. We can and we must work together on this problem in a way that benefits us all.
Here at home, we must move forward by unleashing the full power of free markets and technological innovations to meet the challenge of climate change. I propose a sweeping plan to provide incentives and lift roadblocks to help our companies and our citizens find new and creative ways of reducing greenhouse gas emissions.
Credit: By The New York Times

Clinton Details Global Warming Plan; Market Incentives, Modest Targets Draw Critics From Left and Right: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Warrick, Joby; Baker, Peter

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/clinton-details-global-warming-plan-market/docview/408338791/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: President Clinton pledged yesterday to "harness the power of the free market" for a multipronged assault on greenhouse-gas pollution, outlining a package of incentives and modest targets he says will counter global warming while allowing businesses to prosper. Clinton released long-awaited details of his climate change policy in a speech that drew skepticism abroad and condemnations from both ends of the U.S. political spectrum. But others praised the president for committing to a serious, if cautious, plan for addressing what many scientists see as the planet's greatest environmental threat. Clinton outlined a strategy that beginning in 1998 would offer $5 billion in tax breaks and other incentives to U.S. companies to encourage rapid improvements in fuel efficiency and spur the development of new "clean-energy" technologies to reduce greenhouse gas emissions. He also called for an international emissions trading system that would earn credits for companies that cut pollution, and he promised further reductions under an ambitious restructuring of the electric utilities industry.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Clinton+Details+Global+Warming+Plan%3B+Market+Incentives%2C+Modest+Targets+Draw+Critics+From+Left+and+Right&amp;author=Warrick%2C+Joby%3BBaker%2C+Peter&amp;issn=01908286&amp;title=The+Washington+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-23&amp;spage=A.01&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: President Clinton pledged yesterday to "harness the power of the free market" for a multipronged assault on greenhouse-gas pollution, outlining a package of incentives and modest targets he says will counter global warming while allowing businesses to prosper.
Clinton released long-awaited details of his climate change policy in a speech that drew skepticism abroad and condemnations from both ends of the U.S. political spectrum. But others praised the president for committing to a serious, if cautious, plan for addressing what many scientists see as the planet's greatest environmental threat.
"We must begin now to take out our insurance policy on the future," Clinton told about 400 invited government officials and guests at the National Geographic Society. He said global warming "is real," and the "consequences, sooner or later, will be destructive for America and for the world."
Clinton outlined a strategy that beginning in 1998 would offer $5 billion in tax breaks and other incentives to U.S. companies to encourage rapid improvements in fuel efficiency and spur the development of new "clean-energy" technologies to reduce greenhouse gas emissions. He also called for an international emissions trading system that would earn credits for companies that cut pollution, and he promised further reductions under an ambitious restructuring of the electric utilities industry.
But the specific goals and timet ables he proposed for cutting international emissions fall far short of what some scientists and many environmentalists say are needed. They also are considerably less ambitious than the proposals proffered by other industrial powers.
The president's plan calls for stabilizing the industrialized world's output of carbon dioxide and other greenhouse gases at 1990 levels between 2008 and 2012 -- followed by further, unspecified reductions by 2017. The European Union, by contrast, favors limiting emissions at 15 percent below 1990 levels by 2010.
Talks are underway this week in Bonn, Germany, to try reach an agreement on an international plan to be signed in December in Kyoto, Japan. The treaty would set mandatory ceilings on emissions by nations around the world and impose penalties on countries that fail to comply.
Some business and labor groups say that even the relatively modest restrictions sought by Clinton would strangle the economic growth, raise energy prices and put millions of people out of work. But Clinton said his program would actually boost the economy by increasing efficiency and creating opportunities for new products and markets.
"If we do it right, protecting the climate will yield not costs, but profits, not burdens but benefits, not sacrifice, but a higher standard of living," he said.
White House officials portrayed the policy as "bold" and a "win-win," saying the tax breaks, flexible guidelines and other inducements would minimize the negative impact on industry and result in deep reductions in pollution before the mandatory limits even kick in.
Officials said the plan does not mandate any increase in energy costs, but they acknowledged that prices for gasoline and other fuels could rise. The administration released no economic analyses showing what impact binding targets might have, saying it is virtually impossible to predict the effects 13 years into the future.
But congressional and industry opponents said Clinton's proposed "cure" would be far worse than the disease -- if global warming is indeed a threat at all. A coalition representing oil companies, electric utilities, automobile manufactures and farm groups have launched a multimillion dollar advertising to generate opposition to a treaty in Kyoto.
"The Clinton-Gore global climate plan threatens the quality of life for all Americans," said Rep. F. James Sensenbrenner Jr. (R-Wis.). He said the restrictions would "result in higher energy prices for working Americans and send U.S. jobs overseas."
But some business groups -- especially those representing alternative energy technologies -- praised the president's plan. "This is a measured, appropriate action plan, given what we know about global warming," said Terry Thorn, senior vice president of Enron Corp. of Houston.
More than a dozen senior executives representing such companies as Nike Inc., Bechtel Group Inc. and Mitsubishi Motor Corp. have endorsed a newspaper ad running this week that calls for "strong leadership" by the United States on climate change.
Reaction among environmental groups was mixed. Several mainstream groups that were prepared to endorse the president's package were deeply disappointed when the final details became public. "The air went out of the balloon," said John Adams, executive director of the Natural Resources Defense Council.
"The president has recognized that the global warming train is bearing down on the planet. But the policy announced today will not go nearly far enough to get us out of harm's way," Adams said.
The proposal falls short of the goals that Clinton outlined in 1993, when he called for voluntary cuts in greenhouse gas emissions to 1990 levels by 2000. The administration says that goal is not longer possible because the economy has grown more rapidly than expected.
Some environmentalists said the incentives for immediate cuts in greenhouse gases in the new proposal more than atoned for any shortcomings in the proposed pollution ceilings and timetables.
"The president's proposal puts the United States in the thick of the negotiations rather than on the fringe," Michael Oppenheimer, an atmospheric physicist with the Environmental Defense Fund. "For the first time there is a good chance of a constructive outcome at Kyoto."
Still, with less than two months remaining before the Kyoto conference, the administration faced a difficult task in closing the gap between its proposals and those of its European and Japanese allies.
Administration officials found little encouragement in a new proposal by developing countries, which yesterday called for cuts even more ambitious than the Europeans' -- while essentially exempting themselves from any binding limits in the near future.
A statement by the "Group of 77" countries in Bonn sought commitments from wealthier nations for a 35 percent reduction in greenhouse gases by 2020. It said poorer countries should be compensated for economic hardships that might result from efforts by the West to cut pollution at home.
Clinton, in his speech yesterday, said the United States would insist on participation by developing countries as a condition for signing any global treaty on greenhouse gases. But he gave no specifics.
"Exactly what participation will mean is obviously what our guys are going to be doing in Kyoto," said White House economic adviser Gene Sperling.
Illustration PHOTO,,Robert A. Reeder CAPTION:HIGHLIGHTS CLINTON CLIMATE CHANGE PLAN + Sets binding targets to reach 1990 greenhouse gas emis-sions levels by 2008 to 2012 and get below 1990 levels in the following five years. + Earmarks $5 billion in tax cuts and spending to spur energy efficiency and new technology research and development. + Insists that developing countries also commit to reducing emissions. + Calls for, after a decade, a market-based system in which countries and firms can earn "credits" by reducing emissions more than required and sell them to those that have not met the targets.
