U.S. proposes gradual reduction of greenhouse gases Canada now under pressure to announce position publicly as Clinton extends target to 2012 for mandatory measures

Author: Cohen, Andrew; McIlroy, Anne

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/u-s-proposes-gradual-reduction-greenhouse-gases/docview/384775922/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: "I think that we made a mistake in 1992 at Rio in saying that we would make a commitment to level our emissions to 1990 by the year 2000," Ms. [Christine Stewart] told reporters outside the House of Commons. "We didn't realize what was implied by making that commitment."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=U.S.+proposes+gradual+reduction+of+greenhouse+gases+Canada+now+under+pressure+to+announce+position+publicly+as+Clinton+extends+target+to+2012+for+mandatory+measures&amp;author=Cohen%2C+Andrew%3BMcIlroy%2C+Anne&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-23&amp;spage=A.21&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Canada now under pressure to announce position publicly as Clinton extends target to 2012 for mandatory measures
U.S. proposes gradual reduction of greenhouse gases
Canada now under pressure to announce position publicly as Clinton extends target to 2012 for mandatory measures
Thursday, October 23, 1997
WASHINGTON, OTTAWA -- With a report from Canadian Press

  

As the final negotiations on a global-warming treaty opened in Germany, the United States has proposed a moderate, gradual reduction in greenhouse gases that is likely to win few friends either at home or abroad.
After months of division within his administration, President Bill Clinton said yesterday he would agree to mandatory measures to reduce carbon dioxide, methane and other gases believed to contribute to global warming to the levels of 1990. But he also said that now would take as long as 2012 -- 12 years longer than he had proposed four years ago, and far longer than the European Union, environmentalists and others have urged.
Mr. Clinton also proposed tax breaks and technological innovation -- rather than energy taxes -- to attack global warming. He said they would help the world "take out an insurance policy on the future," calling global warming "one of the most important challenges of the 21st century."
His announcement puts enormous pressure on the Canadian government to say what it is willing to do to deal with greenhouse gases.
Canada now is the only country in the Group of Seven industrialized nations that has not made its position public.
In Ottawa, Environment Minister Christine Stewart both lowered expectations of environmentalists and signalled comfort to those worried that Canada will take drastic action at an international conference in December when she conceded that the federal government made a mistake when it signed the climate-change treaty at the 1992 Earth Summit in Brazil.
In signing, Canada promised to cut emissions to 1990 levels by the year 2000. Instead, Canada has increased emissions by as much as 13 per cent and has been singled out by a United Nations report as one of the worst greenhouse-gas polluters in the world.
"I think that we made a mistake in 1992 at Rio in saying that we would make a commitment to level our emissions to 1990 by the year 2000," Ms. Stewart told reporters outside the House of Commons. "We didn't realize what was implied by making that commitment."
Environmentalists were upset with Ms. Stewart, because they see her as the strongest voice in the cabinet arguing for action on global warming.
They say Mr. Clinton's proposal will do very little to keep the planet from getting hotter or to protect it from the disruptions of a volatile climate. Those disruptions include rising sea levels, disappearing shore lines and more frequent violent storms.
"Ms. Stewart seems to be arguing that it was a mistake because it was a difficult commitment to make," said Robert Hornung, of the Pembina Institute, a environmental research and advocacy group. "The only reason we have not met that commitment is because this government has not had the political courage to do more than simply call for voluntary action by industry to reduce emissions."
Natural Resources Minister Ralph Goodale, meanwhile, said Ottawa may consider using taxes to curb emissions if all other means fail.
Speaking outside the Commons, he rejected the notion of a carbon tax, which would apply to fossil fuels, but said other tax measures were still a possibility.
A spokesman for Canadian energy producers said he was encouraged by Mr. Clinton's comments. Chris Peirce, vice-president of strategic planning for the Canadian Association of Petroleum Producers, said the idea of using incentives rather than sanctions was promising. The targets suggested by Mr. Clinton also are encouraging, he said.
"We think there are clearly some elements there that are moving in the right direction," said Mr. Peirce, whose 180 companies produce 95 per cent of Canada's natural gas and crude oil.
A spokesman for Alberta Environment Minister Ty Lund, meanwhile, confirmed that Alberta would reject any "legally binding" international agreement on climate change signed by Canada at Kyoto. Alberta is concerned that its energy industry could be devastated if Canada commits itself to cutting emissions to 1990 levels.
Mr. Clinton has made a global-warming treaty a pillar of his foreign policy, suggesting that it may be one of his enduring accomplishments. That sense of urgency was reflected in yesterday's remarks, delivered before the National Geographic Society.
"Make no mistake -- the problem is real," he warned, addressing scientists who deny any material threat in greenhouse gases. "If we don't change course now, the consequences sooner or later will be destructive for America and for the world."
Still, in proposing gradual reductions in greenhouse gases, Mr. Clinton was seeking characteristic compromise. Mandatory controls are to appeal to those who argue global warming is a problem and voluntary restraints -- which Mr. Clinton proposed in 1992 -- are not enough. His commitment to gradualism, however, was to appeal to an industry that fears binding controls will raise energy prices, create unemployment and suffocate economic growth.
If Mr. Clinton hoped to find a compromise, it seemed elusive. Senator Chuck Hagel, who sponsored a motion in the Senate last summer against global warming, said Mr. Clinton had not offered strong enough assurances that all nations -- not just the industrialized ones -- would have to bind themselves to the treaty. He predicted that the Senate, which must ratify the treaty by a two-thirds vote, would balk.
Other antitreaty groups, such as the Global Climate Commission, which represents utilities and automakers, also reaffirmed their opposition to any binding commitments.
Supporters, for their part, also were disappointed. They said a modest proposal would be fine if global warming were a modest threat, but they maintained that it isn't.
"If you look at the science and see the overwhelming magnitude of the threat, you can see that the proposal is embarrassingly light," said John Passacantando of Ozone Action, one of the many ad hoc organizations lobbying for the treaty.
"Lincoln did not free half the slaves, Kennedy did not want to go halfway to the moon, and Johnson did not move Rosa Parks to the middle of the bus."
Mr. Clinton's proposal comes days after the Energy Department reported that emissions of greenhouse gases rose 3.5 per cent last year, in contrast to 8 per cent in the preceding six years. The large increase is likely to lend strength to the calls from treaty supporters for Washington to do more.
The Europeans, for example, want to stabilize gases at 1990 levels by the year 2000. They want cuts of 7.5 per cent by 2005 and another 7.5 per cent by 2012. Under Mr. Clinton's proposal, those gases would only reach 1990 levels by 2012, and begin falling gradually over the next five years, probably by about 5 per cent.

U.S. official to set off flurry of high-level visits

Author: Haniffa, Aziz

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/u-s-official-set-off-flurry-high-level-visits/docview/362705217/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Pickering, who will be in India Oct. 17-18 and Pakistan Oct. 19-20 accompanied by senior officials from the State Department's Policy Planning Bureau, will do the preparatory work for the visits by Secretary of State Madeleine Albright - in late November - and the President. Administration officials said Pickering will engage in "broad-based discussions," in both New Delhi and Islamabad, which will be "comprehensive and forward looking and deal with bilateral, regional and global issues." Some officials categorically discounted a possible "deal" between the U.S. and India, in which the latter in return for more U.S. flexibility in the transfer of high technology to India would consider some "give" on the Comprehensive Test Ban Treaty (CTBT) - which India has refused to sign. But other officials said such a possibility "cannot be totally dismissed." While acknowledging that most of the high-level visits will include both India and Pakistan, the officials insisted that this did not mean Washington is indicating an equivalency between Delhi and Islamabad or doing it to recognize a balance of power in the region. One source said, "I think we are better off visiting both India and Pakistan because then we are less likely to be accused of showing a tilt to one side or the other than if we did visit only one country."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=U.S.+official+to+set+off+flurry+of+high-level+visits&amp;author=Haniffa%2C+Aziz&amp;issn=00468932&amp;title=India+Abroad&amp;volume=XXVIII&amp;issue=3&amp;date=1997-10-17&amp;spage=12&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   U.S. official to set off flurry of high-level visits
After a nearly two-decade famine of visits to India by high-level U.S. representatives, the Under Secretary of State for Political Affairs, Thomas Pickering, this week kicks off a veritable feast of visits that will culminate with President Clinton's in February.
Pickering, who will be in India Oct. 17-18 and Pakistan Oct. 19-20 accompanied by senior officials from the State Department's Policy Planning Bureau, will do the preparatory work for the visits by Secretary of State Madeleine Albright - in late November - and the President. Administration officials said Pickering will engage in "broad-based discussions," in both New Delhi and Islamabad, which will be "comprehensive and forward looking and deal with bilateral, regional and global issues."
They said the "purpose is to exchange views and deepen understanding on a broad range of issues," so that when he returns he can brief Albright and the President, who would consequently be on top of the issues that would make up the agenda for the talks during their visits.
The officials said Pickering's visit is envisaged as enabling the Policy Planning Bureau and the South Asia Bureau at the State Department and relevant offices in the National Security Council to "chart the agendas" that hopefully would help put U.S. relations with both India and Pakistan "on track on the strength of each country's merits."
While some sources hoped Pickering's visit would evolve into a "high-level security dialogue" between New Delhi and Washington, other sources said it is far too early to be talking about or for that matter even thinking about "a strategic partnership."
The latter emphasized that "what we basically want to do is strengthen and deepen the relationship so that we (the United States and India) feel totally comfortable with each other and can discuss even the contentious issues in a mature way."
Thus, a strategic partnership is "not something we are looking for," as some staunchly pro-India legislators in Congress are advocating, the sources said. The sources acknowledged that Pickering would be discussing proliferation issues and regional issues, including India's relations with Pakistan and China and how the U.S. could play a catalytic role in improving these ties in a nonintrusive, noninterfering manner.
On the fringes of the United Nations General Assembly and the recent summit between Prime Minister I.K. Gujral and Clinton in New York, Pickering and India's Foreign Secretary K. Raghunath also discussed in "a general way" some of the issues, including security issues, that could figure in the talks when Pickering gets to India, the sources said.
Since Pickering is one of the senior officials in the State Department, having served in postings to several embassies, including the one in India, and holds the title of a career ambassador - the equivalent of a four-star general - the sources said he had an "excellent rapport" with officials belonging to all sectors in South Asia. Thus, the officials said, "he is one who can offer the best balanced judgment that is critical for policy making."
Some officials categorically discounted a possible "deal" between the U.S. and India, in which the latter in return for more U.S. flexibility in the transfer of high technology to India would consider some "give" on the Comprehensive Test Ban Treaty (CTBT) - which India has refused to sign. But other officials said such a possibility "cannot be totally dismissed."
The rationale among some senior officials that the U.S. needs to obtain some sort of a compromise with India on the CTBT is that unless some assurances are forthcoming, the Senate is unlikely to ratify the treaty. During their talks in New York last month, Gujral assured the Clinton that India "would be pleased to engage" the U.S. in further discussion on this subject."
But U.S. officials acknowledged that they are "very much aware of the reality" that Gujral is in no position to acquiesce to any compromise Washington may offer that could be viewed as a concession on India's stand on the CTBT, because of the weak position of government.
Soon after Pickering returns, Health and Human Services Secretary Donna Shalala will visit India on a "fact-finding trip," to get "a feel of how India tackles its enormous health and human services problems and how the U.S. could cooperate in these areas," officials said.
Meanwhile, State Department officials acknowledged that the schedule of Albright "is being formulated as we speak," and said in all probability she should be in India some time after Nov. 20 after attending an economic summit in Doha, hosted by the President of Qatar.
How long she spends in India and Pakistan will depend on how many days she spends in the Middle East, since the South Asia part would be an extension of this trip. "But at max (maximum), I don't expect her to spend more than three days each" in India and Pakistan, one source said.
The officials emphasized that Albright will not be discussing any contentious issues or carrying any proposals, but would only be picking up on some of the themes contained in the agendas prepared by Pickering and others. "Basically the idea behind her trip is to give the region a higher priority than it has enjoyed in recent years, and to show that South Asia will be on our radar screen," one source explained.
Following Albright's visit, Commerce Secretary William Daley will go out to India accompanied by some chief executive officers of leading companies, both top-tier and middle-tier companies doing business in India and exploring the potential of investing in India.
However, officials acknowledged that Daley's trip will hardly be as high profile as the one under taken by the late Commerce Secretary Ron Brown two years ago, which resulted in the signing of $6 billion in memorandums of understanding.
But, they said, it would nonetheless include the signing of some business deals and establishment of joint ventures and "tightening up of some of the projects that are currently on tap." Reinforcing Daley's trip would be the visit of Stuart Eisenstadt, the Under Secretary of State for Economic Affairs who was earlier the Undersecretary of Commerce for International Trade. He will be leading a U.S. delegation to a meeting of the Joint Indo-U.S. Subcommission in New Delhi.
At about the same time, Tim Wirth, Under Secretary of State for Global Affairs, will also visit India in order to lobby for India's support for the U.S.-led climate change accord that will be discussed at a forthcoming summit in Kyodo, Japan.
While acknowledging that most of the high-level visits will include both India and Pakistan, the officials insisted that this did not mean Washington is indicating an equivalency between Delhi and Islamabad or doing it to recognize a balance of power in the region. One source said, "I think we are better off visiting both India and Pakistan because then we are less likely to be accused of showing a tilt to one side or the other than if we did visit only one country."
With regard to Clinton's visit in February, officials said that it's "far too early" to even think about what will be discussed.
But they noted that the high-level visits by his Cabinet officials, particularly Albright, "will certainly set the stage for the President's visit and some of the issues that will figure in the agenda."
The consensus among all the officials was that a permeating theme in the visits by Cabinet members as well as that of the President would be the Indo-U.S. relationship and how "it can be further strengthened and accelerated for mutual benefit."
In this regard, the officials said the Administration was hoping that some of the knotty issues like the privatization of the insurance sector and the import restrictions that had forced the U.S. to haul India before the World Trade Organization dispute settlement body will resolved before Clinton's Air Force One enters India's air space.

Storm breathes life into woodlands: The destructive winds of October 1987 may have benefited the environment: [London edition]

Author: Boulton, Leyla

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/storm-breathes-life-into-woodlands-destructive/docview/248590718/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Great Storm of 1987 was responsible for the worst destruction to hit Britain since the second world war. But 10 years later, some conservationists have decided that the storm was, on balance, good for the environment. Back then, a third of the trees at Kew Gardens, the nation's prized botanical centre, were ripped out of the ground, prompting Christine Brandt, a spokeswoman, to complain: "It is like our children are gone. Kew Gardens will never be the same in our lifetime." The 100mph hurricane winds killed 13 people and caused £2bn worth of damage. But Mr Kirby points out that the gales' impact on the natural environment was not confined to the Kew-style "blow downs which people have concentrated on". Their more common effect was to inject variety into plant and animal life by "punching holes" into woodlands across the south of England.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Storm+breathes+life+into+woodlands%3A+The+destructive+winds+of+October+1987+may+have+benefited+the+environment%3A&amp;author=Boulton%2C+Leyla&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-15&amp;spage=08&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The Great Storm of 1987 was responsible for the worst destruction to hit Britain since the second world war. But 10 years later, some conservationists have decided that the storm was, on balance, good for the environment.
Back then, a third of the trees at Kew Gardens, the nation's prized botanical centre, were ripped out of the ground, prompting Christine Brandt, a spokeswoman, to complain: "It is like our children are gone. Kew Gardens will never be the same in our lifetime."
But 10 years on, Keith Kirby, woodlands officer at English Nature , the official conservation agency, argues that the environment reaped unexpected benefits from the cataclysm.
"For many the storm was obviously a tragedy but from the conservation point of view, it was quite beneficial," he says.
The 100mph hurricane winds killed 13 people and caused £2bn worth of damage. But Mr Kirby points out that the gales' impact on the natural environment was not confined to the Kew-style "blow downs which people have concentrated on". Their more common effect was to inject variety into plant and animal life by "punching holes" into woodlands across the south of England.
In many cases, new plant species have flourished thanks to the space and sunlight cleared by the storm. The starved wood sedge, which had become almost extinct, returned to a site near Godalming in Surrey in one of the more spectacular examples of the environmental improvements wrought by the storm.
The dead wood left on the ground by flattened trees also provided an ideal home for rare fungi, beetles and larvae. "Species associated with dead wood and decay are some of the most threatened because we normally manage forests for timber," explains Mr Kirby. "We don't normally leave dead wood around so anything that depends on it has been having a very tough time for the last 1,000 years."
But some environmentalists view the storm from a darker perspective, linked to global warming. Only last month Sir Robert May, the government's chief scientific adviser, mentioned the 1987 storm in a list of extreme weather events that could be evidence that the planet's climate is already changing.
"The indications from climate models are that . .. by the middle of the next century, the incidence of gales across the country is predicted to increase on average by 30 per cent if no major global actions are taken," he said.
Andrew Dlugolecki, group assistant general manager at General Accident , the insurer, notes that the 1987 storm was a "one-off" compared to ones in 1990 and 1993. But as one of the leaders of the insurance industry's attempts to promote vigorous action to combat climate change, Mr Dlugolecki believes extreme weather events are likely to increase in frequency.
A government study he helped prepare reckons the annual average cost of storm damage could double to £500m as a result of global warming.
Mr Kirby is unconvinced that the recent severe storms are a sign of climate change. But even he agrees that if they do become more frequent, Britain's woods and beetles are unlikely to be so lucky next time.
Leyla Boulton Copyright Financial Times Limited 1997. All Rights Reserved.

Corporate green

Author: Barber, Stephen

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/corporate-green/docview/384769041/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Not content to provide us with just one non sequitur, Mr. [Terence Corcoran] also infers that concerns about climate change are irrelevant because they only protect "wolves and grizzly bears." Dramatic climate changes will also have a serious impact on human quality of life and economic health, a fact that is slowly being recognized by insurance companies.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Corporate+green&amp;author=Barber%2C+Stephen&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-14&amp;spage=B.2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Corporate green
Tuesday, October 14, 1997
Toronto -- It would appear that Terence Corcoran has joined the ranks of those who believe that our economic system exists independently of our environment (Wild World of Corporate Green -- Oct. 7).
His statement that the preservation of some of our remaining natural environment will "destroy property and individual rights" is a perfect example of a conclusion unrelated to the evidence. Protecting the natural environment is simply recognition that individual and property rights must be balanced with the needs of the entire population.
Not content to provide us with just one non sequitur, Mr. Corcoran also infers that concerns about climate change are irrelevant because they only protect "wolves and grizzly bears." Dramatic climate changes will also have a serious impact on human quality of life and economic health, a fact that is slowly being recognized by insurance companies.
Perhaps it is time to remind those who would reassert the tired old anti-environmental rhetoric that human beings are animals, and we rely on the same environment as wolves and grizzly bears for survival. The natural environment will not change to reflect economic "realities." Our economic system must change to reflect the reality of the complicated ecosystem that we all -- human, plant and animal -- depend upon.

Climate Change Imposes Heavy Financial Cost: [Letter]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/climate-change-imposes-heavy-financial-cost/docview/430861952/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: ''Doubts on Cost Are Bedeviling Climate Policy'' (front page, Oct. 6) examines the costs of changing energy policies to address climate change, but the issue of avoiding the economic damages from climate disruption receives less mention. The insurance industry and public health community are not so sanguine.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Climate+Change+Imposes+Heavy+Financial+Cost&amp;author=&amp;issn=03624331&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-13&amp;spage=A.14&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: To the Editor:
''Doubts on Cost Are Bedeviling Climate Policy'' (front page, Oct. 6) examines the costs of changing energy policies to address climate change, but the issue of avoiding the economic damages from climate disruption receives less mention. The insurance industry and public health community are not so sanguine.
Records from the National Climatic Data Center indicate that extreme weather events have increased in the continental United States and elsewhere. Droughts have become longer and bursts of precipitation more intense. Worldwide, the rise in severe wind and flood-related events has caused extraordinary losses for property insurers. Annual United States reinsurance payouts have risen sevenfold this decade.
To avoid the costs of climate disruption, a shift in priorities, credits, subsidies and incentives will be needed to help develop technologies that steer us into a renewable and energy-efficient future. The biggest surprise may be the better distributed economic opportunities produced by this transition.
FRANKLIN NUTTERBoston, Oct. 8, 1997

The writers are, respectively, associate director, Center for Health and the Global Environment, Harvard Medical School, and president, Reinsurance Association of America.

How to avoid the costs of climate change: [City Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/how-avoid-costs-climate-change/docview/403900233/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: An Oct. 10 Associated Press report ("Greenhouse gas proposal studied," National Briefs) describes the economic quandary the Clinton administration faces in trying to change energy policies that address climate change. In this debate, the economic damages from climate disruption have received only minor mention. The insurance industry and public health community are beginning to show major signs of strain.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=How+to+avoid+the+costs+of+climate+change&amp;author=&amp;issn=07431791&amp;title=Boston+Globe&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-13&amp;spage=A.14&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   LETTERS TO THE EDITOR
An Oct. 10 Associated Press report ("Greenhouse gas proposal studied," National Briefs) describes the economic quandary the Clinton administration faces in trying to change energy policies that address climate change. In this debate, the economic damages from climate disruption have received only minor mention. The insurance industry and public health community are beginning to show major signs of strain.
National Climatic Data Center records indicate that extreme weather events have increased in the continental United States and elsewhere. Droughts have become longer and bursts of precipitation more intense. Worldwide, the rise in severe wind and flood-related events has caused extraordinary losses for property insurers. Annual US reinsurance payouts have risen sevenfold this decade.
Extreme weather events and increased climate instability can also have devastating consequences for human health. And outbreaks of pests and diseases can affect agricultural crops, fisheries, livestock and humans; and their impacts can ripple through economies. Surprises such as the enormous haze and pall stemming from Indonesia's blaze (deforestation compounded by severe drought), have acute and chronic respiratory impacts and are harming travel, trade, and tourism.
To avoid the costs of climate disruption, a shift in priorities, credits, subsidies, and incentives will be needed in order to develop the transitional, hybrid, and advanced technologies needed to steer us into a renewable and energy-efficient future. The biggest surprise may be the better distributed economic opportunities produced by this transition. PAUL R. EPSTEIN Cambridge FRANKLIN NUTTER Washington, D.C. Paul R. Epstein is associate director of the Center for Health and the Global Environment, Harvard Medical School. Franklin Nutter is president of the Reinsurance Association of America.

Subject: Climate change

Title: How to avoid the costs of climate change:   [City Edition ]

Publication title: Boston Globe; Boston, Mass.

Pages: A.14

Number of pages: 0

Publication year: 1997

Publication date: Oct 13, 1997

Global-Warming Issue Bears Close Watching

Author: By Alan Murray

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-issue-bears-close-watching/docview/398801180/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: It's no surprise Americans increasingly conclude Washington is irrelevant to their lives. After all, things out there are going pretty well. Things in here have seldom been screwier. Why should people care whether the president greets his guests in the Oval Office or the Map Room? Whether the vice president makes phone calls at the office or at home? Janet Reno may be mad, but the rest of the country is just bored. There is, however, another less visible but no less raging debate going on here that could touch the lives of every American. Its subject: global warming. The White House is considering a plan that would reduce carbon-dioxide emissions in the U.S. to 1990 levels by as early as 2010. The efforts to make that happen, White House economists believe, could raise gasoline prices by 25 cents to 50 cents a gallon and electricity bills by 20% to 40%. The total effect would be equivalent to a tax increase of $100 billion to $200 billion a year. Global warming isn't just a fear; it's a fact-or at least as close to a fact as science usually gets. Average global temperatures have risen about one degree Fahrenheit during the past century. Moreover, research suggests there is a strong connection between average temperatures and carbon dioxide in the atmosphere. Over the next century, the carbon dioxide in the atmosphere is projected to double. As a result, the Earth's average temperature probably will rise by two to six degrees Fahrenheit.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Global-Warming+Issue+Bears+Close+Watching&amp;author=By+Alan+Murray&amp;issn=00999660&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-13&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: WASHINGTON -- It's no surprise Americans increasingly conclude Washington is irrelevant to their lives. After all, things out there are going pretty well. Things in here have seldom been screwier. Why should people care whether the president greets his guests in the Oval Office or the Map Room? Whether the vice president makes phone calls at the office or at home? Janet Reno may be mad, but the rest of the country is just bored. There is, however, another less visible but no less raging debate going on here that could touch the lives of every American. Its subject: global warming.
Enviroskeptics, who have decided to let their grandchildren worry about their own air-conditioning bills, may be inclined to tune this out, too. They shouldn't. It isn't just about global warming in the next century. It's also about what might be done in the next few years to slow it.
The White House is considering a plan that would reduce carbon-dioxide emissions in the U.S. to 1990 levels by as early as 2010. The efforts to make that happen, White House economists believe, could raise gasoline prices by 25 cents to 50 cents a gallon and electricity bills by 20% to 40%. The total effect would be equivalent to a tax increase of $100 billion to $200 billion a year.
Global warming isn't just a fear; it's a fact-or at least as close to a fact as science usually gets. Average global temperatures have risen about one degree Fahrenheit during the past century. Moreover, research suggests there is a strong connection between average temperatures and carbon dioxide in the atmosphere. Over the next century, the carbon dioxide in the atmosphere is projected to double. As a result, the Earth's average temperature probably will rise by two to six degrees Fahrenheit.
That's where science ends and speculation begins. Some effects of global warming can be predicted. The melting of the polar icecaps would put beachfront property in Florida in jeopardy, and sugar maples would disappear from New England. But globalwarming worriers take the argument much further.
Ross Gelbspan's book "The Heat is On" -- which President Clinton was spotted carrying during his vacation -- predicts a biblical onslaught: hurricanes, droughts, waves of disease-carrying vermin, a general breakdown of the ecosystem, chaos and, ultimately, totalitarianism. (A quote on the book jacket refers to Mr. Gelbspan as an "optimist.") Those arguments should be taken with a large bag of rock salt. And one could speculate about some technological breakthrough that might eliminate the need for fossil fuels entirely.
But even without going to extremes, there is reason to worry. The more difficult question that faces President Clinton is this: How much should the nation sacrifice now to buy insurance against threats in the future? To do nothing would be irresponsible. But to pay $100 billion to $200 billion a year in higher energy costs is to pay a lot.
That's exactly the debate going on in the White House. The economists argue the cost is too high, given that the benefits are so uncertain. Environmentalists hate the very notion of cost-benefit analysis and suspect those using it are tools of the oil industry. We only have one Earth, they say. They also dispute cost estimates, noting the government's success in reducing sulfur-dioxide emissions at lower-than-estimated costs.
The current political environment makes the issues even more difficult. President Clinton, worried about how posterity will view him, doesn't want to ignore a problem that could wreck posterity. Nor did he enjoy being lectured by other world leaders at the Denver summit about how his government has failed to address the issues. (The Europeans and Japanese want to reduce emissions below 1990 levels.)
Vice President Al Gore feels the pressure even more intensely. He has built his reputation on environmental issues and can't afford to go into the 2000 election with environmentalists feeling he has betrayed them.
Yet both men know that proposing even something as modest as a 25-centa-gallon gasoline tax for this purpose wouldn't sell with the American public, which is busy buying gas-guzzling sports vehicles, or with Congress.
So what to do? Mr. Clinton will probably try to have it both ways. He will commit to stabilizing emissions at 1990 levels by either 2010 or perhaps 2015 or 2020 but will be vague about the costs. Instead of a tax, he will talk about an emissions-trading system but play down the effect such a system would have on energy prices. And his negotiators will make sure any language agreed to in Kyoto, Japan, has ample wiggle room.
Before the U.S public makes large sacrifices to slow global warming, it will need to be convinced of the magnitude of the problem. On that score, Mr. Clinton and Mr. Gore still have a lot of work to do.
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: Cooler Heads Discuss Global Warning" -- WSJ Oct. 29, 1997)

BRAZIL -- U.S. President Bill Clinton arrives in Brasilia, on his first trip to South America. He will also travel to Argentina and Venezuela.
CHINA -- World financial leaders will gather in Hong Kong at the World Economic Forum's 1997 East Asia Economic Summit.
-- The 15th World Petroleum Congress is on its second day in Beijing, where delegates will map out the petroleum industry's "blueprint" for the next century. The five-day conference also marks China's full integration into global petroleum circles.
GERMANY -- The government's remaining stake in Lufthansa is to be floated.
THE NETHERLANDS -- The International Court of Justice opens, in The Hague, hearings on the dispute between Libya, Britain and the U.S. over the 1988 Pan Am-jet bombing above Lockerbie in Scotland.
NORWAY -- Prime Minister Thorbjoern Jagland and his minority Labor government, after presenting the state budget to Parliament, are to step down following disappointing results in last month's national elections.
U.K. -- The Wilton Park conference on "Key issues in preparing for economic and monetary union," takes place in West Sussex.
Tuesday, Oct. 14
BELGIUM -- The EU and the U.S. are to start two-day talks in Brussels, in a last-minute bid to meet the Oct. 15 deadline for resolving their dispute over the U.S.'s Helms-Burton act, which aims to punish non-U.S. companies that invest in property confiscated by Cuba.
CROATIA -- The country's loss-making state railway company HZ is expected to sign a landmark deal, regulating border-train traffic with neighboring Yugoslavia and to re-open two local lines after six years of war disruption.
CZECH REPUBLIC -- A ministerial conference on illegal migration is scheduled in Prague. (to Oct. 15)
FRANCE -- Labor ministers of the OECD countries will convene in Paris. (to Oct. 15)
GERMANY -- Finance Minister Theo Waigel and his French counterpart, Dominique Strauss-Kahn, are to hold talks in Muenster. The two countries' central- bank presidents, Hans Tietmeyer and Jean-Claude Trichet, are also to attend the regular meeting of a bilateral economic and financial council.
SWEDEN -- The Nobel prize for economics is to be announced in Stockholm.
U.K. -- The London Motor Show kicks off. (to Oct. 26)
Wednesday, Oct. 15
BELGIUM -- The COGEN Europe conference on "Decentralized cogeneration and the climate-change challenge," takes place in Brussels. (to Oct. 16)
ITALY -- A campaign to crack down on software piracy in the country is due to begin. Under the campaign, up to 10,000 companies throughout Italy could be subject to spot checks of their installed software in the battle to combat illegal programs.
MALAYSIA -- The Association of Southeast Asian Nations ministers will gather in Kuala Lumpur.
SWEDEN -- The Nobel physics and chemistry prize winners are to be named in Stockholm.
THAILAND -- The Finance Ministry i set to unveil a financial-restructuring plan.
Thursday, Oct. 16
AUSTRIA -- International Atomic Energy Agency Director-General Hans Blix will speak to the United Nations Security Council in Vienna, on the IAEA's report that Iraq could still be hiding some nuclear secrets because it has not provided a full picture of its atomic program.
DENMARK -- An international conference on "The Social Commitment of Enterprises," takes place in Copenhagen. (to Oct. 18)
FRANCE -- The European Parliament and the Commission Kangaroo Group will hold a conference on "The euro and the European innovative industry," in Toulouse. (to Oct. 18)
THE NETHERLANDS -- FIEJ, the World Association of Newspapers, opens "Beyond the Printed Word," a conference on electronic publishing, in Amsterdam. (to Oct. 17)
SWITZERLAND -- The Dispute Settlement Body of the World Trade Organization opens its doors in Geneva, to tackle trade disputes. The agenda will include the creation of a panel on the issue regarding South Korea's liquor tax laws that the EU and the U.S. argue violate open trading rules. The EU is also due to report on how it will implement a panel and appeals-board ruling, approved by the DSB last month, regarding the high-profile case over its banana import rules which were found to run against global trade accords.
Friday, Oct. 17
HUNGARY -- A conference on NATO enlargement opens in Budapest, with the foreign and defense ministers of Hungary, Poland and the Czech Republic.
POLAND -- The government of ex-communists and the Peasant Party is expected to step down and hand over its draft budget to a new cabinet of center-right parties which won parliamentary elections last month. At the same time, the Solidarity Election Action bloc is due to present its candidate for prime minister.
U.K. -- Barclays Bank PLC workers are scheduled to stage the first of two 24-hour strikes in protest of a new performance-linked pay system which they say will freeze wages for 25,000 workers.
SWITZERLAND -- WTO members will meet in Geneva to review progress in talks on a new agreement aimed at liberalizing financial services (banking, insurance, asset-management, etc.). The deadline for the talks is set for Dec. 12.

TALKING "BOUT THE WEATHER

Author: CHARLES J. HANLEY Associated Press

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/talking-bout-weather/docview/274230865/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Up in walrus country, unhappy Eskimo hunters say the frozen sea is breaking up early these days. An ocean away, in the balmy mid-Pacific, islanders report the tides are suddenly stealing away chunks of precious land. Things are going wrong out at the edge of the world, and people there blame global warming. But back in the world's capitals, where 1997 is the year for action on climate change, things are going slowly. Behind diplomacy's closed doors, negotiators are drafting a treaty to control carbon dioxide and other "greenhouse" emissions, gases many scientists say are warming the planet. Emissaries are to gather in Kyoto, Japan, in December to sign the unprecedented new commitments.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=TALKING+%22BOUT+THE+WEATHER&amp;author=CHARLES+J.+HANLEY+Associated+Press&amp;issn=10434313&amp;title=York+Daily+Record&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-12&amp;spage=01&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Up in walrus country, unhappy Eskimo hunters say the frozen sea is breaking up early these days. An ocean away, in the balmy mid-Pacific, islanders report the tides are suddenly stealing away chunks of precious land.
Things are going wrong out at the edge of the world, and people there blame global warming. But back in the world's capitals, where 1997 is the year for action on climate change, things are going slowly.
Behind diplomacy's closed doors, negotiators are drafting a treaty to control carbon dioxide and other "greenhouse" emissions, gases many scientists say are warming the planet. Emissaries are to gather in Kyoto, Japan, in December to sign the unprecedented new commitments.
After eons of talking about the weather, mankind may finally be ready to do something about it.
But as the weeks tick down and the debate heats up, the outcome looks less and less certain.
At preliminary sessions in Bonn, Germany, last month, the U.S. delegation once again took no detailed negotiating position. Reduction targets were not discussed. Those who favor decisive action grew discouraged.
"I am not very optimistic about Kyoto," Bert Bolin, a leading atmospheric scientist, said in a telephone interview from Stockholm, Sweden.
The planetary debate pits rich countries against poor, islands against continents, oil producers against conservationists.
It means many things to many people. American negotiator Tim Wirth calls climate change the top "environmental challenge" of our time. But U.S. industrialists describe the treaty negotiations as the century's most important "economic decision."
The environment vs. the economy: The fear of damage from a changing climate weighed against the fear of lost business and jobs from energy restraints.
In the end, Kyoto may produce an incomplete agreement, a basis for more talk and small steps forward, a process like the world trade negotiations, which covered almost a half-century.
And while diplomats duel, scientists work - atop glaciers, in air-monitoring stations, through satellite launches - to clear up nagging uncertainties still clouding climate change.
There is nothing uncertain about the underlying theory.
In 1898, as industry expanded in Europe and America, Swedish scientist Svante Arrhenius warned that carbon dioxide pouring into the atmosphere would trap more of the Earth's heat - like the glass of a greenhouse.
Carbon dioxide, methane, nitrous oxide and other gases that trap heat are transparent to incoming sunlight, but absorb the infrared radiation that Earth, maintaining its heat balance, emits back to space.
Fuel combustion - in engines powering everything from automobiles to electricity plants - is the biggest man-made source of carbon dioxide and nitrous oxide. Decomposition of organic waste is a major source of methane.
From 1800 to 1994, carbon dioxide in the atmosphere jumped from 280 parts per million to 358. And during the past century, the average global temperature rose by about 1 degree Fahrenheit.
Four of the five hottest years on record have occurred since 1990. Glaciers almost everywhere are in retreat, and oceans - which expand when warmed - have risen by 4 to 10 inches.
Reports arrive from across the globe of unusual seas and weather.
In Alaska, Charlie Johnson, a leader of Eskimo hunters, said the walrus harvest is down the past few years because the Arctic sea ice - the place where man meets walrus - breaks up earlier than usual each spring.
"The people in the villages and everybody else feels the ice is leaving earlier," Johnson said by telephone from Nome.
On the remote Pacific atoll of Nukuoro, in the Federated States of Micronesia, a rising sea has flooded the few acres where islanders can grow taro, their staple crop.
"If something's not done, Nukuoro is going to be abandoned," said John Mooteb, a Micronesia government official.
The sea can rise or fall for various reasons, oceanographers note. In the same cautious vein, scientists have long shied from flatly attributing the warming of recent decades to gas emissions. But in 1995 they crossed a threshold.
A U.N. report, produced with the help of more than 2,000 specialists worldwide, concluded that "the balance of evidence suggests a discernible human impact on Earth's climate system."
The scientific panel, led by Bolin, the Swedish climatologist, predicted emissions at current rates would raise global temperatures by an additional 2 to 6 degrees Fahrenheit by 2100 and sea levels by 6 inches to 3 feet, inundating islands and shorelines.
Weather would grow more turbulent, and climate zones might shift hundreds of miles toward the poles, abruptly making regions wetter, drier, warmer or colder.
Some areas might benefit - from crop-nurturing rains, for example. But climate surprises could be devastating: Mosquitoes might bring malaria to new areas, for example, or the northern permafrost might soften, buckling roads and buildings.
Some skeptics remain. Some say the link to greenhouse gases still is not shown beyond doubt. Some note the consensus projections have broad margins of error.
"The signal of warming is gradually getting stronger," said David A. Randall at Colorado State University, who is editor of the Journal of Climate. But although the past century's warming "is definitely very strong ... one century isn't a long time in this field."
One doubter, University of Virginia climatologist Pat Michaels, acknowledges man has affected the climate. "My point," he said, "is not whether human beings change the climate, but how much and how."
The mainstream majority supporting the U.N. report concedes that big questions remain.
For one thing, a warmer world will evaporate water more rapidly, forming more clouds in the atmosphere. Depending on how and where they form, they may either cool or further warm the surface.
Another question: Will the oceans, absorbing enough warmth quickly enough, hold down rising air temperatures? Answers would make projections more precise. But answers may not come until after the world begins feeling the worst effects of warming.
"Despite the uncertainty, the risk of a significant change of climate does not go away," Bolin said. He said political leaders must act on "risk analysis."
In 1992, they acted by postponing. A 165-nation treaty on climate change called for voluntary emissions cutbacks. That failed and in 1995 governments agreed to toughen the treaty by negotiating mandatory rollbacks by industrial nations.
Island states proposed that by 2005, emissions be cut by 20 percent below 1990 levels. The European Union favors a 15 percent reduction by 2010.
Such proposals don't prescribe how to do it, but studies suggest a range of ways: switching from coal and oil to nuclear or solar energy; developing more fuel-efficient engines; ending subsidies and imposing heavier taxes on carbon fuels; saving forests, which consume carbon dioxide.
President Clinton has pledged a "strong American commitment to realistic and binding limits." But the United States, responsible for one-quarter of global emissions, has yet to commit to specific targets and timetables.
American business lobbyists contend emissions reductions could wipe out hundreds of thousands of jobs in the coal, oil, chemical and other industries - in part because companies would move operations to developing countries. And the U.S. Senate declared, by a 95-0 vote, it will not ratify a treaty that does not also require emission cutbacks in the developing world.
Behind the scenes, the Clinton administration is readying a proposal expected to bring developing countries under the treaty in some new way and to introduce emissions-quota "trading" and other devices making compliance more flexible.
To build U.S. public support, Clinton has scheduled a White House conference on climate change for early October. But the slow movement in Washington still worries others.
"We should take care that what the U.S. Congress has proposed is not going to stalemate the rest of the negotiations," said Robert Wester, spokesman for the Dutch Environment Ministry.
Washington is not alone. Japan, Australia and some other nations, under pressure from local industry, also are reluctant partners in the talks, whose complexity is producing unlikely alliances.
British Petroleum, for example, a big player in solar energy, has broken ranks with other oil producers to stand with environmentalists. And insurance companies, fearing mounting damage claims from higher sea levels, floods, storms and other severe weather, support quick action against climate change.
"A lot is going on behind the business lobbying," said Michael Zammit Cutajar, the Maltese official who is executive secretary for the negotiations.
With the treaty in mind, industry is working hard on energy-saving technologies, he said. "Those who get there first with a low-emission car engine, for example, stand to make a lot of money."
He believes Kyoto may produce an immediate accord on cutback targets - most think no more than 10 percent - and agreement in principle in other areas. Those might include coverage of developing countries and market-based compliance systems.
Then the talks would continue, round for round, like the long-running trade negotiations, Zammit Cutajar said.
"Kyoto is another step on a long road."

Money matters: Excuse me, sir but I think your slip is showing Subsidence claims are soaring and insurance premiums could follow, reports NEASA MacERLEAN

Author: NEASA MACERLEAN

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/money-matters-excuse-me-sir-i-think-your-slip-is/docview/250440961/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: HOMEOWNERS in areas prone to subsidence may face higher insurance premiums after a surge in claims. Norwich Union predicts that 1997 will set a new record for claims - up more than 15 per cent in value from last year. Commercial Union expects an increase this year of between 30 and 50 per cent - 'and there is no indication this will be tailing off'. Figures from the Association of British Insurers suggest claims are 'on an upward trend' - totalling pounds 170 million from more than 21,000 householders in the first half of the year. Lack of moisture is a prime ingredient in most subsidence. The Norwich Union view is supported by subsidence expert Dr Mike Crilly at the Building Research Establishment: 'If the dryness of the last three years carries on, we will see claims running at the same fairly high level. But I don't think that climate change happens that fast.'

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Money+matters%3A+Excuse+me%2C+sir+but+I+think+your+slip+is+showing+Subsidence+claims+are+soaring+and+insurance+premiums+could+follow%2C+reports+NEASA+MacERLEAN&amp;author=NEASA+MACERLEAN&amp;issn=00297712&amp;title=The+Observer&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-12&amp;spage=022&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   HOMEOWNERS in areas prone to subsidence may face higher insurance premiums after a surge in claims.
Norwich Union predicts that 1997 will set a new record for claims - up more than 15 per cent in value from last year. Commercial Union expects an increase this year of between 30 and 50 per cent - 'and there is no indication this will be tailing off'. Figures from the Association of British Insurers suggest claims are 'on an upward trend' - totalling pounds 170 million from more than 21,000 householders in the first half of the year.
These statistics are being interpreted in two ways. Norwich Union does not believe that premiums will have to rise. It says claims will fall again in the next few years. This view is based on an assumption that the climate has not permanently and suddenly become warmer.
Lack of moisture is a prime ingredient in most subsidence. The Norwich Union view is supported by subsidence expert Dr Mike Crilly at the Building Research Establishment: 'If the dryness of the last three years carries on, we will see claims running at the same fairly high level. But I don't think that climate change happens that fast.'
Others are more pessimistic. Chris Jordan of the Subsidence Claims Advisory Bureau in Bexhill says: 'There is a feeling that insurance rates will have to go up - probably early next year.'
Some companies have been marketing themselves particularly to people in low subsidence areas. This could mean higher premiums eventually for homes that are not 'cherry-picked' in this way.
The process of claiming may be easier after a new insurance agreement that came into place at the start of this month. The domestic subsidence tree root claims agreement, arranged by the Association of British Insurers, simplifies procedures if subsidence is caused by a tree in a neighbour's garden. This will particularly help homeowners in London and other areas of dense population, where tree roots frequently cross garden borders.
In the past, companies would sometimes spend months or years trying to establish whose tree was to blame for subsidence. If the tree was in the next garden, the insurer for the next door property would pick up the bill. Under the new agreement, there will be no discussion about blame in most cases: the insurer of the home where the damage is incurredwill be liable. The ABI says companies representing 'most of household business' are now signatories.
ON the other hand, more responsibility will be placed on homeowners to stop their trees causing damage. 'The owner of a neighbouring tree could be put under notice that their tree is likely to cause damage,' says Rob Hooker, surveyor at the Subsidence Claims Advisory Bureau. If the tree was not pruned back, the neighbours would no longer be covered by the tree root claims agreement and their insurance may be invalidated.
Many homeowners will need to take professional advice before they start pruning. Dramatic cuts can also cause damage - a swelling in the ground, known as 'heave', if there is suddenly an unexpected amount of moisture in the earth. If subsidence remains a problem, insurers are likely to launch campaigns to tell people how to manage their gardens. About 79 per cent of subsidence claims are caused by trees or shrubs, says the Building Research Establishment.
The recovery in the housing market is another factor in the increase in claims. People whose homes are inspected by surveyors sometimes find they have problems they were unaware of. This also applies to homeowners who remortgage.
Research by AA Insurance suggests that the areas most prone to subsidence include parts of Nottingham, Chelmsford, Portsmouth and London.

Title: Money matters: Excuse me, sir but I think your slip is showing Subsidence claims are soaring and insurance premiums could follow, reports NEASA MacERLEAN

Publication title: The Observer; London (UK)

Pages: 022

Number of pages: 0

Publication year: 1997

Publication date: Oct 12, 1997

Section: The Observer Business Page

Place of publication: London (UK)

Whom to believe on global warming: [METRO Edition]

Author: Ivins, Molly

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/whom-believe-on-global-warming/docview/426905781/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: I just spent a week in the crystalline air of the high desert of Northern Mexico - well, crystalline except for the cloud of black gunk that rose every day from a small nearby city and crept toward the mountains. Beloved Mexico is not a country where the muffler gets a lot of respect. I returned to the hill country of central Texas - land of blue skies and swift, green rivers - only to find the joint covered with gray smaze and under a steady ozone alert. Ozone alert is what makes your lungs labor like bellows and your head ache when you go out in it. I bring this up because it is part of the backdrop for a curious "debate" now going on. "It's only in America that there is a debate over what's happening to the global climate," observed a German journalist quoted in Ross Gelbspan's new book, "The Heat Is On." In Europe, "the only debates are how fast and with what impacts the changes will happen."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Whom+to+believe+on+global+warming&amp;author=Ivins%2C+Molly&amp;issn=08952825&amp;title=Star+Tribune&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-09&amp;spage=23.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Lungs have been on my mind lately, partly because I'm in the midst of my 2,917th attempt to quit smoking.
I just spent a week in the crystalline air of the high desert of Northern Mexico - well, crystalline except for the cloud of black gunk that rose every day from a small nearby city and crept toward the mountains. Beloved Mexico is not a country where the muffler gets a lot of respect.
I returned to the hill country of central Texas - land of blue skies and swift, green rivers - only to find the joint covered with gray smaze and under a steady ozone alert. Ozone alert is what makes your lungs labor like bellows and your head ache when you go out in it.
I bring this up because it is part of the backdrop for a curious "debate" now going on. "It's only in America that there is a debate over what's happening to the global climate," observed a German journalist quoted in Ross Gelbspan's new book, "The Heat Is On." In Europe, "the only debates are how fast and with what impacts the changes will happen."
Actually, there's not a debate - in the sense of opposing arguments each backed up with facts - in this country, either. What we have is scientific opinion, which is clear and close to unanimous, and a multimillion-dollar advertising campaign sponsored by corporate interests afraid that the scientists' findings will cost them money.
It's as though your doctor found a suspicious lump on you and did a biopsy and the test proved it malignant. But then your insurance agent, worried that his company would have to foot the bills, said confidently: "Oh, no, that's not cancer - that lump is just a spider bite." Would you listen to the insurance agent? Of course not. But the media in this country treat both arguments as though they were of equal weight and soundness because, you see, we have been trained to assume there are two sides to every story.
What you want to keep in mind as you listen to the escalating "debate" about global warming is that the scientists have no financial stake in their findings. There's nothing in it for them; they're just reporting what they find. Nor do the environmentalists, who would prefer to spend their time saving whales, baby critters and the remaining wilderness if they weren't convinced that global warming is going to get us all first.
The book by Gelbspan, who has already won a Pulitzer for his journalism, details the propaganda campaign sponsored by oil and coal companies and OPEC governments, all with a vested interest in the continued burning of fossil fuels. In this country, the National Association of Manufacturers, automobile manufacturers and the Farm Bureau, always a foe of environmentalists, have joined to fund a $13 million advertising campaign to pooh-pooh the notion of global warming and/or wildly exaggerate the economic consequences of doing anything about it. Until last week, when Ted Turner put the kibosh on it, their ads were blanketing CNN.
The 2,600 scientists worldwide who signed the report of the U.N. Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change in 1995 agree on what is happening to the global climate, but their predictions as to the consequences vary with the computer models they use, ranging from quite serious to absolutely disastrous. The prospect of island nations sinking under the waves and coastal cities being inundated by the middle of the next century if nothing is done about global warming is the sort of nightmare scenario that seems like apocalyptic science fiction, and so lends itself beautifully to that standard human response, denial: Let's not think about it - let's watch the football game instead. But the insurance industry, which has its own obvious stake in the effects of global warming, along with a few progressive industry leaders such as the CEO of British Petroleum, are taking the effects of global warming quite seriously indeed.
The media, with our eternal tendency toward on-the-one-hand-this/on-the-other-hand-that, equal-time, fair-play doctrines, are doing a real disservice to the public. What we need is the kind of reporting that Gelbspan has done, showing the collusion of special interests against the public interest in perhaps the brassiest display of greed, lying and self-interest since the time all those tobacco executives solemnly swore to Congress that there was nothing addictive about nicotine. The depth of their irresponsibility, of their chutzpah, is just astounding - how dare they put their own short-term profits ahead of the lives of millions of people? Talk about no shame.
As if the battle of scientists against propagandists weren't bad enough, the whole issue now moves into the political realm, where you can be assured that big campaign contributions will have their effect. President Clinton, in an almost comically Clintonesque fashion, is waging a campaign to raise public consciousness on the issue while simultaneously dithering about how to negotiate the binding emissions caps at the December meeting in Kyoto, Japan.
The big problem there is the developed-vs.-developing nations, or the North-South dilemma. The industrialized countries contribute by far the most greenhouse gases to the atmosphere, but the developing nations are afraid that their fragile economies will be crushed if they are put under the same constraints as the big guys.
One optimistic school of thought on all this is that technology will cure the problems technology has created - that we can go to solar-powered cars, renewable energy sources, etc. But such new technologies depend on whether huge amounts of investment capital are put into them, and as we see, the big money is already sticking with the status quo. This reminds me of the great game plan: "Stupidity got us into this mess, and stupidity will get us out of it."
- Molly Ivins is a columnist for the Fort Worth (Texas) Star-Telegram.
Illustration CARTOON
Credit: Creators Syndicate

Big Business Warps U.S. Debate About Global Warming Policies

Author: MOLLY IVINS CREATORS SYNDICATE

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/big-business-warps-u-s-debate-about-global/docview/288792966/se-2?accountid=10226

Full text:   AUSTIN -- I just spent a week in the crystalline air of the high desert of Northern Mexico -- well, crystalline except for the cloud of black gunk that rose every day from a small nearby city and crept toward the mountains.

Happiness Is a Warm Planet

Author: By Thomas Gale Moore

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/happiness-is-warm-planet/docview/308300476/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: President Clinton convened a conference on global warming Monday, as the White House agonizes over its posture at the forthcoming talks in Kyoto, Japan, on a world-wide global warming treaty. Mr. Clinton is eager to please his environmentalist supporters, but industry, labor and members of the U.S. Senate have told the administration that this treaty would wreck the U.S. economy, cost millions of jobs and provoke a flight of investment to more hospitable climes. If mankind had to choose between a warmer or a cooler climate, we would certainly choose the former: Humans, nearly all other animals and most plants would be better off with higher temperatures. The climate models suggest, and so far the record confirms, that under global warming nighttime winter temperatures would rise the most, and daytime summer temperatures the least. Most Americans prefer a warmer climate to a colder one -- and that preference is justified. More people die of the cold than of the heat; more die in the winter than the summer. Statistical evidence suggests that the climate predicted for the end of the next century might reduce U.S. deaths by about 40,000 annually.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Happiness+Is+a+Warm+Planet&amp;author=By+Thomas+Gale+Moore&amp;issn=09219986&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-08&amp;spage=6&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: President Clinton convened a conference on global warming Monday, as the White House agonizes over its posture at the forthcoming talks in Kyoto, Japan, on a world-wide global warming treaty. Mr. Clinton is eager to please his environmentalist supporters, but industry, labor and members of the U.S. Senate have told the administration that this treaty would wreck the U.S. economy, cost millions of jobs and provoke a flight of investment to more hospitable climes.
A crucial point gets lost in the debate: Global warming, if it were to occur, would probably benefit most Americans.
If mankind had to choose between a warmer or a cooler climate, we would certainly choose the former: Humans, nearly all other animals and most plants would be better off with higher temperatures. The climate models suggest, and so far the record confirms, that under global warming nighttime winter temperatures would rise the most, and daytime summer temperatures the least. Most Americans prefer a warmer climate to a colder one -- and that preference is justified. More people die of the cold than of the heat; more die in the winter than the summer. Statistical evidence suggests that the climate predicted for the end of the next century might reduce U.S. deaths by about 40,000 annually.
In addition, less snow and ice would reduce transportation delays and accidents. A warmer winter would cut heating costs, more than offsetting any increase in air conditioning expenses in the summer. Manufacturing, mining and most services would be unaffected. Longer growing seasons, more rainfall and higher concentrations of carbon dioxide would benefit plant growth. Already there is evidence that trees and other plants are growing more vigorously. Although some locales may become too dry, too wet or too warm, on the whole mankind should benefit from an upward tick in the thermometer.
What about the economic effects? In the pessimistic view of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, the costs of global warming might be as high as 1.5% of the U.S. gross domestic product by the end of the next century. The cost of reducing carbon dioxide emissions, however, would be much higher. William Cline of the Institute for International Economics has calculated that the cost of cutting emissions by one-third from current levels by 2040 would be 3.5% of world-wide GDP. The IPCC also reviewed various estimates of losses from stabilizing emissions at 1990 levels, a more modest objective, and concluded that the cost to the U.S. economy would be at least 1.5% of GDP by 2050, with the burden continuing to increase thereafter.
The forecast cost of warming is for the end of the next century, not the middle. Adjusting for the time difference, the cost to the U.S. from a warmer climate at midcentury, according to the IPCC, would be at most 0.75% of GDP, meaning that the costs of holding carbon dioxide to 1990 levels would be twice the gain from preventing any climate change. But the benefit-cost calculus is even worse. The Clinton administration is planning to exempt Third World nations, such as China, India and Brazil, from the requirements of the treaty. Under such a scheme, Americans would pay a huge price for virtually no benefit.
And even if the developing countries agreed to return emissions to 1990 levels, greenhouse gas concentrations would not be stabilized. Since for many decades more carbon dioxide would be added to the atmosphere than removed through natural processes, the buildup would only slow; consequently temperatures would continue to go up. Instead of saving the full 0.75% of GDP by keeping emissions at 1990 levels, the U.S. would be saving much less.
It is true that whatever dangers global warming may pose, they will be most pronounced in the developing world. It is much easier for rich countries to adapt to any long-term shift in weather than it is for poor countries, which tend to be much more dependent on agriculture. Poor countries lack the resources to aid their flora and fauna in adapting, and many of their farmers earn too little to survive a shift to new conditions. But the best insurance for these poor countries is an increase in their wealth, which would diminish their dependence on agriculture and make it easier for them to adjust to changes in weather, including increases in precipitation and possible flooding or higher sea level. Subjecting U.S. citizens to high taxes and onerous regulations will help neither them -- the U.S. could buy less from them -- nor Americans.
The optimal way to deal with potential climate change is not to embark on a futile attempt to prevent it, but to promote growth and prosperity so that people will have the resources to deal with the normal set of natural disasters. Based on the evidence, including historical records, global warming is likely to be good for most of mankind. The additional carbon, rain and warmth should promote the plant growth necessary to sustain an expanding world population. Global change is inevitable; warmer is better; richer is healthier.
---
Mr. Moore is a senior fellow at the Hoover Institution. His book "A Politically Incorrect View of Global Warming: Foreign Aid Masquerading as Climate Policy" will be published next year by the Cato Institute.

Subject: Gross Domestic Product--GDP; Developing countries--LDCs; Costs; Global warming; Emissions; Environmental policy; Greenhouse effect; Climate change; Rain; Carbon dioxide; Plant growth; Emission standards

Happiness Is a Warm Planet

Author: By Thomas Gale Moore

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/happiness-is-warm-planet/docview/398814575/se-2?accountid=10226

Full text: President Clinton convened a conference on global warming yesterday, as the White House agonizes over its posture at the forthcoming talks in Kyoto, Japan, on a world-wide global warming treaty. Leaders seek to fulfil climate convention: [Life and Times, , 2* Edition]

Author: Singh, Gurmit

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/leaders-seek-fulfil-climate-convention/docview/269164562/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: IN the last column, we discussed the forces that challenge the implementation of the United Nations Climate Convention (UNFCCC) and how they were going to extremes to prevent the adoption of any meaningful protocol in Kyoto, Japan, in December. Government leaders have banded together under the AOSIS (Alliance Of Small Island States) banner and they were the first to table a protocol proposal at the Berlin Meeting of the UNFCCC. Among the other developing countries, Malaysia, the Philippines and Thailand have supported a strong protocol. A number of African countries, led by Zimbabwe, recently pushed for concrete action to limit greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Leaders+seek+to+fulfil+climate+convention&amp;author=Singh%2C+Gurmit&amp;issn=&amp;title=New+Straits+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-10-07&amp;spage=06&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   IN the last column, we discussed the forces that challenge the implementation of the United Nations Climate Convention (UNFCCC) and how they were going to extremes to prevent the adoption of any meaningful protocol in Kyoto, Japan, in December.
Let us now examine these so-called "good guys" who take action to curb global warming.
Government leaders have banded together under the AOSIS (Alliance Of Small Island States) banner and they were the first to table a protocol proposal at the Berlin Meeting of the UNFCCC.
The AOSIS proposal is still the most substantive one on the negotiating table - calling for 20 per cent reduction of carbon monoxide emissions below 1990 levels by all developed countries by the year 2005. But not all of them are enthusiastic - Singapore has been one notable exception, ratifying the convention just recently.
Among the other developing countries, Malaysia, the Philippines and Thailand have supported a strong protocol. A number of African countries, led by Zimbabwe, recently pushed for concrete action to limit greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions.
However, Asean, China and India have not come out to support the AOSIS proposal.
Within developed countries, Denmark has been the most progressive in taking action within its boundaries to reduce GHG emissions while pushing the EU to table a useful protocol.
The EU proposal, although considerably weaker than that from AOSIS, is still the strongest that has emerged from countries in the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development.
After years of uncertainty and debate, the majority of the world's scientists, represented in the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), stated in 1995 that there was evidence of global warming as a result of gases being emitted by human activity.
The IPCC now favours quick action to curb global warming.
Among businesses, the renewable energy industries, including those dealing with gas and energy conservation, have over the last two years supported reduction action among developed nations.
The insurance industry also opposes global climate change, which is costing the industry dearly in terms of insurance claims arising from floods and storms.
The International Gogeneration Alliance has also been on the good side as their activities reduce the consumption of fossil fuels (and hence GHGH emissions).
Local authorities grouped under the International Council of Local Environmental Initiatives (ICLEI) have also been urging positive action as their members demonstrate various initiatives across the world to reduce GHG emissions.
Environmental groups, especially those organised around the global and informal Climate Action Network (Can), have been pushing for quick and meaningful actions to reduce GHG emissions.
But within Can there are shades of emphasis with Can Southeast Asia (Cansea) and Climate Network Europe pushing for the strongest possible targets.
Last year, Japanese NGOs organised themselves under an umbrella group (Kiko Forum) and urged the Japanese Government to provide effective leadership in Kyoto so that the resulting protocol makes a real dent against global warming.
In the United States, NGOs like Ozone Action and the Union of Concerned Scientists have countered misleading media stories and political campaigns that are being waged by the "forces of darkness".
The Sierra Club in Canada has been leading efforts to improve the Canadian Government's position while international NGOs like Greenpeace and WWF International have been working with Can to push the negotiations and action forward.
Malaysian environmental groups that are part of Cansea are CETDEM, EPSM and MNS. They watch developments in Malaysia while working as part of Cansea to ensure forceful lobbying for a useful Kyoto protocol.
