Global Warming Talks Go Slowly as Apparent Effects Speed Up; Environment: Negotiators are drafting treaty to control 'greenhouse' emissions. But fears about economic effects are slowing progress.: [Bulldog Edition]

Author: Hanley, Charles J

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-talks-go-slowly-as-apparent/docview/421226940/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Up in walrus country, unhappy Eskimo hunters say the frozen sea is breaking up early these days. An ocean away, in the balmy mid-Pacific, islanders report the tides are suddenly stealing away chunks of precious land. Things are going wrong out at the edge of the world, and people there blame global warming. But back in the world's capitals, where 1997 is the year for action on climate change, things are going slowly. Behind diplomacy's closed doors, negotiators are drafting a treaty to control carbon dioxide and other "greenhouse" emissions, gases many scientists say are warming the planet. Emissaries are to gather in Kyoto, Japan, in December to sign the unprecedented new commitments.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Global+Warming+Talks+Go+Slowly+as+Apparent+Effects+Speed+Up%3B+Environment%3A+Negotiators+are+drafting+treaty+to+control+%27greenhouse%27+emissions.+But+fears+about+economic+effects+are+slowing+progress.&amp;author=Hanley%2C+Charles+J&amp;issn=04583035&amp;title=Los+Angeles+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-09-28&amp;spage=2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Up in walrus country, unhappy Eskimo hunters say the frozen sea is breaking up early these days. An ocean away, in the balmy mid-Pacific, islanders report the tides are suddenly stealing away chunks of precious land.
Things are going wrong out at the edge of the world, and people there blame global warming. But back in the world's capitals, where 1997 is the year for action on climate change, things are going slowly.
Behind diplomacy's closed doors, negotiators are drafting a treaty to control carbon dioxide and other "greenhouse" emissions, gases many scientists say are warming the planet. Emissaries are to gather in Kyoto, Japan, in December to sign the unprecedented new commitments.
After eons of talking about the weather, mankind may finally be ready to do something about it.
But as the weeks tick down and the debate heats up, the outcome looks less and less certain.
At preliminary sessions in Bonn, Germany, in August, the U.S. delegation once again took no detailed negotiating position. Reduction targets were not discussed. Those who favor decisive action grew discouraged.
"I am not very optimistic about Kyoto," Bert Bolin, a leading atmospheric scientist, said from Stockholm.
The planetary debate pits rich countries against poor, islands against continents, oil producers against conservationists.
It means many things to many people. American negotiator Tim Wirth calls climate change the top "environmental challenge" of our time. But U.S. industrialists describe the treaty negotiations as the century's most important "economic decision."
The environment versus the economy: The fear of damage from a changing climate weighed against the fear of lost business and jobs from energy restraints.
In the end, Kyoto may produce an incomplete agreement, a basis for more talk and small steps forward, a process like the world trade negotiations, which covered almost half a century.
And while diplomats duel, scientists work--atop glaciers, in air-monitoring stations, through satellite launches--to clear up nagging uncertainties still clouding climate change.
There is nothing uncertain about the underlying theory.
In 1898, as industry expanded in Europe and America, Swedish scientist Svante Arrhenius warned that carbon dioxide pouring into the atmosphere would trap more of the Earth's heat--like the glass of a greenhouse.
Carbon dioxide, methane, nitrous oxide and other gases that trap heat are transparent to incoming sunlight, but absorb the infrared radiation that Earth, maintaining its heat balance, emits back to space.
Fuel combustion--in engines powering everything from automobiles to electric plants--is the biggest man-made source of carbon dioxide and nitrous oxide. Decomposition of organic waste is a major source of methane.
From 1800 to 1994, carbon dioxide in the atmosphere jumped from 280 parts per million to 358. And during the last century, the average global temperature rose by about 1 degree Fahrenheit.
Four of the five hottest years on record have occurred since 1990. Glaciers almost everywhere are in retreat, and oceans--which expand when warmed--have risen by 4 to 10 inches.
Reports arrive from across the globe of unusual seas and weather.
In Alaska, Charlie Johnson, a leader of Eskimo hunters, said the walrus harvest is down the last few years because the Arctic sea ice--the place where man meets walrus--breaks up earlier than usual each spring.
"The people in the villages and everybody else feels the ice is leaving earlier," Johnson said by telephone from Nome.
On the remote Pacific atoll of Nukuoro, in the Federated States of Micronesia, a rising sea has flooded the few acres where islanders can grow taro, their staple crop.
"If something's not done, Nukuoro is going to be abandoned," said John Mooteb, a Micronesia government official.
The sea can rise or fall for various reasons, oceanographers note. In the same cautious vein, scientists have long shied from flatly attributing the warming of recent decades to gas emissions. But in 1995 they crossed a threshold.
A U.N. report, produced with the help of more than 2,000 specialists worldwide, concluded that "the balance of evidence suggests a discernible human impact on Earth's climate system."
The scientific panel, led by Bolin, the Swedish climatologist, predicted emissions at current rates would raise global temperatures by an additional 2 to 6 degrees Fahrenheit by 2100 and sea levels by 6 inches to 3 feet, inundating islands and shorelines.
Weather would grow more turbulent and climate zones might shift hundreds of miles toward the poles, abruptly making regions wetter, drier, warmer or colder.
Some areas might benefit--from crop-nurturing rains, for example. But climate surprises could be devastating: Mosquitoes might bring malaria to new areas, for example, or the northern permafrost might soften, buckling roads and buildings.
Skeptics remain. Some say the link to greenhouse gases still is not shown beyond doubt. Some note the consensus projections have broad margins of error.
"The signal of warming is gradually getting stronger," said David A. Randall at Colorado State University, who is editor of the Journal of Climate. But although the last century's warming "is definitely very strong . . . one century isn't a long time in this field."
One doubter, University of Virginia climatologist Pat Michaels, acknowledges that man has affected the climate. "My point," he said, "is not whether human beings change the climate, but how much and how."
The mainstream majority supporting the U.N. report concedes that big questions remain.
For one thing, a warmer world will evaporate water more rapidly, forming more clouds in the atmosphere. Depending on how and where they form, they may either cool or further warm the surface.
Another question: Will the oceans, absorbing enough warmth quickly enough, hold down rising air temperatures?
Answers would make projections more precise. But answers may not come until after the world begins feeling the worst effects of warming.
"Despite the uncertainty, the risk of a significant change of climate does not go away," Bolin said. He said political leaders must act on "risk analysis."
In 1992, they acted by postponing. A 165-nation treaty on climate change called for voluntary emissions cutbacks. That failed, and in 1995 governments agreed to toughen the treaty by negotiating mandatory rollbacks by industrial nations.
Island states proposed that by 2005, emissions be cut by 20% below 1990 levels. The European Union favors a 15% reduction by 2010.
Such proposals don't prescribe how to do it, but studies suggest a range of ways: switching from coal and oil to nuclear or solar energy; developing more fuel-efficient engines; ending subsidies and imposing heavier taxes on carbon fuels; saving forests, which consume carbon dioxide.
President Clinton has pledged a "strong American commitment to realistic and binding limits." But the United States, responsible for one-quarter of global emissions, has yet to commit to specific targets and timetables.
American business lobbyists contend that emissions reductions could wipe out hundreds of thousands of jobs in the coal, oil, chemical and other industries--in part because companies would move operations to developing countries. And the U.S. Senate declared, by a 95 to 0 vote, that it will not ratify a treaty that does not also require emission cutbacks in the developing world.
Behind the scenes, the Clinton administration is readying a proposal expected to bring developing countries under the treaty in some new way and to introduce emissions-quota "trading" and other devices making compliance more flexible.
To build U.S. public support, Clinton has scheduled a White House conference on climate change for early October. But the slow movement in Washington still worries others.
"We should take care that what the U.S. Congress has proposed is not going to stalemate the rest of the negotiations," said Robert Wester, a spokesman for the Dutch Environment Ministry.
Washington is not alone. Japan, Australia and some other nations, under pressure from local industry, also are reluctant partners in the talks, whose complexity is producing unlikely alliances.
British Petroleum, for example, a big player in solar energy, has broken ranks with other oil producers to stand with environmentalists. And insurance companies, fearing mounting damage claims from higher sea levels, floods, storms and other severe weather, support quick action against climate change.
"A lot is going on behind the business lobbying," said Michael Zammit Cutajar, the Maltese official who is executive secretary for the negotiations.
With the treaty in mind, industry is working hard on energy-saving technologies, he said. "Those who get there first with a low-emission car engine, for example, stand to make a lot of money."
He believes Kyoto may produce an immediate accord on cutback targets--most think no more than 10%--and agreement in principle in other areas. Those might include coverage of developing countries and market-based compliance systems.
Then the talks would continue, round for round, like the long-running trade negotiations, Zammit Cutajar said.
"Kyoto is another step on a long road."
PHOTO: Los Angeles' heavy traffic contributes to global warming, scientists say, which threatens to inundate island nation of The Maldives. At right, concrete blocks are used in an attempt to halt beach erosion on Male.; PHOTOGRAPHER: B.K. BANGASH / Associated Press
Credit: ASSOCIATED PRESS

Browne extols virtues of turning green: Leyla Boulton meets the oil industry chief trying to dodge accusations of hypocrisy: [London edition]

Author: Boulton, Leyla

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/browne-extols-virtues-turning-green-leyla-boulton/docview/248578639/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: John Browne "is not a prophet, just a rational man," says a colleague of the chief executive of British Petroleum who has taken a lead in accepting climate change as a threat to his industry. Indeed, Mr Browne seems approachably ordinary in asking a journalist to help him move a sofa at the start of an interview. But in spite of making statements that are ground-breaking for an oil chief, Mr Browne is keen to reject the label of "green maverick" applied by some commentators to his company.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Browne+extols+virtues+of+turning+green%3A+Leyla+Boulton+meets+the+oil+industry+chief+trying+to+dodge+accusations+of+hypocrisy%3A&amp;author=Boulton%2C+Leyla&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-09-24&amp;spage=22&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: John Browne "is not a prophet, just a rational man," says a colleague of the chief executive of British Petroleum who has taken a lead in accepting climate change as a threat to his industry.
Indeed, Mr Browne seems approachably ordinary in asking a journalist to help him move a sofa at the start of an interview.
He then sets out his view - ahead of a speech to German parliamentarians next Tuesday - on the need for "continuous steps" to tackle climate change, one cause of which may be fossil fuel consumption.
Most of his industry peers prefer to cite the scientific uncertainty still surrounding climate change as a reason for inaction.
"There could be a conclusion in 20 years' time that none of that [what society did] made any difference," says Mr Browne. "One might equally say that one did not do enough."
But in spite of making statements that are ground-breaking for an oil chief, Mr Browne is keen to reject the label of "green maverick" applied by some commentators to his company.
"The words are very bad for business," says Mr Browne. "We talk about health, safety, and environment before we talk about financial performance and operating performance."
With much of its business done in joint ventures, BP knows it has a "lot in common with other oil companies on how we do things on the ground" and that includes minimising damage to the environment.
Having said that, Mr Browne concedes that "not all oil companies are the same". BP has spent more money than other oil companies to build market share in emerging solar energy technology for example.
He says that the $160m (£99m) spent by BP on developing its solar energy business "over the past medium-term" is neither "chicken feed" nor "tinkering". The group, which had turnover of about $70bn last year, aims to take solar panel sales to $1bn over the next decade from $100m now.
"We happen to believe that solar will grow," says Mr Browne. "Our position is that the energy mix in 50 years will . . . beradically different from today."
Although demand for oil and gas will also continue to expand, Mr Browne has some practical advice for governments seeking to agree "precautionary" action to curb climate change gases at international negotiations in Kyoto this December.
Mr Browne points out that governments should aim for "low hanging fruit" rather than agree unpopular discriminatory taxes on one group of society, say motorists, which will fail.
He is calling for an evenly distributed tax on energy waste and is backing plans on the table at Kyoto to enable developed countries to transfer energy-efficient technology to developing countries.
Despite a recent dinner with Lord Melchett, the executive director of Greenpeace, after its activists scaled BP oil installations in the Atlantic this summer, Mr Browne says he is not trying to please environmentalist groups.
But he makes no secret of the fact that the company's goal is to be at one with "our customers" in a fiercely competitive retail market.
Most fund managers say the environment is only one of several issues on which they judge the company. But one institutional investor was sufficiently impressed by BP's handling of this summer's protests by Greenpeace to contrast it favourably with Shell .
The investor was referring to Shell's response to protests against its attempts to dump the Brent Spar oil rig at sea two years ago .
The investor declined to be identified but his sentiments were shared by Carlos Joly, vice-president of Storebrand , the Norwegian insurance company, which has set up a fund to invest in companies that minimise harm to the environment. "All other things being equal, I would choose to invest in BP rather than Shell," says Mr Joly. For the moment, however, Storebrand is awaiting more information from Shell and BP.
This leads to another reason for BP's reluctance to explicitly proclaim its "green" virtue. This is to avoid accusations of hypocrisy lobbed at companies which wear concern for the environment or ethics on their sleeve.
ITV television showed a documentary renewing allegations of BP's double standards in Colombia this week.
But while rejecting most of the criticisms as "not new", Mr Browne says the company is pursuing continuous improvement in this area like any other. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1997. All Rights Reserved.

Canada gets a severe weather warning: [Final Edition]

Author: By Brian McAndrew Toronto Star

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/canada-gets-severe-weather-warning/docview/437730242/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Canada is in for more floods along with prolonged droughts, frequent tornadoes, severe heat waves and fewer deep freezes, the country's environment ministers were told yesterday. A lone insurance company executive delivered the bad news to them, including new federal Environment Minister Christine Stewart attending her first meeting of the Canadian Council of Ministers of the Environment. ``On the climate change front we are seeing tremendous changes in the frequency and severity of extreme weather activity in Canada,'' Sorema Management president Angus Ross told the closed-door meeting in Toronto, according to a copy of his presentation obtained by The Star.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Canada+gets+a+severe+weather+warning&amp;author=By+Brian+McAndrew+Toronto+Star&amp;issn=03190781&amp;title=Toronto+Star&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-09-20&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Canada is in for more floods along with prolonged droughts, frequent tornadoes, severe heat waves and fewer deep freezes, the country's environment ministers were told yesterday.
But it wasn't just government scientists giving that message to a ministers' ``learning session'' in Toronto on the anticipated effects of global warming that is projected to reach its peak by 2050.
A lone insurance company executive delivered the bad news to them, including new federal Environment Minister Christine Stewart attending her first meeting of the Canadian Council of Ministers of the Environment.
``On the climate change front we are seeing tremendous changes in the frequency and severity of extreme weather activity in Canada,'' Sorema Management president Angus Ross told the closed-door meeting in Toronto, according to a copy of his presentation obtained by The Star.
The insurance industry is taking a lead in ringing alarm bells over the potential disastrous impact of climate change if massive reductions of greenhouse gas emissions are not achieved.
Ross provided the meeting with a list of seven large insurance and reinsurance companies put out of business because of huge claims based on an increasing number of natural disasters world-wide.
``All ran into financial difficulties because of environmental claims or major weather losses and I could add another 200 or so (companies) to this list,'' Ross said.
Insurance companies believe increasing claims from natural disasters are caused by the influence of global warming that is projected to reach a peak of climate-related disasters by 2050 when carbon dioxide greenhouse gas is expected to be double its current level in the atmosphere.
The day-long meeting was to prepare the ministers for a November meeting with their energy ministry counterparts to put the final touches on the position Canada will take at negotiations in Kyoto, Japan, in December for a new international treaty on climate change.
``I hope they develop enough of a spine to stand up to the energy ministers,'' said the Sierra Club's Louise Comeau, a leading environmental activists on climate change.
Energy interests traditionally take priority over environmental protection in Canada, Comeau said.
Norm Sterling, who serves as Ontario's environment and energy minister, agreed with the environmentalists that energy interests hold the upper hand.
Sterling said he fears the November meeting of ministers will be too late to make any effective changes in the negotiating strategy the federal government will take to Kyoto.
``The federal minister realizes it's the provincial governments that have to get these reductions made,'' Sterling said. ``They have to think about how this will be fairly shared among the partners in Canada.''
Greenhouse gases like carbon dioxide that trap heat in the atmosphere, raising the over-all temperature of the planet, are created by the burning of fossil fuels such as oil, coal and natural gas.
Following the Earth Summit in Rio di Janeiro five years ago, Canada was among the nations that agreed to reduce greenhouse gas emissions to 1990 levels by the year 2000.
But Canada and most other developed countries won't meet that goal and the Kyoto meetings could see a 10-year extension on meeting the reduced emission levels, Comeau said.
Reducing emissions cannot be accomplished without a major shift away from the use of fossil fuels, said Greenpeace climate change campaigner Kevin Jardine.

TALKS TO CURB GLOBAL WARMING RUN HOT AND COLD NATIONS' COMPETING INTERESTS MAKE NEGOTIATIONS DIFFICULT: [EVENING UPDATE, C EDITION]

Author: Hanley, Charles J

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/talks-curb-global-warming-run-hot-cold-nations/docview/418410734/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Editor's note: For months, the world's nations have been negotiating a treaty to cut back, for the first time, on global fuel emissions. The reason: the looming reality of climate change. "The people in the villages and everybody else feels the ice is leaving earlier," Johnson said from Nome.
On the remote Pacific atoll of Nukuoro, in the Federated States of Micronesia, a rising sea has flooded the few acres where islanders can grow taro, their staple crop.
"If something's not done, Nukuoro is going to be abandoned," said John Mooteb, a Micronesia government official.
The sea can rise or fall for various reasons, oceanographers note. In the same cautious vein, scientists have long shied from flatly attributing the warming of recent decades to gas emissions. But in 1995 they crossed a threshold.
A UN report, produced with the help of more than 2,000 specialists worldwide, concluded that "the balance of evidence suggests a discernible human impact on Earth's climate system."
The scientific panel, led by Bolin, the Swedish climatologist, predicted emissions at current rates would raise global temperatures by an additional 2 to 6 degrees Fahrenheit by 2100 and sea levels by 6 inches to 3 feet, inundating islands and shorelines.
Weather would grow more turbulent, and climate zones might shift hundreds of miles toward the poles, abruptly making regions wetter, drier, warmer or colder.
Some skeptics remain. Some say the link to greenhouse gases still is not shown beyond doubt. Some note the consensus projections have broad margins of error.
"The signal of warming is gradually getting stronger," said David A. Randall at Colorado State University, who is editor of the Journal of Climate. But although the past century's warming "is definitely very strong. . . one century isn't a long time in this field."
One doubter, University of Virginia climatologist Pat Michaels, acknowledges man has affected the climate. "My point," he said, "is not whether human beings change the climate, but how much and how."
The mainstream majority supporting the UN report concedes that big questions remain.
For one thing, a warmer world will evaporate water more rapidly, forming more clouds in the atmosphere. Depending on how and where they form, they may either cool or further warm the surface.
Another question: Will the oceans, absorbing enough warmth quickly enough, hold down rising air temperatures?
Answers would make projections more precise. But answers may not come until after the world begins feeling the strongest effects of warming.
"Despite the uncertainty, the risk of a significant change of climate does not go away," Bolin said. He said political leaders must act on "risk analysis."
In 1992, they acted by postponing. A 165-nation treaty on climate change called for voluntary emissions cutbacks. That failed and in 1995 governments agreed to toughen the treaty by negotiating mandatory rollbacks by industrial nations.
President Clinton has pledged a "strong American commitment to realistic and binding limits." But the United States, responsible for one-quarter of global emissions, has yet to commit to specific targets and timetables.
American business lobbyists contend emissions reductions could wipe out hundreds of thousands of jobs in the coal, oil, chemical and other industries--in part because companies would move operations to developing countries. And the U.S. Senate declared, by a 95-0 vote, it won't ratify a treaty that doesn't also require emission cutbacks in the developing world.
Washington is not alone. Japan, Australia and some other nations, under pressure from local industry, also are reluctant partners in the talks, whose complexity is producing unlikely alliances.
British Petroleum, for example, a big player in solar energy, has broken ranks with other oil producers to stand with environmentalists. Insurance companies, fearing mounting damage claims from higher sea levels, floods, storms and other severe weather, support quick action against climate change.
"A lot is going on behind the business lobbying," said Michael Zammit Cutajar, the Maltese official who is executive secretary for the negotiations.
With the treaty in mind, industry is working hard on energy-saving technologies, he said. "Those who get there first with a low-emission car engine, for example, stand to make a lot of money."
He believes Kyoto may produce an immediate accord on cutback targets--most think no more than 10 percent--and agreement in principle in other areas.
Then the talks would continue, round for round, like the long-running trade negotiations, Zammit Cutajar said.
"Kyoto is another step on a long road."
Illustration PHOTOS 2; Caption: PHOTOS (color): James Hansen (standing), director of NASA's Goddard Institute of Space in New York, works with student Josh Wilder. Hansen is a leader in the study of atmospheric changes. Emissions from automobiles (below) are primary sources of carbon dioxide and nitrous oxide, which feed the "greenhouse effect." AP photos.

The Climate Controversy; Meeting to Address Emissions, But Will Anything Get Done?; Emissions: Meeting to Address Cuts

Author: CHARLES J. HANLEY THE ASSOCIATED PRESS

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/climate-controversy-meeting-address-emissions/docview/288836000/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Up in walrus country, unhappy Eskimo hunters say the frozen sea is breaking up early these days. An ocean away, in the balmy mid-Pacific, islanders report the tides are suddenly stealing away chunks of precious land. Things are going wrong out at the edge of the world, and people there blame global warming. But back in the world's capitals, where 1997 is the year for action on climate change, things are going slowly. Behind diplomacy's closed doors, negotiators are drafting a treaty to control carbon dioxide and other ``greenhouse'' emissions, gases many scientists say are warming the planet. Emissaries are to gather in Kyoto, Japan, in December to sign the unprecedented new commitments.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+Climate+Controversy%3B+Meeting+to+Address+Emissions%2C+But+Will+Anything+Get+Done%3F%3B+Emissions%3A+Meeting+to+Address+Cuts&amp;author=CHARLES+J.+HANLEY+THE+ASSOCIATED+PRESS&amp;issn=07463502&amp;title=The+Salt+Lake+Tribune&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-09-14&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Up in walrus country, unhappy Eskimo hunters say the frozen sea is breaking up early these days. An ocean away, in the balmy mid-Pacific, islanders report the tides are suddenly stealing away chunks of precious land.
Things are going wrong out at the edge of the world, and people there blame global warming. But back in the world's capitals, where 1997 is the year for action on climate change, things are going slowly.
Behind diplomacy's closed doors, negotiators are drafting a treaty to control carbon dioxide and other ``greenhouse'' emissions, gases many scientists say are warming the planet. Emissaries are to gather in Kyoto, Japan, in December to sign the unprecedented new commitments.
After eons of talking about the weather, humankind may finally be ready to do something about it.
But as the weeks tick down and the debate heats up, the outcome looks less and less certain.
The planetary debate pits rich countries against poor, islands against continents, oil producers against conservationists.
It means many things to many people. American negotiator Tim Wirth calls climate change the top ``environmental challenge'' of our time. But U.S. industrialists describe the treaty negotiations as the century's most important ``economic decision.''
The environment vs. the economy: The fear of damage from a changing climate weighed against the fear of lost business and jobs from energy restraints.
In the end, Kyoto may produce an incomplete agreement, a basis for more talk and small steps forward.
And while diplomats duel, scientists work to clear up nagging uncertainties still clouding climate change.
There is nothing uncertain about the underlying theory.
In 1898, as industry expanded in Europe and America, Swedish scientist Svante Arrhenius warned that carbon dioxide pouring into the atmosphere would trap more of the Earth's heat -- like the glass of a greenhouse.
Carbon dioxide, methane, nitrous oxide and other gases that trap heat are transparent to incoming sunlight, but absorb the infrared radiation that Earth, maintaining its heat balance, emits back to space.
Fuel combustion -- in engines powering everything from automobiles to electricity plants -- is the biggest human-made source of carbon dioxide and nitrous oxide. Decomposition of organic waste is a major source of methane.
From 1800 to 1994, carbon dioxide in the atmosphere jumped from 280 parts per million to 358. And during the past century, the average global temperature rose by about 1 degree Fahrenheit.
Four of the five hottest years on record have occurred since 1990. Glaciers almost everywhere are in retreat, and oceans -- which expand when warmed -- have risen by 4 to 10 inches.
Reports arrive from across the globe of unusual seas and weather.
In Alaska, Charlie Johnson, a leader of Eskimo hunters, said the walrus harvest is down the past few years because the Arctic sea ice -- the place where human meets walrus -- breaks up earlier than usual each spring.
The sea can rise or fall for various reasons, oceanographers note. In the same cautious vein, scientists have long shied from flatly attributing the warming of recent decades to gas emissions. But in 1995 they crossed a threshold.
A U.N. report, produced with the help of more than 2,000 specialists worldwide, concluded that ``the balance of evidence suggests a discernible human impact on Earth's climate system.''
The scientific panel, led by Bert Bolin of Sweden, a leading atmospheric scientist, predicted emissions at current rates would raise global temperatures by an additional 2 to 6 degrees Fahrenheit by 2100 and sea levels by 6 inches to 3 feet, inundating islands and shorelines.
Weather would grow more turbulent, and climate zones might shift hundreds of miles toward the poles, abruptly making regions wetter, drier, warmer or colder.
Some skeptics remain. Some say the link to greenhouse gases still is not shown beyond doubt. Some note the consensus projections have broad margins of error.
``The signal of warming is gradually getting stronger,'' said David A. Randall at Colorado State University, who is editor of the Journal of Climate. But although the past century's warming ``is definitely very strong . . . one century isn't a long time in this field.''
The mainstream majority supporting the U.N. report concedes that big questions remain.
For one thing, a warmer world will evaporate water more rapidly, forming more clouds in the atmosphere. Depending on how and where they form, they may either cool or further warm the surface.
Another question: Will the oceans, absorbing enough warmth quickly enough, hold down rising air temperatures?
Answers would make projections more precise. But answers may not come until after the world begins feeling the worst effects of warming.
``Despite the uncertainty, the risk of a significant change of climate does not go away,'' Bolin said. He said political leaders must act on ``risk analysis.''
In 1992, they acted by postponing. A 165-nation treaty on climate change called for voluntary emissions cutbacks. That failed, and in 1995, governments agreed to toughen the treaty by negotiating mandatory rollbacks by industrial nations.
Island states proposed that by 2005, emissions be cut by 20 percent below 1990 levels. The European Union favors a 15 percent reduction by 2010.
Such proposals don't prescribe how to do it, but studies suggest a range of ways: switching from coal and oil to nuclear or solar energy; developing more fuel-efficient engines; ending subsidies and imposing heavier taxes on carbon fuels; saving forests, which consume carbon dioxide.
President Clinton has pledged a ``strong American commitment to realistic and binding limits.'' But the United States, responsible for one-quarter of global emissions, has yet to commit to specific targets and timetables.
American business lobbyists contend emissions reductions could wipe out hundreds of thousands of jobs in the coal, oil, chemical and other industries -- in part because companies would move operations to developing countries. And the U.S. Senate declared, by a 95-0 vote, it will not ratify a treaty that does not also require emission cutbacks in the developing world.
Behind the scenes, the Clinton administration is readying a proposal expected to bring developing countries under the treaty in some new way and to introduce emissions-quota ``trading'' and other devices making compliance more flexible.
Washington is not alone. Japan, Australia and some other nations, under pressure from local industry, also are reluctant partners in the talks, whose complexity is producing unlikely alliances.
British Petroleum, for example, a big player in solar energy, has broken ranks with other oil producers to stand with environmentalists. And insurance companies, fearing mounting damage claims from higher sea levels, floods, storms and other severe weather, support quick action against climate change.
``A lot is going on behind the business lobbying,'' said Michael Zammit Cutajar, the Maltese official who is executive secretary for the negotiations.
With the treaty in mind, industry is working hard on energy-saving technologies, he said. ``Those who get there first with a low-emission car engine, for example, stand to make a lot of money.'

Explosion of interest Academics may identify a natural disaster waiting to happen, but how can they prevent the tragic effects? Tim Radford on the new money in hazard warning

Author: Radford, Tim

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/explosion-interest-academics-may-identify-natural/docview/245180504/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: `Within UCL, we had people working on natural hazards within about 10 different departments. We wanted funding to pull this research together. The obvious people to look to were the insurance and reinsurance industries. We had a very positive response all round. Within a year, the reinsurance broker Greig Fester came up with the funding for my position. They also provide us with a number of projects each year.' For instance, Dr Mark Saunders, from the UCL's department of space and climate physics, has been working on a new predictor for numbers of hurricanes. `What he has discovered is the warmer the sea in the Atlantic, the more hurricanes,' Prof Maguire says. `With 27C sea surface temperatures, you have two hurricanes a year. If you go up to 28C, you are talking something like seven. Greig Fester were incredibly interested in that, and they funded a PhD student to work on this for the next three years.' The centre is reporting on the short- and long-term impact of the Central European floods in July, which ruined homes, shut down factories and destroyed farmland by depositing layers of contaminants and raw sewage on to fields that should have been laden with crops. `In most areas, only one in three people were insured,' says (Bill) McGuire. `The water was incredibly deep. People were being flooded out of the upper floors of their houses.' The volcanic eruption in Montserrat has been an uncomfortable lesson. Vulcanologists would like to develop better warning machinery, not so much for Montserrat - two years of eruption have already done appalling damage - but for other islands in the same Caribbean arc: Dominica, St Vincent, Guadeloupe and others.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Explosion+of+interest+Academics+may+identify+a+natural+disaster+waiting+to+happen%2C+but+how+can+they+prevent+the+tragic+effects%3F+Tim+Radford+on+the+new+money+in+hazard+warning&amp;author=Radford%2C+Tim&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-09-09&amp;spage=004&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: For some dons, disaster is a way of life; for others, it can be anything but. Twelve vulcanologists - one of them Geoff Brown, of the Open University - have perished since 1991. Yet that hasn't stopped the pursuit of knowledge vital to our understanding of natural disasters.
The insurance industry - which has had to deal with awesome losses this decade because of US hurricanes and the earthquakes in Los Angeles and Kobe, Japan - takes hazard seriously. It has also been looking with alarm at the spectre of climate change. `They have an enormous amount to lose if they don't get things right,' says Professor Bill McGuire, director of a new unit called the Greig Fester Centre for Hazard Research, at University College, London. `They want to know the risks in a changing planet.
`Within UCL, we had people working on natural hazards within about 10 different departments. We wanted funding to pull this research together. The obvious people to look to were the insurance and reinsurance industries. We had a very positive response all round. Within a year, the reinsurance broker Greig Fester came up with the funding for my position. They also provide us with a number of projects each year.' For instance, Dr Mark Saunders, from the UCL's department of space and climate physics, has been working on a new predictor for numbers of hurricanes. `What he has discovered is the warmer the sea in the Atlantic, the more hurricanes,' Prof Maguire says. `With 27C sea surface temperatures, you have two hurricanes a year. If you go up to 28C, you are talking something like seven. Greig Fester were incredibly interested in that, and they funded a PhD student to work on this for the next three years.' The centre is reporting on the short- and long-term impact of the Central European floods in July, which ruined homes, shut down factories and destroyed farmland by depositing layers of contaminants and raw sewage on to fields that should have been laden with crops. `In most areas, only one in three people were insured,' says McGuire. `The water was incredibly deep. People were being flooded out of the upper floors of their houses.' The volcanic eruption in Montserrat has been an uncomfortable lesson. Vulcanologists would like to develop better warning machinery, not so much for Montserrat - two years of eruption have already done appalling damage - but for other islands in the same Caribbean arc: Dominica, St Vincent, Guadeloupe and others.
`They all have explosive volcanoes,' Prof Maguire says. `Some are more explosive than Montserrat and, potentially, they could cause very big problems. But the real problem is getting people educated, getting authorities trained, getting plans in place before the volcano becomes active, so that they know what to do when things get started.
`Montserrat is a case in question. We had seismic activity under the volcano in the latter part of the last century, and in the 1930s and in the 1960s - every 30 years. In the '80s, a hazard report was written for the volcano, saying keep an eye on it in the '80s. Nothing whatsoever was done about that report. It vanished somewhere. But plans for disaster mitigation should have been put in place at that time.' The UCL centre is unique - but not alone. York University has a Postwar Reconstruction and Development Unit, which is concerned with one kind of human tragedy; Cranfield Institute of Technology runs a Disaster Preparedness Centre; Oxford Brookes University has a Centre for Emergency and Development Planning.
Dr Ian Davis, of the Oxford Centre for Disaster Studies, is linked to all of them - and he believes things can be done. He takes heart from the Philippines, which has been hit by floods, earthquakes, tsunamis, typhoons and a calamitious eruption of Mt Pinatubo.
`A whole disaster culture seems to have developed in the Philippines because of the frequency of cyclones and floods,' he says. `Each of these different events seems to reinforce the others, and the people there know what to do. They have developed a culture of preparedness.
`Another good example is in India, where the evacuations before cyclones are really becoming very effective indeed. They are evacuating enormous numbers of people - up to 700,000 in one event.' The '90s are supposed to be, everywhere, concerned with disaster. The UN declared this the International Decade for Natural Disaster Reduction. Every year, early in October, nations have celebrated an IDNDR day. In Britain, disaster day has had about as much impact as Flea Awareness Week.
But since 1990, a committee of British academics, meteorologists, seismologists and engineers has been working on practical ways to save life in potential disasters. They are looking at ways to spread warnings of calamitous storms around tiny islands in the Indian ocean, for example, or ways of preparing for droughts in central Africa.
This year, the scientists are taking a bloodcurdling message to schools: on October 8, there will be afternoon conferences for fifth- and sixth-formers in London, Cardiff, Manchester, Glasgow and Belfast. Prof Maguire says: `There are two aspects. There is the scientific aspect, where you improve methodologies and techniques for forecasting and predicting. These things are going on all the time, developing better science. And the second thing you need to do is to have your scientific instrumentation where it is needed. There could be 1,000 active volcanoes in the world, yet we are looking at only 120.' Yet it doesn't matter how good the science is, he says, if you can't get the message across to the civil authorities and the public. So academics still have an education problem: to teach people and reach people.
The classic case was an eruption of Nevada del Ruiz, in Colombia, which triggered a catastrophic mudslide. `There shouldn't have been anybody killed there,' Prof Maguire insists. `The warnings were given, people had plenty of time to get out, yet nearly 25,000 died. I would say raising awareness during the decade has been at least as important as scientific methodology.'
For details of Natural Disaster Reduction Day programmes, contact Tony Eades at the Royal Academy of Engineering: 0171-222 2688.

Abstract: WHAT A PRETTY VIEW THIS IS. A STRETCH OF GRASS, A FRINGE OF Queen Anne's lace, then sand followed by white, bouncing waves at the turbulent edge of a wide expanse of the restless Atlantic, stretching eastward to the horizon. I am sitting in an idyllic little cottage where I do my writing, on Nantucket Island, a few yards from the sea.

Reports fuel global warming campaign: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/reports-fuel-global-warming-campaign/docview/244603360/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Environment Minister Christine Stewart is about to launch a massive education campaign to make Canadians aware of the impending danger of global warming. The program, which officially begins later this month, is built around six Environment Canada reports that detail the current and future effects of global warming on each region of the country. Stewart said Canadians must be prepared for the ``dramatic measures'' required to halt global warming, the same way they came to understand and embrace the need for deficit reduction.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Reports+fuel+global+warming+campaign&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew&amp;issn=08281815&amp;title=Calgary+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-09-06&amp;spage=A.16&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Environment Minister Christine Stewart is about to launch a massive education campaign to make Canadians aware of the impending danger of global warming.
The program, which officially begins later this month, is built around six Environment Canada reports that detail the current and future effects of global warming on each region of the country.
It will culminate in a national report to be released in November, on the eve of an international conference that's to establish a binding greenhouse gas emissions treaty.
Targets set at the Kyoto Conference in December could require a massive restructuring of the energy-intensive Canadian economy, which is the second largest per-capita source of greenhouse gases in the world.
Only the United States on a per-capita basis produces more of the emissions that cause global warming.
Stewart said Canadians must be prepared for the ``dramatic measures'' required to halt global warming, the same way they came to understand and embrace the need for deficit reduction.
``One of my priorities is to help the Canadian public understand the issue and make sure they have the information and knowledge they need to be supportive of the measures that have to be taken,'' she said.
In the United States, President Bill Clinton has launched a similar public information campaign.
He recently invited the top U.S. scientists to a White House symposium on global warming; another national forum is scheduled for October.
Internationally, the issue is the subject of fierce debate. Insurance companies, hard hit by rising claims from storms, floods and other natural disasters, want a greatly improved global treaty.
Lined up against them are the fossil fuel and automotive industries that could face costly new restrictions under tough emissions targets.
The European Union has called for a 15-per-cent reduction in greenhouse gas emissions by 2010.
Small island states in the Caribbean and South Pacific, threatened with rising sea levels that could wipe out entire countries, want a 20-per-cent cut by 2005.
Canada has yet to endorse any target or range of targets. Prime Minister Jean Chretien has said only that Canada will agree to ``binding, realistic and enforceable'' goals for gas emissions.
Greenhouse gases -- primarily carbon dioxide, nitrous oxide and methane -- accumulate in the planet's atmosphere, trapping heat that should escape into outer space.
The vast majority of greenhouse gases come from the use of fossil fuels -- oil, coal and gas -- in factories, vehicles and generating plants.
The greenhouse effect has already raised the earth's average temperature and scientists predict it will climb gradually by one to 3.5 degrees by 2100.
Canada, as a northern country, is particularly vulnerable to climate change. New projections from Environment Canada suggest that central and northern Canada will warm four to six degrees by 2050, decreasing to three to four degrees along eastern and western coastlines.
Climate models suggest such a rise will have serious effects on regional weather patterns, forests, fisheries and agriculture.
The prairies will experience more droughts, the north more forest fires and the entire country more severe storms.
Stewart, appointed in June as environment minister, is determined to make global warming a central focus of federal policy-making.
But to do so, she'll have to convince key cabinet colleagues, such as Finance Minister Paul Martin, Industry Minister John Manley and Natural Resource Minister Ralph Goodale, that global warming poses a real threat to Canada's environment and economy.
``If we don't deal with the climate change issue, which international science now says is due in large part to human activities, we won't need to worry about smog or toxics or endangered species because there won't be any left,'' Stewart said.
``Some Canadians say there could be benefits from climate change, that our cold winters will get warmer, but there's a really serious downside to that as well,'' she said.
Those negatives will be explored in six reports that examine how global warming will affect Canada.
The reports will comprise 3,500 pages of scientific research conducted since 1990.
- The issue: Public awareness of impact of global warming.
- What's new: Six federal reports detail and forecast effects of warming trends.
- What's next: Complete report will be released in November.

GLOBAL WARMING Anxious eyes on the climate: [Surveys edition]

Author: Prest, Michael

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-anxious-eyes-on-climate/docview/248528655/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: WHY should global warming be of any interest to reinsurers? The simple answer is that it could cost them a lot of money. The more complex and interesting answer is that it could change the way the industry thinks about risk, and therefore the part insurance plays in economic life. Full text: * by Michael Prest
Anxious eyes on the climate
Reinsurers are keeping a weather eye on the problem of rising temperatures
WHY should global warming be of any interest to reinsurers? The simple answer is that it could cost them a lot of money. The more complex and interesting answer is that it could change the way the industry thinks about risk, and therefore the part insurance plays in economic life.
Ivo Knoepfel, climate and environment adviser to Swiss Re , one of the world's biggest reinsurers, said: "We are convinced that global warming is a serious issue. We reckon that the loss burden due to natural catastrophes will increase because of climate changes and other factors."
The question is what to do about it. There is little dispute now that global warming - defined as a rise in the temperature of the atmosphere - is occurring. It is also widely, although not universally, accepted that the frequency of severe weather, such as storms and hurricanes, has increased over the past 20 years or so.
The industry is painfully aware that Hurricane Andrew in 1992, the Mississippi floods of 1993 and the eastern European floods of this year caused damage amounting to billions of dollars. What is disputed, however, is why our atmosphere is getting warmer and whether that change is related to apparently more common violent and costly weather.
Global warming may be part of a natural long-term trend. But it is also highly likely to be caused in some part by the build-up in the atmosphere of carbon dioxide and other gases from the burning of fossil fuels. The original calculations on the effect of greenhouse gas emissions were done by the Swedish scientist Svante August Arrhenius more than a century ago.
More recent calculations by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change estimate that human activity will raise global temperatures faster than at any time in the past 10,000 years - perhaps by 0.3 degrees centigrade a decade. The impact of this increase on climate could be dramatic.
Temperatures are expected to go up more in the northern than in the southern hemisphere, precipitation will increase generally and the incidence of heavy precipitation will also grow. Sea levels will rise, perhaps by as much as 65 centimetres by the end of the next century.
Oliver Peterken, director of research and development for reinsurance brokers Willis Faber & Dumas , says: "The implications are very profound. If climate is changing radically, and perhaps very quickly, how can risks be assessed and priced? Will insurers be forced to withdraw or sharply reduce cover? If that happens, who will pay for damage? How will society allocate the costs? What might the implications be for life and economic activity in regions considered to be high risk? Might entire vulnerable cities such as Miami even have to be abandoned?"
The core of the issue is that insurance is essentially a local undertaking. What matters is not general climate change but the consequences for a specific place at a specific time. As Michael Waceck, managing director of St Paul Reinsurance , part of The St Paul Cos , the American insurance group, said: "It's going to be local weather which is most important for us."
This detailed application of the global warming problem is compounded by another phenomenon. Recent years have seen a rapid growth in population and economic activity in the coastal and sub-tropical areas thought to be most vulnerable to the consequences of climate change: Florida and the coastal US, some of the fast-emerging Asian economies.
Put another way, the cost of disaster is mounting. This has prompted Swiss Re to talk of prosperity loss. "We are no longer contemplating temporary damage to individual properties or businesses but long-term or even permanent damage to whole economic sub-systems, or in the case of small countries possibly entire national economies."
The City of London, for example, is in a low-lying area which might become even more prone to floods from a combination of higher rainfall, rising sea levels, and the separate sinking of eastern England. Will the Thames Barrier be an adequate defence in such relatively severe conditions which were not anticipated when it was built? The same question could be asked of protection against avalanches in the Alps, or tropical storms in Australia, or flooding in Bangladesh.
Unfortunately, nobody knows the answer. It is virtually impossible in the existing very limited state of knowledge about global warming and the climate system to foresee all the implications for the insurance industry. Mr Knoepfel says: "If underlying climatic conditions change, it will become impossible to use past claims experience to quantify natural hazards."
Reinsurance companies suffer a further disadvantage. They are removed from the risks that they ultimately carry. One way in which they have sought to compensate for their lack of first-hand information about risks is to spread their reinsurance portfolio around the world. The assumption, drawn from experience, was that disasters did not happen everywhere at once. But what if we are experiencing a systemic change in climate which renders that assumption invalid?
Not everyone believes that the picture is hopelessly gloomy. Mr Waceck argues that a great deal will depend on government, particularly local authorities, to enforce planning regulations - say for the construction and siting of buildings - designed to reduce potential damage. At the same time, most contracts are negotiated annually. The industry is therefore able to adjust its pricing in frequent steps to take account of events.
That, however, begs the question of whether government and society face up to the implications of global warming. One of the most important lines of defence being erected by reinsurers is to stimulate public discussion. Since information firmly grounded in science is the key to informed debate on global warming, reinsurers have started to back research into the subject - with at least one eye on gaining an advantage over rival companies.
A related instance is the Tsunami Project, from the Japanese word for a tidal wave caused by an earthquake. The project is a joint venture between the Department of Trade and Industry and a group of British insurance companies to finance research into natural disasters. The idea is to give the British insurance industry a competitive edge.
Better information will enable reinsurers to judge the economic rate for risk - the prime contribution the industry makes to economic activity. It might, for example, lead to higher deductibles in exposed areas or to improved protection against natural hazards. But it will be a slow process. In the end, the best weapon in the reinsurance industry's armoury to combat global warming is its flexibility. "I think our industry has the tools to cope with change. That's our business," Mr Knoepfel said. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1997. All Rights Reserved.

Minister targets global warming: Canadians will be told that `dramatic measures' might have to be taken to combat problem: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/minister-targets-global-warming-canadians-will-be/docview/433228094/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Environment Minister Christine Stewart is about to launch a massive education campaign to make Canadians aware of the impending danger of global warming. The program, which officially begins later this month, is built around six Environment Canada reports that detail the current and future effects of global warming on each region of the country. Stewart said Canadians must be prepared for the ``dramatic measures'' required to halt global warming, the same way they came to understand and embrace the need for deficit reduction.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Minister+targets+global+warming%3A+Canadians+will+be+told+that+%60dramatic+measures%27+might+have+to+be+taken+to+combat+problem&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew&amp;issn=03841294&amp;title=The+Gazette&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-09-02&amp;spage=A.6&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Environment Minister Christine Stewart is about to launch a massive education campaign to make Canadians aware of the impending danger of global warming.
The program, which officially begins later this month, is built around six Environment Canada reports that detail the current and future effects of global warming on each region of the country.
It will culminate in a national report to be made public in November, shortly before an international conference that's to establish a binding greenhouse-gas emissions treaty.
Targets set at the Kyoto Conference in December could require a massive restructuring of the energy-intensive Canadian economy, which is the second largest per-capita source of greenhouse gases in the world.
Only the United States on a per-capita basis produces more of the emissions that cause global warming.
Stewart said Canadians must be prepared for the ``dramatic measures'' required to halt global warming, the same way they came to understand and embrace the need for deficit reduction.
``One of my priorities is to help the Canadian public understand the issue and make sure they have the information and knowledge they need to be supportive of the measures that have to be taken,'' she said in an interview.
In the U.S., President Bill Clinton has launched a similar public-information campaign. Clinton, who became a convert to the issue through the repeated lectures of Vice-President Al Gore, recently invited the nation's top scientists to a White House symposium on global warming; another national forum is scheduled for October.
Internationally, the issue is the subject of fierce debate. Insurance companies, hard hit by rising claims from storms, floods and other natural disasters, want a greatly improved global treaty. Lined up against them are the fossil-fuel and automotive industries, which could face costly new restrictions under tough emissions targets.
The European Union has called for a 15-per-cent reduction in greenhouse- gas emissions by 2010. Small island states in the Caribbean and South Pacific, threatened with rising sea levels that could wipe out whole nations, want a 20-per-cent cut by 2005.
Canada has yet to endorse any target or range of targets. Prime Minister Jean Chretien has said only that Canada will agree to ``binding, realistic and enforceable'' goals for gas emissions.
Greenhouse gases - primarily carbon dioxide, nitrous oxide and methane - accumulate in the planet's atmosphere, trapping heat that should escape into outer space.
The vast majority of greenhouse gases come from the use of fossil fuels - oil, coal and gas - in factories, vehicles and generating plants. The greenhouse effect has already raised the earth's average temperature and scientists predict it will climb gradually by one to 3.5 degrees Celsius by 2100.
Canada, as a northern country, is particularly vulnerable to climate change. New projections from Environment Canada suggest that central and northern Canada will warm four to six degrees by 2050, decreasing to three to four degrees along eastern and western coastlines.
Climate models suggest such a rise will have serious effects on regional weather patterns, forests, fisheries and agriculture. The prairies will experience more droughts, the north more forest fires and the entire country more severe storms.
Stewart, appointed in June as environment minister, is determined to make global warming a central focus of federal policy-making. But to do so, she'll have to convince key cabinet colleagues, such as Finance Minister Paul Martin, Industry Minister John Manley and Natural Resource Minister Ralph Goodale, that global warming poses a real threat to Canada's environment and economy.
``If we don't deal with the climate change issue, which international science now says is due in large part to human activities, we won't need to worry about smog or toxics or endangered species because there won't be any left,'' Stewart said.
``Some Canadians say there could be benefits from climate change, that our cold winters will get warmer, but there's a really serious downside to that as well,'' she said.
Those negatives will be explored in six reports that examine how global warming will affect the Pacific and Yukon, Prairie and Northern Canada, Ontario, Quebec, Atlantic Canada and the Arctic. The reports will comprise 3,500 pages of scientific research conducted since 1990.
In mid-month, Stewart is scheduled to meet with the country's environment ministers in Toronto, where she'll attempt to build support for a national emissions- reduction scheme.
``I would like to see us come to a consensus about the urgency and the fact that we all have to take part, that it's in the best interests of all of us to take a national approach,'' Stewart said.
At the Rio Earth Summit in 1992, Canada agreed to reduce its greenhouse-gas emissions to 1990 levels by 2000. Ottawa established a voluntary industry-reductions program, but it hasn't come close to meeting the Rio target. Canada's emissions in 1995 were 9.2 per cent above 1990 levels and they're projected to increase substantially in the next century.

It will culminate in a national report to be released in November, on the eve of an international conference that's to establish a binding greenhouse gas emissions treaty.

Only the United States on a per-capita basis produces more of the emissions that cause global warming.

Canada has yet to endorse any target or range of targets. Prime Minister Jean Chretien has said only that Canada will agree to "binding, realistic and enforceable" goals for gas emissions.
Fossil fuels
Greenhouse gases -- primarily carbon dioxide, nitrous oxide and methane -- accumulate in the planet's atmosphere, trapping heat that should escape into outer space. The vast majority of greenhouse gases come from the use of fossil fuels -- oil, coal and gas -- in factories, vehicles and generating plants.
The greenhouse effect has already raised the earth's average temperature and scientists predict it will climb gradually by one to 3.5 degrees by 2100.
Canada, as a northern country, is particularly vulnerable to climate change. New projections from Environment Canada suggest that central and northern Canada will warm four to six degrees by 2050, decreasing to three to four degrees along eastern and western coastlines.
Climate models suggest such a rise will have serious effects on regional weather patterns, forests, fisheries and agriculture. The prairies will experience more droughts, the north more forest fires and the entire country more severe storms.
Stewart, appointed in June as environment minister, is determined to make global warming a central focus of federal policy-making.
But to do so, she'll have to convince key cabinet colleagues, such as Finance Minister Paul Martin, Industry Minister John Manley and Natural Resource Minister Ralph Goodale, that global warming poses a real threat to Canada's environment and economy.
"If we don't deal with the climate change issue, which international science now says is due in large part to human activities, we won't need to worry about smog or toxics or endangered species because there won't be any left," Stewart said.
"Some Canadians say there could be benefits from climate change, that our cold winters will get warmer, but there's a really serious downside to that as well," she said.
Those negatives will be explored in six reports that examine how global warming will affect the Pacific and Yukon, Prairie and Northern Canada, Ontario, Quebec, Atlantic Canada and the Arctic. The reports will comprise 3,500 pages of scientific research conducted since 1990.
Emissions reduction
In mid-September, Stewart is scheduled to meet with the country's environment ministers in Toronto, where she'll attempt to build support for a national emissions reduction scheme.
"I would like to see us come to a consensus about the urgency and the fact that we all have to take part, that it's in the best interests of all of us to take a national approach," Stewart said.
At the Rio Earth Summit in 1992, Canada agreed to reduce its greenhouse gas emissions to 1990 levels by 2000.
Ottawa established a voluntary industry reductions program, but it hasn't come close to meeting the Rio target.
Canada's emissions in 1995 were 9.2 per cent above 1990 levels and they're projected to increase substantially in the next century.

Massive push against global warming; Canada second-worst offender: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/massive-push-against-global-warming-canada-second/docview/252461102/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Environment Minister Christine Stewart is about to launch a massive education campaign to make Canadians aware of the impending danger of global warming. The program, which officially begins later this month, is built around six Environment Canada reports that detail the current and future effects of global warming on each region of the country. Targets set at the Kyoto Conference in December could require a massive restructuring of the energy-intensive Canadian economy, which is the second-largest per capita source of greenhouse gases in the world.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Massive+push+against+global+warming%3B+Canada+second-worst+offender&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew&amp;issn=0839296X&amp;title=Edmonton+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-09-02&amp;spage=A.3&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Environment Minister Christine Stewart is about to launch a massive education campaign to make Canadians aware of the impending danger of global warming.
The program, which officially begins later this month, is built around six Environment Canada reports that detail the current and future effects of global warming on each region of the country.
It will culminate in a national report to be released in November, on the eve of an international conference that's to establish a binding greenhouse gas emissions treaty.
Targets set at the Kyoto Conference in December could require a massive restructuring of the energy-intensive Canadian economy, which is the second-largest per capita source of greenhouse gases in the world.
Only the United States on a percapita basis produces more of the emissions that cause global warming.
Stewart said Canadians must be prepared for the ``dramatic measures'' required to halt global warming, the same way they came to understand and embrace the need for deficit reduction.
``One of my priorities is to help the Canadian public understand the issue and make sure they have the information and knowledge they need to be supportive of the measures that have to be taken,'' she said in an interview.
In the U.S., President Bill Clinton has launched a similar public information campaign. Clinton, who became a convert to the issue through the repeated lectures of Vice-President Al Gore, recently invited the nation's top scientists to a White House symposium on global warming; another national forum is scheduled for October.
Internationally, the issue is the subject of fierce debate. Insurance companies, hard hit by rising claims from storms, floods and other natural disasters, want a greatly improved global treaty. Lined up against them are the fossil fuel and automotive industries which could face costly new restrictions under tough emissions targets.
The European Union has called for a 15-per-cent reduction in greenhouse gas emissions by 2010. Small island states in the Caribbean and South Pacific, threatened with rising sea levels that could wipe out nations, want a 20-per-cent cut by 2005.
Canada has yet to endorse any target or range of targets. Prime Minister Jean Chretien has said only that Canada will agree to ``binding, realistic and enforceable'' goals for gas emissions.
Greenhouse gases -- primarily carbon dioxide, nitrous oxide and methane -- accumulate in the planet's atmosphere, trapping heat that should escape into outer space. The vast majority of greenhouse gases come from the use of fossil fuels -- oil, coal and gas -- in factories, vehicles and generating plants.
The greenhouse effect has already raised the Earth's average temperature and scientists predict it will climb gradually by one to 3.5 degrees by 2100.
Canada, as a northern country, is particularly vulnerable to climate change. New projections from Environment Canada suggest that central and northern Canada will warm four to six degrees by 2050, decreasing to three to four degrees along eastern and western coastlines.
Climate models suggest such a rise will have serious effects on regional weather patterns, forests, fisheries and agriculture. The prairies will experience more droughts, the north more forest fires and the entire country more severe storms.
In mid-September, Stewart is to meet with the country's environment ministers in Toronto.

Campaign will warn Canadians of global warming danger: Problem demands `dramatic measures,' environment minister says: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/campaign-will-warn-canadians-global-warming/docview/240100292/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Environment Minister Christine Stewart is about to launch a massive education campaign to make Canadians aware of the impending danger of global warming. The program, which officially begins this month, is built around six Environment Canada reports that detail the current and future effects of global warming on each region of the country. It will culminate in a national report to be released in November, on the eve of an international conference that's to establish a binding treaty on greenhouse gas emissions. Targets set at the Kyoto Conference in December could require a massive restructuring of the energy-intensive Canadian economy, which is the second-largest per-capita source of greenhouse gases in the world.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Campaign+will+warn+Canadians+of+global+warming+danger%3A+Problem+demands+%60dramatic+measures%2C%27+environment+minister+says&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew&amp;issn=08393222&amp;title=The+Ottawa+Citizen&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1997-09-02&amp;spage=A.8&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Environment Minister Christine Stewart is about to launch a massive education campaign to make Canadians aware of the impending danger of global warming.
The program, which officially begins this month, is built around six Environment Canada reports that detail the current and future effects of global warming on each region of the country. It will culminate in a national report to be released in November, on the eve of an international conference that's to establish a binding treaty on greenhouse gas emissions.
Targets set at the Kyoto Conference in December could require a massive restructuring of the energy-intensive Canadian economy, which is the second-largest per-capita source of greenhouse gases in the world.
Only the United States produces more of the emissions that cause global warming per capita.
Ms. Stewart said Canadians must be prepared for the ``dramatic measures'' required to halt global warming, just as they came to understand and embrace the need for deficit reduction.
``One of my priorities is to help the Canadian public understand the issue and make sure they have the information and knowledge they need to be supportive of the measures that have to be taken,'' she said.
In the U.S., President Bill Clinton has launched a similar public information campaign. Mr. Clinton, who became a convert to the issue under the influence of Vice-President Al Gore, recently invited the nation's top scientists to a White House symposium on global warming; another national forum is scheduled for October.
Internationally, the issue is the subject of fierce debate. Insurance companies, hard hit by rising claims from storms, floods and other natural disasters, want a greatly improved global treaty. Lined up against them are the fossil fuel and automotive industries, which could face costly new restrictions under tough emissions targets.
The European Union has called for a 15-per-cent reduction in greenhouse gas emissions by 2010. Small island states in the Caribbean and South Pacific, threatened with rising sea levels that could wipe out whole nations, want a 20-per-cent cut by 2005.
Canada has yet to endorse any targets. Prime Minister Jean Chretien has said only that Canada will agree to ``binding, realistic and enforceable'' goals for gas emissions.
Greenhouse gases -- primarily carbon dioxide, nitrous oxide and methane -- accumulate in the planet's atmosphere, trapping heat that should escape into outer space. The vast majority of greenhouse gases come from the use of fossil fuels -- oil, coal and gas -- in factories, vehicles and generating plants. The greenhouse effect has already raised the Earth's average temperature, and scientists predict it will climb gradually by between one and 3.5 degrees by 2100.
As a northern country, Canada is particularly vulnerable to climate change. New projections from Environment Canada suggest that Central and Northern Canada will warm four to six degrees by 2050, while the east and west coasts will see a decrease in temperatures of three to four degrees.
Climate models suggest such a rise will have serious effects on regional weather patterns, forests, fisheries and agriculture. The Prairies will experience more droughts, the North more forest fires and the entire country will have severer storms.
Ms. Stewart, who was appointed to the cabinet post in June, is determined to make global warming a central focus of federal policy-making. But to do so, she'll have to convince key colleagues, such as Finance Minister Paul Martin, Industry Minister John Manley and Natural Resources Minister Ralph Goodale, that global warming poses a real threat to Canada's environment and economy.
In mid-September, Ms. Stewart is to meet the provincial environment ministers in Toronto, where she'll attempt to build support for a national emissions reduction scheme.
At the Rio Earth Summit in 1992, Canada agreed to reduce its greenhouse gas emissions to 1990 levels by 2000. The federal government established a voluntary program to encourage industry reductions, but it hasn't come close to meeting the Rio target.
Canada's emissions in 1995 were 9.2 per cent above 1990 levels and they're projected to rise in the next century.
